###1###
When I behold thy beauty, fresh and young,
And mark how swiftly envious Time doth fly,
My heart fears lest this bloom be soon unsung,
And all thy grace beneath his sickle lie.
For age, with wrinkles, waits to steal thy prime,
And frosts of winter blight the summer's flower,
No force can stop the onward march of time,
Nor bid him stay his all-consuming power.
Yet in these lines, thy youth shall ever live,
When marble statues crumble into dust,
A fairer monument I hope to give,
Against Oblivion's dark and hungry rust.
So long as men can breathe, or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###2###
My love is not a star, though bright she gleams,
Nor like the sun, whose fiery course doth range,
But constant as the moon in silver dreams,
Above the shifting world of earthly change.
Let envious clouds obscure the morning's light,
And fickle fortune turn her wheel about,
My faith in her shall stand through darkest night,
Unmoved by slander, malice, fear, or doubt.
For true affection knows no ebb nor flow,
But holds its course, steadfast in every trial,
A rooted oak, where winter tempests blow,
It scorns deceit, and shuns the tongue of guile.
Thus fixed in truth, my heart shall ever be,
As constant as her love, eternally.

###3###
The raven locks that once were rich and deep,
Are streaked with silver now, and thin they grow;
The eyes that shone, now heavy seem with sleep,
And youth's bright fire begins to burn full low.
For Time, that thief, steals beauty's golden hour,
And plants his furrows where the roses played,
He nips the bud, and doth consume the flower,
Leaving but ghosts of what his hand hath made.
Alas, no remedy can stay his hand,
No magic charm can make his scythe delay,
He sweeps all life throughout the weary land,
And bears our fleeting glories far away.
Yet in these lines, some shadow may remain,
To tell the world thy beauty lived in vain.

###4###
Shall I compare thy wit to diamonds bright,
Whose keenest facets pierce the mind's dark night?
Or to the sun, that brings the world to light,
And doth disperse all shadows with its might?
No, for thy thoughts are swifter than the day,
More subtle than the moon's ethereal gleam,
They dance like fairies in a sportive way,
And wake the slumbering soul from drowsy dream.
Thy words, like honey, flow with such delight,
They charm the ear, and soothe the troubled breast,
Dispelling sorrow with their gentle might,
And granting comfort to the sore distressed.
Thus doth thy mind a wondrous kingdom hold,
More rich than gems, more precious far than gold.

###5###
When absent from the solace of thine eyes,
My heart, a rudderless and fragile bark,
Is tossed on waves of sorrow, sighs on sighs,
And wanders blindly through the bitter dark.
The sun itself seems to withdraw its gleam,
The birds forget their joyous morning lay,
And life itself appears a troubled dream,
Since thou, my guiding star, art far away.
Each moment stretched, an age of endless pain,
Each thought a thorn, each memory a sting,
I long to feel thy tender hand again,
And hear the music that thy voice doth bring.
Come back, my love, and banish all my fear,
And let thy presence make my spirit clear.

###6###
Sleep, gentle Sleep, thou balm of weary minds,
That knitt'st up sorrows, and dost ease all pain,
Thou art a comfort that the troubled finds,
And bring'st oblivion to life's harsh domain.
When troubled thoughts like spectres haunt the day,
And reason's lamp burns low with anxious dread,
Thou com'st with silence, chasing cares away,
And lay'st a soft hand on the aching head.
Thy drowsy touch can calm the fevered breast,
And bid the restless spirit find its rest.
In thy soft arms, all mortals are made blest,
From king to peasant, by thy grace caressed.
So welcome, Sleep, descend with silent wing,
And to my soul thy sweet repose now bring.

###7###
The fickle world, whose praises ebb and flow,
Doth lift its darlings high, then casts them down;
And fortune's wheel, that doth so swiftly go,
Bestows a laurel, then withdraws a crown.
Who trusts her smiles, builds castles in the air,
For her false promises betray the wise,
She lures with hopes, then plunges to despair,
And mocks the tears that stream from weeping eyes.
Let others chase her gaudy, painted prize,
And stake their peace upon her shifting sands,
My soul shall seek where truer comfort lies,
Beyond the grasp of her capricious hands.
For lasting joy, no earthly wealth can buy,
It dwells within, where truest virtues lie.

###8###
My friend, whose counsel lights my darkest way,
And whose firm hand sustains my faltering tread,
What words can tell the debt I owe this day,
For comfort granted, and for solace spread?
When worldly cares did press upon my soul,
And bitter envy poisoned every thought,
Thy gentle wisdom made my spirit whole,
And peace, which I had lost, was newly caught.
No fleeting fancy ties my heart to thine,
But bonds of truth, by trial made more strong,
A sacred trust, a loyalty divine,
That shall endure though ages roll along.
So long as life within this breast shall dwell,
My grateful heart thy goodness shall compel.

###9###
When Nature's art doth paint the eastern skies,
With hues of crimson, gold, and tender blue,
And morning's breath bids drowsy creatures rise,
Unveiling all the world to mortal view;
I marvel at the glory of the light,
The dew-kissed grass, the blossoms freshly born,
Dispelling shadows of the vanished night,
And ushering in a sweet and hopeful morn.
Each tiny leaf, a miracle to see,
Each feathered songster, pouring forth its strain,
Reveals a power, boundless, wild, and free,
That banishes all sorrow, grief, and pain.
Oh, that my soul could hold this joy complete,
And learn from earth a lesson pure and sweet!

###10###
What cruel defect holds thy love in chains,
That thou shouldst choose to perish all alone?
No mirror shows a face where beauty wanes,
But one whose glory might sustain a throne.
Then why refuse to multiply thy grace,
And leave behind a copy of thy form?
Lest cruel Time erase each lovely trace,
And sweep thy fairness through oblivion's storm.
Be not so unkind to thyself and kind,
To let thy beauty die without an heir;
A perfect pattern, of the noblest kind,
Should not be lost, nor vanish into air.
Give to the world thy living portraiture,
And make thy beauty evermore endure.

###11###
Thou art not fair, for beauty's fleeting show,
Can oft deceive, and lead the heart astray;
Nor wise, for wisdom sometimes doth not know
The hidden snares that lurk along life's way.
But thou art true, a virtue rarely found,
In this false age, where honesty grows cold;
Thy heart sincere, on firmest honour ground,
More precious far than gems or purest gold.
Let outward grace with changing fashion turn,
And painted charms allure the common eye,
From thy fixed spirit I desire to learn,
How steadfast faith can make a soul stand high.
So hold thy truth, let others praise the fair,
For in thy truth, a lasting beauty's there.

###12###
My muse, now weary, finds no fresh delight,
To weave new verses, or to coin new phrase,
Her youthful vigour vanished from her sight,
And lost the fire of her brighter days.
The well of fancy seems to run full dry,
No spring of inspiration doth now flow,
But barren thoughts beneath a barren sky,
Where shadowed doubts begin to darkly grow.
Perhaps the season of my art is past,
And winter's chill upon my spirit lies,
My fading breath, no longer built to last,
Can only utter weak and broken sighs.
Yet if thy beauty I could still recall,
My frozen tongue would break its silent thrall.

###13###
When first I saw thy face, it was as dawn,
Breaking upon a world of weary night,
And all my sorrow, then, was swiftly gone,
Absorbed and vanquished by thy radiant light.
Thy smile, a sunbeam, chased away the fear,
Thy gentle voice, a melody so sweet,
It filled my spirit, banished every tear,
And made my trembling heart with gladness beat.
I knew not love could hold such potent sway,
To mend a soul that sorrow had undone,
And lead me from the paths of dark dismay,
Into a haven, where new hopes had spun.
So blest am I, that fate did thee impart,
To be the sovereign ruler of my heart.

###14###
The boast of titles, and the pride of birth,
The gilded trappings of a high estate,
Are but a mask to hide a hollow dearth,
And cannot long delay the stroke of fate.
For wealth and power, though they seem so grand,
Are fleeting shadows, fading with the day,
And often slip from out the grasp of hand,
When time's swift current bears them far away.
But honest labour, and a virtuous mind,
A noble spirit, built on truth and grace,
These lasting treasures mortals truly find,
Beyond the reach of fortune's changing face.
So let me strive for what is true and good,
And scorn the pomp by worldly fools pursued.

###15###
How many woes doth man endure below,
From false ambition, and from love betrayed!
What bitter fruits from seeds of malice grow,
By envious tongues and broken vows displayed!
The world's a stage, where fickle actors play,
Their tragic parts, with masks of painted lies,
And swiftly fade the glory of a day,
Beneath the gaze of unforgiving skies.
O, were there strength to pierce the veiled deceit,
And read the heart that hides its dark design,
To crush the serpent 'neath our righteous feet,
And bring to light the treachery malign!
But truth must wait till time's last curtain falls,
When righteous judgment answers virtue's calls.

###16###
My fears do whisper, that thy love may stray,
Or that some rival's charm might lure thy sight,
And so they steal my comfort, night and day,
And fill my waking hours with dark affright.
I watch thy glances, weigh each careless word,
And seek for signs where none are to be found,
My heart, a prisoner, by doubts deterred,
On false suspicions builds its barren ground.
But then thy steady gaze, so clear and deep,
Doth shame my madness, and my folly chide,
And tells my trembling spirit, "Cease to weep,"
For in thy truth, no falsehood can abide.
So let my doubts, like shadows, flee away,
And in thy constant love, my spirit stay.

###17###
When winter winds do howl and skies are grey,
And all the verdant leaves have turned to brown,
My memory flies to one far summer's day,
When love's first blossom opened in this town.
I see again the sunlight on thy hair,
Thy laughing eyes, that held the heaven's blue,
The sweet communion, banishing all care,
And whispers soft, that promised always true.
Though seasons change, and years do swiftly roll,
And time's cold hand doth touch with chill regret,
That golden image lives within my soul,
A cherished joy, I cannot quite forget.
So in my heart, that summer lingers still,
Defying winter's unrelenting will.

###18###
The bitter draught of sorrow I must drain,
For parting's hour doth bring a heavy toll;
And absence looms, a source of endless pain,
To tear apart the fabric of my soul.
No sun will shine as brightly as before,
No song of birds will bring such sweet delight,
For half my being journeys to a shore,
Where shadows lengthen, and where fades the light.
Oh, that our days were linked in endless chain,
That fate might never force us far apart,
To suffer this intolerable pain,
And bear the rending of a broken heart.
Yet in my thoughts, thou ever shalt reside,
And be my comfort, though we walk not side by side.

###19###
As one who wanders through a tangled wood,
And loses path, where thorns and shadows grow,
My mind, perplexed, by subtle thoughts pursued,
Doth lose its way, where reason fain would go.
For worldly cares, like briars, catch and hold,
The soaring spirit, bound by earthly ties,
And fancies wild, by passion's breath made bold,
Do cloud the mirror of my inner eyes.
Oh, for a guide to lead me from this maze,
A ray of truth to pierce the mental night,
To banish doubts that steal my brightest days,
And fill my spirit with a purer light.
So let my soul seek wisdom's guiding star,
And find the haven where true virtues are.

###20###
The earth, with greedy mouth, consumes its own,
And Time, that tyrant, spares no living thing,
All flesh returns to dust, and overthrown,
Are kings and paupers, by his sweeping wing.
Yet man, with brief and fragile span of breath,
Doth strive to leave some token of his stay,
Against the final victory of death,
A verse, a deed, to shine beyond his day.
For love and truth, though mortal life may cease,
Are etched in hearts, beyond decay's control,
They find in noble minds a lasting peace,
And live forever in the deathless soul.
So fear not dissolution's dark command,
For truth and love shall ever firmly stand.

###21###
When cruel time doth steal fair beauty's hold,
And youthful prime from fading cheek doth fly,
What comfort then shall make our story told,
Or shield us from oblivion's heavy eye?
For earthly glory, with a swift advance,
Doth wither swift, as summer's tender rose,
And all our fleeting joy, a fading trance,
Into the silent dust and darkness goes.
But if thy spirit in these lines doth dwell,
Though age and pain thy outward grace consume,
My humble verse thy wondrous worth shall tell,
And snatch thy memory from time's cold tomb.
So shalt thou live, beyond thy mortal stay,
And conquer all, save death's appointed day.

###22###
My love, a sun that knows no dark eclipse,
Doth shine as constant as the northern star,
No fickle breeze, nor treachery of lips,
Can taint the truth of what our spirits are.
Though seasons change and flowers lose their hue,
And swift decay makes strong foundations fall,
My steadfast heart shall ever rest with you,
And answer to your spirit's gentle call.
Let fortune frown, or envious tongues conspire,
To cast a shadow where our joys have grown,
My soul shall burn with an unyielding fire,
And find its solace in your love alone.
For true affection mocks at worldly strife,
And proves the very anchor of my life.

###23###
The mind, a stage where countless thoughts do play,
Doth oft present a pageant to the soul,
Of joys long past or griefs that cloud the day,
Ere reason can their surging tide control.
And so I muse on pleasures that are gone,
On vanished faces, loved in youthful prime,
And hear the echoes of a whispered dawn,
Lost to the cruel, devouring hand of time.
But then thine image, like a morning bright,
Doth steal upon my melancholy mood,
And banish all the shadows of the night,
Restoring to my breast a cheerful good.
Thus from thy sight such solace I derive,
That past regrets no longer can survive.

###24###
Shall I compare thy grace to blossoms frail,
Whose tender beauty fades at morning's dew?
Or to the moon, whose silver light doth fail
When harsh clouds gather, hiding her from view?
No, for thy virtues shine with brighter gleam,
More constant than the sun in summer's heat,
Dispelling all the shadows of a dream,
And making every bitter moment sweet.
No envious frost can touch thy gentle heart,
Nor winter's tempest dim thy inner light,
For nature did but lend thee her best art,
To banish sorrow, and adorn the night.
So long as men can breathe, or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###25###
When troubled thoughts within my bosom dwell,
And fortune's adverse glance doth make me sigh,
I turn to thee, my comfort and my well,
And find the solace which I cannot buy.
For in thy gaze, a gentle peace I find,
A balm to heal the wounds of worldly care,
A sanctuary for my wearied mind,
Escaping from each burden I must bear.
The envious crowd may mock my simple plea,
And deem my passion but a fool's desire,
But little know they what thou art to me,
Thou kindler of my soul's undying fire.
So let them scorn, their judgments I defy,
For in thy love, I choose to live and die.

###26###
The fleeting hours, that bear us to our end,
Do steal our youth and beauty day by day,
And swift as shadows, time doth often bend
Our firmest purpose, bearing it away.
The eye that sparkled with a youthful gleam,
The cheek where roses freshly did reside,
Shall yield their glory to oblivion's stream,
And be by wrinkled age and sorrow tried.
Yet fear not, love, though years conspire to chill
Thy outward grace, and steal thy youthful prime,
My constant verse shall keep thy beauty still,
And mock the ravage of relentless time.
For in these lines, thy memory shall stay,
And live forever, till the judgment day.

###27###
How oft I wander, lost in silent thought,
And search for comfort that I cannot find,
For every joy with bitter sorrow's fraught,
And leaves a restless torment in my mind.
The world, a stage where fleeting shadows glide,
Doth offer little for a soul like mine,
But masks and feigning, where no truths abide,
And hollow praises, meant but to decline.
Then do I turn to beauty's gentle face,
And find a refuge from the common strife,
A vision fair, replete with truth and grace,
That brings sweet purpose to my weary life.
So in thy presence, all my sorrows cease,
And my vexed spirit finds enduring peace.

###28###
My restless heart, by cares of life oppressed,
Doth yearn for quiet, far from worldly sound,
To lay its burdens on a tender breast,
Where truest sympathy may still be found.
For common converse yields but shallow praise,
And hollow laughter hides a secret sting,
While false companions tread divergent ways,
And every pleasure doth its sorrow bring.
But when thy image rises to my view,
A sudden light doth pierce the gathering gloom,
My weary spirit feels its strength renew,
And hope springs forth, like flowers from a tomb.
So in thy love, my true contentment lies,
And finds its heaven mirrored in thine eyes.

###29###
When winter's breath doth chill the tender year,
And icy grip doth bind the frozen ground,
The joyful songs of birds no longer hear,
Nor blossoms in the barren fields are found.
The trees stand stark, their branches bare and thin,
A mournful silence hangs upon the air,
As if the world were cloaked in grief within,
And burdened deeply by a heavy care.
Yet hope remains, though nature seems to die,
That spring shall come, with life's rekindled fire,
And green shoots burst beneath a smiling sky,
To banish winter's desolate attire.
So in my heart, though troubles make me grieve,
I wait for solace, and in hope believe.

###30###
The envious hours, with stealthy, silent tread,
Do steal the freshness from the morning's light,
And bring the shadows where fair youth was bred,
Consuming all that blossoms pure and bright.
The lily's whiteness and the rose's flush,
The dimpled cheek, the laughter in the eye,
Are hurried onward in a silent rush,
To perish with a soft and fading sigh.
Yet shall my verse, if truth it can impart,
Preserve the essence of thy fairer claim,
And plant thy beauty deep within each heart,
Immortalized within this deathless frame.
So shalt thou live, beyond all earthly scope,
A lasting monument to love and hope.

###31###
Lo, how the sun doth climb the eastern sky,
And with his beams doth chase the night away,
Revealing wonders to the waking eye,
And crowning with his gold the new-born day.
So doth thy presence banish all my dread,
And light the corners of my darkest thought,
A gentle spirit, kindly born and bred,
With grace and virtue from above inwrought.
Let common minds pursue their earthly gain,
And find their solace in a fleeting gleam,
My soul doth rise, released from sorrow's chain,
To dwell forever in thy holy dream.
For in thy worth, a heaven I espy,
A constant star that guides me till I die.

###32###
My muse, though oft inclined to deeper themes,
Of Time's decay, or Fortune's cruel deceit,
Is drawn by thee, as rivers to their streams,
To sing thy praises, wondrous, pure, and sweet.
For in thy form, such perfect grace doth lie,
As nature did in lavish bounty mould,
To charm the heart and captivate the eye,
A priceless treasure, worth far more than gold.
And though my skill but lends a feeble aid,
To paint the splendor which I scarce define,
Let truth itself within these lines be laid,
To show the world a beauty so divine.
So read these verses, though they falter oft,
And know my love, that lifts my spirit aloft.

###33###
When first I saw thy face, a wondrous sight,
My spirit soared, released from earthly bound,
As dawn's first blush doth banish darkest night,
And gentle music fills the air around.
No common beauty did my eyes behold,
But grace and virtue in their purest form,
A tale of heaven, by a spirit told,
To weather every cruel and bitter storm.
And though the world with envy may look on,
And petty tongues may strive to do thee wrong,
My heart shall shield thee till the day is gone,
And raise thy praises in eternal song.
For in thy worth, my dearest hope I place,
And find my solace in thy gentle grace.

###34###
The fleeting breath that gives us life and fire,
Doth swiftly pass, as shadows on the wall,
And all our earthly hopes, our fond desire,
Are destined soon to vanish, fade, and fall.
The strongest oak, by time is brought to dust,
The proudest towers crumble to the ground,
And all we cherish, built on mortal trust,
By ruin's hand, too quickly is unbound.
But fear not, friend, though death may claim its toll,
And steal the vigour from thy youthful years,
There is a part of thee, a deathless soul,
That conquers sorrow and transcends all fears.
So let thy spirit rise above decay,
And live forever, past thy fleeting day.

###35###
O, let me not compare thee to a flower,
Whose fragile petals wither in the sun,
Or to a joy that lasts but for an hour,
Before its brief, sweet pilgrimage is run.
For in thy spirit, true perfections dwell,
A steadfast light that knows no slow decline,
A spring of wisdom, from a hidden well,
And virtues pure, that make thy essence shine.
Let envious age attempt to steal thy grace,
And furrow lines upon thy youthful brow,
My verse shall hold thy beauty in this place,
And speak thy praises, as I speak them now.
So shall thy worth forever be renowned,
When fleeting glories lie beneath the ground.

###36###
My weary eyes, by day with tasks oppressed,
Do long for slumber, bringing sweet release,
To lay my thoughts upon a tranquil rest,
And find within the night a silent peace.
But then thy image steals into my mind,
And banishes all comfort from my bed,
For every sense to thee is then inclined,
And restless visions fill my waking head.
My spirit flies to thee on airy wings,
And haunts thy presence through the lonely night,
Imagining the joy thy presence brings,
And feasting on thy beauty's gentle light.
Thus, though by day I toil, by night I weep,
For thee I wake, for thee I cannot sleep.

###37###
When sorrow's cloud doth dim my spirit's sight,
And worldly cares with heavy burdens press,
I call on thee, my beacon and my light,
To ease the weight of my profound distress.
For in thy gentle mien, a balm I find,
To soothe the anguish of a troubled breast,
A sacred haven for my weary mind,
Where all my anxious thoughts are laid to rest.
Let others seek for fame or worldly power,
And chase the shadows of a fleeting dream,
My heart finds solace in that peaceful hour,
When thy bright image doth upon me beam.
So in thy love, my truest joy I see,
And all my comfort is derived from thee.

###38###
The spring, when all the world doth wake anew,
And blossoms burst forth in a thousand dyes,
Doth bring a freshness, like the morning dew,
And fill the air with joyful melodies.
But sweeter far than all that spring can lend,
More pure than roses bathed in golden light,
Art thou, my love, my faithful, constant friend,
Whose gentle presence makes my spirit bright.
No winter's chill can touch thy inner grace,
Nor autumn's fade thy steadfast heart impair,
For timeless beauty shines upon thy face,
And banishes all shadows of despair.
So live thou ever, in thy perfect prime,
Beyond the reach of sorrow or of time.

###39###
My pen, though weak, doth strive with earnest might,
To capture beauty ere it pass away,
And bring thy virtues to eternal light,
To shine forever, past thy mortal day.
For all too swift do earthly glories fade,
And bloom and perish in a single hour,
As by the hand of cruel oblivion made,
To yield their freshness to destruction's power.
But what I write, though simple it may seem,
Shall bear thy image to a distant age,
And prove thy worth, no idle, fading dream,
But truth enshrined upon this humble page.
So shall thy praise through future years be known,
When monuments of stone are overthrown.

###40###
What lasting treasure can the world impart,
That may compare with true and loyal love?
A sacred bond that dwells within the heart,
Sent from the purest heavens from above.
For transient joys, like shadows, swiftly flee,
And earthly riches vanish into dust,
But constant faith and firm fidelity,
Outlast the rust of time, and all its trust.
Let changeful fortune smile or let her frown,
And fickle friends their promises forsake,
My steadfast spirit shall not be cast down,
Nor from thy side my loyal purpose break.
For in this love, my dearest hope I find,
A perfect union of heart and mind.

###41###
When wasteful Time with hands doth spoil the rose,
And beauty's summer to harsh winter flies,
How swiftly youth's fair blossom onward goes,
And envious age steals lustre from thine eyes.
Thy golden locks shall turn to silver threads,
Thy vibrant blood grow cold, thy vigour wane,
And all the joy whereon thy spirit feeds,
Shall vanish quite, nor ever come again.
Yet in these lines, my love, thou shalt endure,
When marble tombs and gilded monuments,
Shall crumble down, and pass beyond all cure,
Outliving all Time's cruelest armaments.
So live thou still, though earthly breath be past,
My verse thy beauty's lasting hold shall cast.

###42###
How heavy hangs my heart when thou art gone,
And dull the day, though sunbeam bright appear,
As if the world had lost its morning dawn,
And music's sweetest note did wound the ear.
Each moment creeps with slow and painful tread,
A barren waste where joy was wont to bloom,
And phantom hopes are swiftly struck down dead,
Consumed by absence in a silent tomb.
My waking thoughts are ghosts of what hath been,
My sleeping dreams but shadows of thy face,
No solace finds my soul in any scene,
When thou, my star, art absent from thy place.
Come back, dear love, and banish this dark night,
And fill my yearning spirit with thy light.

###43###
When first thy gentle gaze did meet mine eye,
A sudden warmth did through my bosom spread,
As winter's ice beneath the spring suns lie,
And dormant spirits from their slumber shed.
No greater beauty hath the world revealed,
Than in the depths of thy celestial sight,
My every doubt and fear were then repealed,
And all my being bathed in purest light.
For in those pools, where love itself doth dwell,
I see a heaven far beyond the stars,
A sacred truth, a magic potent spell,
That heals all wounds and mends all ancient scars.
O, let me gaze, and drown within that sea,
And hold my soul forever bound to thee.

###44###
My heart, a prisoner, bound by silken chain,
Doth pine and languish for thy cold regard,
And though I plead with fervent prayer in vain,
Thy stony will doth ever stand on guard.
I pour my passion forth, a weeping stream,
That seeks to melt the frost upon thy breast,
But all my labours fade as any dream,
And leave my spirit grievously oppressed.
Why dost thou scorn the love that I do bear?
Or deem my constant troth of little worth?
A crueler fate no mortal soul can share,
Than to be spurned by her who gave him birth
To sorrow's thrall, and grief's perpetual sting,
While hope takes flight on disappointment's wing.

###45###
Fair creature, from whom we desire increase,
That thereby beauty's rose might never die,
But as the riper should by time decrease,
His tender heir might bear his memory.
But thou, contracted to thine own bright eyes,
Feed'st thy light's flame with self-substantial fuel,
Making a famine where abundance lies,
Thyself thy foe, to thy sweet self too cruel.
Thou that art now the world's fresh ornament,
And only herald to the gaudy spring,
Within thine own bud buriest thy content,
And, tender churl, mak'st waste in niggarding.
Pity the world, or else this glutton be,
To eat the world's due by the grave and thee.

###46###
When April's breath doth kiss the sleeping earth,
And tender buds begin their shy display,
A joyous spirit springs from nature's birth,
And birds sing sweetly at the break of day.
The meadows don their robes of emerald green,
The sunbeams dance upon the crystal stream,
A fairer sight hath seldom e'er been seen,
As if the world were waking from a dream.
Yet all this beauty, though it doth abound,
And tempt the eye with colours bright and bold,
Is but a shadow on enchanted ground,
Compared to charms thy lovely form doth hold.
For nature's art, however fair and grand,
Is but a foil for beauty in thy hand.

###47###
Let others praise the changing, fickle tide,
Or hold their faith to Fortune's shifting gaze,
My love for thee shall constantly abide,
Through summer's heat and winter's dreary days.
Though envious tongues may whisper words unkind,
And slander strive to dim thy sterling name,
No shadow shall they cast upon my mind,
Nor damp the ardour of my steady flame.
For well I know thy truth, thy pure intent,
And trust thy heart as sure as stars above,
My loyal spirit ever on thee bent,
Unshaken by the fears that lesser prove.
So let the world revolve and seasons turn,
My constant love for thee shall ever burn.

###48###
Not marble, nor the gilded monuments,
Of princes, shall outlive this powerful rhyme;
But you shall shine more bright in these contents,
Than unswept stone, besmear'd with sluttish time.
When wasteful war shall statues overturn,
And broils root out the work of masonry,
Nor Mars his sword nor war's quick fire shall burn
The living record of your memory.
'Gainst death and all oblivious enmity
Shall you pace forth; your praise shall still find room
Even in the eyes of all posterity
That wear this world out to the ending doom.
So till the judgment that yourself arise,
You live in this, and dwell in lovers' eyes.

###49###
When I do doubt if thou dost truly care,
My anxious thoughts do vex my troubled breast,
And phantom fears, a heavy burden, bear,
To rob my weary soul of peace and rest.
I count my faults, and weigh each slight misdeed,
Believing them too great for love to mend,
And nurture sorrow, though my heart may bleed,
Lest thy dear favour soon should have an end.
Why should such darkness shadow brightly days?
And apprehension cling where joy should be?
It is the mind, with strange and crooked ways,
That builds its torment, fashioning misery.
For when I look into thy loving eye,
My baseless fears like morning mists do fly.

###50###
How swiftly doth a smile upon thy face,
Dissolve the clouds that gather in my mind,
And bring to being such a joyful grace,
That all my previous sorrows are defined
As naught but shadows, fleeting and unsure,
That vanish quite when thou dost deign to beam,
A sacred magic, potent to allure,
More bright than any poet's fairest dream.
For in that light, a thousand flowers bloom,
And birds burst forth in melody so sweet,
Dispelling every hint of winter's gloom,
Making my trembling heart with gladness beat.
O, let thy smile forever be my guide,
And in its warmth, my willing soul abide.

###51###
When thoughtful Memory doth present to mind
The days of old, when youth and joy were free,
A bitter sweetness doth my spirit find,
Recalling moments spent, dear friend, with thee.
For though the pleasure of those times was great,
And laughter echoed through the sunlit hours,
I know too well that unrelenting fate
Hath plucked and scattered all those tender flowers.
The vanished past, a ghost that haunts my sleep,
Doth bring both comfort and a gentle pain,
For joys recalled make weary spirits weep,
To know they shall not e'er return again.
Yet hold I dear each moment we have known,
Though memory's gift be mixed with sorrow's moan.

###52###
Ah, where are now the days of childhood's glee,
When every morn brought wonders to explore?
And burdens light, and spirits wild and free,
Ere knowledge opened sorrow's bitter door?
The world then seemed a garden, fresh and green,
Where evil's shadow dared not cast its stain,
And simple truths were readily foreseen,
Before life's tempest brought its chilling rain.
Now wisdom's heavy hand doth press me low,
And worldly cares do weigh upon my soul,
The paths I tread, with thorns do thickly grow,
And broken dreams extract their heavy toll.
Oh, for a heart untainted, pure, and bright,
To walk again in childhood's golden light.

###53###
When worldly tempests rage and skies are grey,
And fearsome troubles gather thick and fast,
My weary spirit yearns for end of day,
A haven where my trembling heart may rest.
For in thy love, I find a strong defence,
A citadel against all harm and strife,
A sacred bond, where truth hath chief pretense,
And hope doth spring, renewing weary life.
Let Fortune frown, and envy cast its dart,
Let fickle friends depart and foes appear,
While thou, dear love, art rooted in my heart,
I shall not yield to sorrow, doubt, or fear.
For thy dear constancy, a beacon bright,
Doth guide me safely through the darkest night.

###54###
The world doth hold a mirror to the eye,
And often shows a face both false and fair,
Where painted smiles a hidden malice lie,
And outward show doth mask a foul despair.
The gaudy flower that boasts its splendid hue,
May yet contain a poison deep within,
And spoken vows, though seeming ever true,
May hide the hollow heart of deepest sin.
But thou, my love, art fashioned without art,
Thy virtues shine, no borrowed light they claim,
A truth profound resides within thy heart,
Unstained by falsehood, pure as any flame.
So let the world its fleeting masks embrace,
Thy genuine worth outshines all outward grace.

###55###
When centuries have crumbled towers of might,
And ancient cities turn to dust and sand,
And Time, with hungry maw, devours the light,
That once illumed the proudest of the land,
Thy beauty shall not yield to such decay,
Nor fade away with mortal, fleeting breath,
But live in these poor lines, from day to day,
Triumphant over ruin, age, and death.
For in this scroll, thy image shall remain,
More lasting than the carven stone or brass,
Escaping Time's inexorable chain,
As generations come and swiftly pass.
So read thy name, embalmed in poet's art,
A deathless wonder, fixed within my heart.

###56###
How oft I gaze upon thy lovely face,
And find no answer to my silent plea,
No gentle warmth, no sympathetic grace,
But cold indifference, ever turned to me.
My faithful heart, though brimming full of love,
Doth beat in vain against a wall of stone,
While hope's faint star is banished far above,
And I am left to suffer here alone.
I crave no riches, nor a crown to wear,
No worldly honours, nor a great renown,
But just one kindly glance, one word to share,
To ease the sorrow that hath borne me down.
Alas, dear love, this pain I must endure,
A heart unloved finds no enduring cure.

###57###
My outward mien, though calm it may appear,
Doth mask a tempest raging deep within,
A hidden sea of doubt, and grief, and fear,
Where weary thoughts their restless voyage begin.
For whilst I smile, and speak of common things,
My soul doth wrestle with a bitter fray,
And secret sighs the gentle spirit wrings,
As shadows lengthen at the close of day.
The world perceives a placid, tranquil brow,
Unknowing of the turmoil I endure,
And judges all by what is seen just now,
While my true suffering finds no earthly cure.
Oh, could they know the burdens that I bear,
They would perceive the depth of my despair.

###58###
When from thy lips the sweetest accents flow,
A magic melody fills all the air,
My spirit listens, raptured, soft, and low,
And every earthly sound seems less than fair.
No instrument, though cunningly devised,
Can match the music of thy gentle tongue,
Its subtle cadences, by love comprised,
More sweet than songs that ancient bards have sung.
It hath the power to soothe the troubled mind,
To banish sorrow, banish every dread,
A solace for the soul that it can find,
A balm to heal the words that once were said.
Oh, speak again, and charm my listening ear,
And chase away each doubt and every fear.

###59###
If true love's flame could burn eternally,
Untouched by time, unmarred by change's breath,
And loyal hearts could live perpetually,
Defying sorrow, sickness, age, and death,
Then would my spirit fear no coming day,
Nor dread the hour when youth must take its flight,
But revel in love's everlasting ray,
Enveloped in its never-ending light.
Alas, that all things fair must fade and cease,
And beauty's bloom be gathered to the grave,
That joy itself should find but brief release,
And all we cherish, Time doth soon engrave.
Yet in my heart, this hope shall brightly gleam:
That our true love may prove a deathless dream.

###60###
My love for thee, a pilgrim's steady quest,
Doth walk through sun and storm, through thorn and mire,
Nor seeks a pause, nor craves a moment's rest,
But onward presses with unflagging fire.
Through deserts bare, where solace finds no stay,
And mountains steep, where trials do abound,
My constant spirit finds its destined way,
And yields no ground, though dangers hem it round.
The path is long, and shadows oft descend,
And weariness doth sometimes claim its toll,
But at each step, my thoughts to thee I send,
As to the sacred altar of my soul.
So let me journey, till my life is done,
My pilgrimage of love, forever run.

###61###
When forty winters shall besiege thy brow,
And make deep trenches where fair beauty lay,
Thy youth's proud livery, so gazèd on now,
Will be but tattered rags, consumed by day.
Then being asked where all thy beauty lies,
Where all the treasure of thy lusty prime,
To say within thine own deep-sunken eyes,
Were an all-eating shame, and thriftless crime.
How much more praise deserved thy beauty's use,
If thou couldst answer, "This fair child of mine
Shall sum my count, and make my old excuse,"
Proving thy beauty's worth, and making thine.
This were to be new born when thou art old,
And see thy blood warm when thou feel'st it cold.

###62###
Shall I compare thee to a summer's day?
Thou art more lovely and more temperate:
Rough winds do shake the darling buds of May,
And summer's lease hath all too short a date:
Sometime too hot the eye of heaven shines,
And often is his gold complexion dimmed;
And every fair from fair sometime declines,
By chance, or nature's changing course, untrimmed;
But thy eternal summer shall not fade,
Nor lose possession of that fair thou ow'st;
Nor shall Death brag thou wander'st in his shade,
When in eternal lines to time thou grow'st:
So long as men can breathe, or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###63###
When my love swears that she is made of truth,
I do believe her, though I know she lies,
That she might think me some untutor'd youth,
Unlearned in the world's false subtleties.
Thus vainly thinking that she thinks me young,
Although she knows my days are past the best,
Simply I credit her false-speaking tongue:
On both sides thus is simple truth suppressed.
But wherefore says she not she is unjust?
And wherefore say not I that I am old?
O, love's best habit is in seeming trust,
And age in love loves not to have years told:
Therefore I lie with her, and she with me,
And in our faults by lies we flatter'd be.

###64###
Against that time, if ever that time come,
When I shall see thee frown on my defects,
When as thy love hath cast its utmost sum,
Called to that audit by advisèd respects;
Against that time when thou shalt strangely pass,
And scarcely greet me with a dying eye,
When love converted from the thing it was,
Shall reasons find to falsify thereby;
Against that time do I insconce me here,
Within the knowledge of my powers to fight,
Lest I be sick with dread of thy unkindness' tear,
And made a
bject to the
world's harsh spite.
So shall I be prepared against thy near,
As I have seen, and by experience bear.

###65###
When I do count the clock that tells the time,
And see the brave day sunk in hideous night;
When I behold the violet past prime,
And sable curls all silvered o'er with white;
When lofty trees I see barren of leaves,
Which erst from heat did canopy the herd,
And summer's green all girded up in sheaves,
Borne on the bier with white and bristly beard;
Then of the beauty of my love I think,
How with the seasons' wreck it too must fade,
For neither strong tower nor gates of beaten brink,
But must yield up their strength to time's sharp blade.
O fearful meditation! where, alas,
Shall time's best jewel from time's chest be passed?

###66###
No longer mourn for me when I am dead
Than you shall hear the surly sullen bell
Give warning to the world that I am fled
From this vile world, with vilest worms to dwell.
Nay, if you read this line, remember not
The hand that writ it; for I love you so,
That I in your sweet thoughts would be forgot,
If thinking on me then should make you woe.
O, if, I say, you look upon this verse
When I perhaps compounded am with clay,
Do not so much as my poor name rehearse,
But let your love even with my life decay,
Lest the wise world should look into your moan,
And mock you with me after I am gone.

###67###
My mistress' eyes are nothing like the sun;
Coral is far more red than her lips' red;
If snow be white, why then her breasts are dun;
If hairs be wires, black wires grow on her head.
I have seen roses damask'd, red and white,
But no such roses see I in her cheeks;
And in some perfumes is there more delight
Than in the breath that from my mistress reeks.
I love to hear her speak, yet well I know
That music hath a far more pleasing sound;
I grant I never saw a goddess go;
My mistress, when she walks, treads on the ground.
And yet, by heaven, I think my love as rare
As any she belied with false compare.

###68###
Poor soul, the center of my sinful earth,
Fooled by these rebel powers that thee array,
Why dost thou pine within and suffer dearth,
Painting thy outward walls so costly gay?
Why so large cost, having so short a lease,
Dost thou upon thy fading mansion spend?
Shall worms, inheritors of this excess,
Eat up thy charge? Is this thy body's end?
Then, soul, live thou upon thy servant's loss,
And let that pine to aggravate thy store;
Buy terms divine in selling hours of dross;
The canker Gout within thee evermore
Shall then be fed, and, fed, live in thy breast,
And thou shalt feed on Death, that feeds on rest.

###69###
When in disgrace with fortune and men's eyes,
I all alone beweep my outcast state,
And trouble deaf heaven with my bootless cries,
And look upon myself and curse my fate,
Wishing me like to one more rich in hope,
Featured like him, like him with friends possessed,
Desiring this man's art, and that man's scope,
With what I most enjoy contented least;
Yet in these thoughts myself almost despising,
Haply I think on thee, and then my state,
Like to the lark at break of day arising
From sullen earth, sings hymns at heaven's gate;
For thy sweet love remembered such wealth brings
That then I scorn to change my state with kings.

###70###
That time of year thou mayst in me behold
When yellow leaves, or none, or few, do hang
Upon those boughs which shake against the cold,
Bare ruin'd choirs, where late the sweet birds sang.
In me thou see'st the twilight of such day
As after sunset fadeth in the west,
Which by and by black night doth take away,
Death's second self, that seals up all in rest.
In me thou see'st the glowing of such fire
That on the ashes of his youth doth lie,
As the death-bed whereon it must expire,
Consumed with that which it was nourished by.
This thou perceivest, which makes thy love more strong,
To love that well which thou must leave ere long.

###71###
Some say thy fault is youth, some want of skill,
Some say thy grace is of a common birth,
Some say thy humour is too much self-will,
Some say thy beauty is not of this earth.
But these defects, as I with sorrow learn,
Are but the shadows which thy virtues make;
For on thy face a thousand graces burn,
And from thine eye a thousand loves do wake.
My love to thee no limit can embrace,
No bound confine, no measure can define,
For in thy heart doth dwell such truth and grace,
As makes all other beauties seem less fine.
Thus, though the world may chide thy youth's excess,
My love for thee shall never grow the less.

###72###
The glorious lamp of heaven, the sun,
Doth not disdain to shine upon the stream,
Nor doth the ocean scorn to turn and run
From mighty banks to kiss the earth's soft dream.
Even so, my love, a greater light than these,
A purer flame doth in thy spirit dwell,
That warms my heart and puts my soul at ease,
And banishes the power of any spell.
For in thine eyes I see a gentle ray
That speaks of kindness and a noble mind,
And in thy smile there dawns a brighter day
That leaves all earthly troubles far behind.
So let no cloud obscure this beauteous sight,
But shine, my love, with ever constant light.

###73###
Let those who are in favour with their stars
Of public honour and proud titles boast,
Whilst I, whom fortune of such triumph bars,
Unlooked for joy in that I honour most.
Great princes' favourites their fair leaves spread
But as the marigold at the sun's eye,
And in themselves their pride lies buried,
For at a frown they in their glory die.
The painful warrior famousèd for fight,
After a thousand victories once foiled,
Is from the book of honour razed quite,
And all the rest forgot for which he toiled.
Then happy I, that love and am beloved,
Where I may not remove nor be removed.

###74###
O, lest the world should task you to recite
What commendations you afford to me,
And that deep shame might ere invade your sight,
And make you blush that you have loved so free,
Then say, my love, that you have known me ill,
That you did vouch that I was something worth,
But that a show of doubt did ever fill
My deeds, and gave my tongue a double birth.
For if you were to speak to praise my name,
Your truth might then be deemed a loving lie,
To make me seem much better than I am,
And so your honour suffer by and by.
Then do not, when my heart hath ceased to beat,
Too lavish love upon my poor name greet.

###75###
As an unperfect actor on the stage,
Who with his fear is put beside his part,
Or some fierce thing replete with too much rage,
Whose strength's abundance weakens his own heart,
So I, for fear of trust, forget to say
The perfect ceremony of love's rite,
And in mine own love's strength seem to decay,
O'ercharged with burthen of mine own love's might.
O, let my books be then the eloquence
And dumb presagers of my speaking breast,
Who plead for love and look for recompense,
More than that tongue that more hath more expressed.
O, learn to read what silent love hath writ:
To hear with eyes belongs to love's fine wit.

###76###
Why is my verse so barren of new pride,
So far from variation or quick change?
Why with the time do I not glance aside
To new-found methods and to compounds strange?
Why write I still all one, ever the same,
And keep invention in a noted weed,
That every word doth almost tell my name,
Showing their birth and where they did proceed?
O, know, sweet love, I always write of you,
And you alone fill every line I pen,
And what is new is but what is most true,
For in your beauty is my freshest ken.
So all my poems to one subject cleave,
And what is yours, in them, I strive to leave.

###77###
How like a winter hath my absence been
From thee, the pleasure of the fleeting year!
What freezing blasts have I endured unseen!
What bitter storms of woe, and how much fear!
What freezings have I felt, what dark despair,
When all the joys of summer were away!
My love for thee, a bloom beyond compare,
Did languish then, and faded day by day.
But now, with thy sweet presence, spring returns,
And fills my heart with an unbounded mirth;
The chilling frost no longer sadly burns,
But warmth and solace greet the barren earth.
So let our meeting banish every pain,
And bring the sun into our lives again.

###78###
My love is as a fever, longing still
For that which longer nurseth the disease,
Feeding on that which doth preserve the ill,
The uncertain sickly appetite to please.
My reason, the physician to my love,
Angry that his prescriptions are not kept,
Hath left me, and I desperate now approve
Desire is health's decay, which makes me wept.
Past cure I am, now reason is past care,
And frantic-mad with evermore unrest;
My thoughts and my discourse as madmen's are,
At random from the truth vainly expressed;
For I have sworn thee fair, and thought thee bright,
Who art as black as hell, as dark as night.

###79###
When thou shalt be disposed to set me light,
And place my merit in the scorn of praise,
Then in thy judgment, ere thou lose me quite,
Do not in words my simple truth abase.
For I have sworn thee fair, and thought thee bright,
Who art as black as hell, as dark as night.
No, I meant to say, when thy sweet love shall cease,
And from my verse thy beauty shall depart,
When cruel time shall bring my heart no peace,
And sorrow's hand shall tear my soul apart.
Then let my lines, though poor and worn with age,
Bear witness to the love I bore for thee,
And on their fading, melancholy page,
Proclaim thy worth, for all the world to see.
So shall thy beauty live, though I am gone,
And in my rhymes, thy memory linger on.

###80###
O, for my sake do thou with fortune chide,
The guilty goddess of my harmful deeds,
That did not better for my life provide
Than public means which public manners breeds.
Thence comes it that my name receives a brand,
And almost thence my nature is subdued
To what it works in, like the dyer's hand:
Pity me then, and wish I were renewed;
Whilst, like a willing patient, I will drink
Potions of eisel 'gainst my strong infection;
No bitterness that I will bitter think,
Nor double injury of vile complexion.
But do not so; I love thee in such sort,
As, thou being mine, all mine is thy report.

###81###
When cruel Time, with his swift-footed pace,
Shall spoil the beauty of thy youthful prime,
And furrowed wrinkles mar thy lovely face,
Consuming all that's fair within his time,
Then shall the spirit of my loving verse
Preserve thy virtues from oblivion's dust,
And like a beacon, brilliantly disperse
The shadows cast by Rust's corroding rust.
Though lofty towers no more erect shall stand,
And marble effigies resign their breath,
Thy worth, upheld by this poetic hand,
Shall triumph o'er the silent pangs of death.
So long as men can breathe, or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###82###
When envious Time doth steal thy youth away,
And silver threads among thy locks are sown,
How shall thy beauty then resist decay,
When all thy former glory is o'erthrown?
Unless thou breed, to make thy beauty live,
A fair resemblance of thyself anew,
That to the world a fairer self may give,
And pay to nature what to her is due.
For then, though wrinkled age thy face deform,
And winter's frost invade thy summer's lease,
Thy young successor, sheltered from the storm,
Shall in thy freshest state thy soul appease.
This were to triumph o'er oblivion's might,
And keep thy beauty ever in our sight.

###83###
My love is like to summer's ripened day,
When golden sunbeams grace the verdant plain;
But soon, alas, these splendours pass away,
And stormy clouds return with chilling rain.
So fleeting is the beauty we behold,
A transient flower that withers in an hour;
Its vibrant hues, though bright, cannot hold
Against the blighting breath of winter's power.
Yet in my verse, thy beauty shall remain,
A constant star when other lights decline;
Nor shall oblivion triumph o'er thy name,
But shine eternal in this hallowed line.
So live thou ever, fresh and ever young,
The theme of poets, praised by every tongue.

###84###
When I behold the world's most fading show,
And see how riches melt and honours waste,
How fickle fortune makes men high and low,
And what swift ruin follows envious haste;
Then think I on thy firm and constant heart,
Whose truth no change of season can confound,
A jewel set from transient things apart,
On virtue's rock, immovably firm bound.
Let others chase the shadow of a name,
Or bend their knees to power's uncertain sway;
My truest treasure is thy loyal flame,
That shines unwavering through the darkest day.
What earthly goods compare to thee, my friend?
Thou art my guide, until my journey's end.

###85###
Mine eye hath made a league with my sad heart,
To see itself in every lovely show;
For what the eye beholds, it doth impart
Unto the heart, where thoughts of sorrow grow.
But when I gaze on thy most gracious face,
A sudden balm doth all my pains assuage;
Then banish'd is all grief from sorrow's place,
And joy doth hold my troubled soul in cage.
Thus do I find in thee my perfect cure,
A sovereign medicine for each dire ill;
Whose beauty makes my heavy spirit pure,
And bids my stormy passions to be still.
So let me ever on thy features dwell,
For in thy sight, my heart is ever well.

###86###
When I do count the clock that tells the time,
And see the brave day sunk in hideous night;
When I behold the violet in its prime,
And how its sweet perfections fade from sight;
When I perceive how swiftly seasons run,
And how the summer yields to winter's cold,
How all things fair beneath the circling sun
But for a fleeting moment can unfold;
Then do I think on thee, my dearest friend,
Whose youth and beauty time would soon consume,
Did not my verse thine essence here extend,
And rescue thee from an untimely tomb.
So shall thy grace in these my lines be seen,
Though all the world beside to dust hath been.

###87###
Let those who are in favour with their stars
Of public honour and proud titles boast,
Whilst I, whom fortune of such triumph bars,
Unlooked-for joy in thy dear love do most.
The rising sun, whose golden eye doth warm
The drowsy earth, seems not so fair to me,
Nor morning's dew, nor gentle evening's charm,
As thy sweet presence, which I long to see.
For when thou art away, my spirit faints,
And all the world seems clad in hues of grey;
No music charms, no painted beauty paints,
But solitude doth hold relentless sway.
Return, my light, and chase this gloom away,
And make my night more glorious than the day.

###88###
Some say thy face is framed of perfect grace,
And some do praise the heavens in thine eyes;
Some hold thy smile a sun in darkest place,
And some thy voice, a joy that never dies.
But how shall I, who am so little skill'd
In flatt'ring praise, thy worthy gifts define?
My simple words, by common thoughts are fill'd,
And cannot reach such excellence as thine.
Yet, were my pen inspired by thy bright fire,
Or could my heart but half thy worth contain,
Then might I hope to raise thee somewhat higher,
And make thy beauty grace this humble strain.
For truth needs not the paint of artful lies,
But shines most brightly in its native guise.

###89###
When scorned by fortune, and by men restrain'd,
I mourn alone my wretched worldly plight,
And deem my fervent prayers are but complain'd,
With heavy heart that knows no cheering light;
Then do I crave some other's brighter art,
Some richer hope, or friend of greater sway,
Wishing my soul held not so sad a part,
And longing for a far more prosperous day.
But from these glooms, when thou dost cross my mind,
My burdened spirit leaps with sudden cheer,
As sun-kissed flowers leave the earth behind,
And banish every doubt and every fear.
Thy love to me such precious comforts lend,
My sorrows cease, my heavy labours end.

###90###
Why should my verse be barren of all praise,
When every other pen doth gild thy name?
Why do I stand mute in these latter days,
While others sing thy worth with burning flame?
It is not that my love doth less abound,
But that I fear to stain thy perfect grace,
By weaving words that are not truly sound,
Or fail to capture beauty of thy face.
For when I strive to tell what in thee lies,
My clumsy tongue doth falter and doth cease;
I find no metaphor beneath the skies
To match thy worth, and bring my soul to peace.
So let my silence speak my deeper heart,
More eloquent than any printed art.

###91###
The summer's warmth, though pleasant to the sense,
Too quickly fades, and yields to autumn's chill;
And nature's fleeting, sweet munificence
But for a season can her bounty fill.
The budding rose, though fair, must lose its prime,
Its tender petals scattered by the breeze;
Such is the course of all consuming Time,
Which brings to dust the stoutest of the trees.
But thou, my love, a fairer bloom dost bear,
Whose gentle essence no decay can blight;
A timeless beauty, far beyond compare,
That shines eternally with purest light.
So let no season steal thy radiant hue,
For in my verse, thy grace shall live anew.

###92###
When I consider everything that grows
Holds in perfection but a little moment,
That this huge stage presenteth many shows
Whereon the world's best actors are but lent;
When I perceive that men as plants do fade,
And beauty's mask from every face doth fall,
How can I then this fragile world persuade
To hold thy grace beyond Time's common thrall?
O, what is strength, if swift oblivion's tide
Doth wash away all record of its fame?
What is fair youth, if it cannot abide,
But yields to ruin, leaving but a name?
My hope is this: that in these lines of mine,
Thy radiant spirit evermore shall shine.

###93###
My tongue-tied love, too fearful to express
The depth of passion which my bosom holds,
Doth often find itself in sore distress,
When silence all my truest thought enfolds.
For common words, though spoken with much art,
Cannot convey the fervor of my soul;
They seem too cold to tell my burning heart,
And lose their power, beyond my weak control.
So when I greet thee with a modest look,
And cast mine eyes upon thy beauty's bloom,
Know that a silent, unrecorded book
Of untold praises fills my lonely room.
My very gaze, a language doth impart,
More eloquent than speech, to know my heart.

###94###
O, how much more doth beauty beauteous seem
By that sweet ornament which truth doth give!
The rose looks fairer, when we it esteem
Not for its scent alone, but that it live.
The canker blooms have thorns, and summer's breath
Smells far more sweet, though tainted by the weed,
But virtue lives beyond decay and death,
If in the soul, a loyal truth take seed.
The fairest flower that blossoms in the field,
If it but perish, unregarded dies;
But if sweet virtue do its perfume yield,
It triumphs over time in deathless guise.
So thee, my dearest, virtue doth adorn,
And makes thy loveliness outshine the morn.

###95###
The eye of heaven, that doth the day control,
Doth often dim his golden, fiery face;
And every fair from fair sometime doth roll
Its destined journey through a fading space.
For shifting winds do mar the perfect spring,
And summer's heat too hot doth often burn;
The loveliest buds, when winter's cold doth sting,
To early graves and sudden endings turn.
But thy eternal summer shall not fade,
Nor lose possession of that fair thou ow'st;
Nor shall Death brag thou wander'st in his shade,
When in eternal lines to time thou grow'st.
So long as men can breathe, or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###96###
When I behold the stars, which clearly shine,
And mark their motion in the vaulted sky,
How they revolve by certain laws divine,
And through the boundless firmament do fly;
Then do I muse upon our earthly state,
And how our lives, like shadows, pass away,
Subject to fortune's ever-changing gate,
That gives us joy, then takes it on a day.
For what is man, that he should hope to stand
Against the currents of devouring time?
A fragile vessel, built of shifting sand,
That sinks at last, forgotten in its prime.
But love, which dwells within the constant heart,
Shall play no transient, but eternal part.

###97###
My thoughts, like weary travellers, do stray
To thee, whose image fills my lonely mind;
And finding no repose throughout the day,
Return at night, no joyful solace find.
For thou art gone, and with thee all my light,
My summer's warmth, my comfort and my cheer;
And I am left to dwell in endless night,
While every moment brings a starting tear.
Oh, absence, foe to every loving soul!
That dost torment me with such cruel despite,
Thou mak'st my spirit lose all self-control,
And banishest all colour from my sight.
So haste thee home, my sun, my dearest friend,
And let this weary separation end.

###98###
Not stately walls, nor columns carved in stone,
Can long withstand the ruin of Time's might;
The pride of empires, suddenly o'erthrown,
Doth vanish swift, like shadows of the night.
But thy fair name, which my poor pen doth guard,
Shall live beyond the grasp of fleeting years;
Thy gentle virtues, faithfully declared,
Shall quell oblivion's dark and chilling fears.
Though ancient tombs shall crumble into dust,
And fading memories yield their dying breath,
My steadfast lines, on which thy spirit trust,
Shall mock the ravage of relentless death.
So long as men can breathe, or eyes can see,
This verse shall live, and shall give life to thee.

###99###
The weary world, oppressed by age and care,
Doth often sigh for pleasures that are fled;
And all the burden that its creatures bear
Seems but a prelude to the silent dead.
For beauty fades, and strength doth soon decay,
And wisdom's light grows dim with passing years;
The fondest hopes, like flowers, pass away,
And joy itself dissolves in sudden tears.
But when I think on thee, my dearest love,
A sudden hope within my spirit springs;
As if a message from the heavens above
Had brought me tidings on an angel's wings.
For in thy grace, a timeless charm I find,
That soothes my soul and elevates my mind.

###100###
When in the glass I see my wrinkles grey,
And tell the hours that have stol'n my youth,
I marvel how swift time doth haste away,
And how decay encroacheth on all truth.
For every line that carves itself on me
Is but a record of the days long spent;
A silent witness that I cannot flee
The common doom to which all flesh is lent.
Then do I turn to thee, whose constant eye
Doth hold a mirror to my vanished prime;
And in thy changeless beauty I espy
A sweet defiance of all-conquering Time.
So live thou ever fair, and ever new,
While I in thee my fading self renew.

###101###
When I behold thy face, so fair and bright,
A summer's day, thy beauty doth outshine;
I fear the fading of such sweet delight,
As envious Time will steal what now is thine.
For swift he flies, with scythe and ruthless hand,
To mow down youth and vigour in their prime;
And leave but ruins on a barren land,
A monument to his all-conquering time.
Yet in my verse, thy grace shall ever live,
A spirit breathed that never can decay;
To future ages, what I here do give,
Shall bear thy image, though thou pass away.
So shalt thou triumph o'er the dust and grave,
And beauty's essence from oblivion save.

###102###
My love is true, though silent I appear,
And seem to lack the praise that others show;
No lavish words my honest heart can bear,
For deepest streams do often softly flow.
When first I sung, my verse did soar on high,
To celebrate thy worth with fervent art;
But now I find, to speak would be to lie,
For silent love doth hold my swelling heart.
I have no need of freshly painted phrase,
To tell the world how dear thou art to me;
My constant heart, through all my length of days,
Shall speak more truth than any poetry.
So think not less, because I hold my tongue,
My love is grown, when songs are left unsung.

###103###
What can I give, to make thy beauty last,
When summer's gold shall turn to winter's grey?
And all thy grace, by cruel seasons cast,
Be swept by time, and withered quite away?
No painter's brush, nor poet's gilded pen,
Can catch the shadow of thy fleeting grace;
For art itself, though praised by mortal men,
Doth strive in vain to hold thee in thy place.
I see thee now, a rose within its bloom,
A transient wonder, fading by degrees;
And ponder deep upon thy coming doom,
Which steals thy glory, borne upon the breeze.
My love alone, eternal, shall not die,
But watch thy passing with a weeping eye.

###104###
Sweet sleep, whose gentle hand doth close mine eyes,
A brief respite from labour and from care;
Thou dost present a world where fancy flies,
And dreams, like painted ships, float in the air.
Anon I walk in fields of endless green,
Or speak with forms long vanished from the light;
And oft my love, in fairer guise, is seen,
A living star, that shines throughout the night.
But when the cock doth crow, and morning breaks,
And weary lids reveal the waking day,
The lovely vision swift its flight forsakes,
And melts, like frost, and vanishes away.
So sweet illusion doth my senses hold,
A priceless tale, too quickly to unfold.

###105###
The morning star doth herald forth the day,
And chase the lingering shadows from the sky;
So from my heart doth sorrow flee away,
When thy bright presence meets my longing eye.
For like a sun, thy beams do light my soul,
Dispelling clouds that hover o'er my mind;
And make my fractured spirit once more whole,
Leaving no trace of sadness there behind.
What bliss is this, that thou dost so impart,
A balm to grief, a solace to despair?
Thou art the sovereign medicine for my heart,
Beyond compare, a treasure rich and rare.
So let me bask within thy gentle ray,
And let my darkness yield to thy sweet day.

###106###
When I remember how I once did err,
And waste my youth on folly's fleeting call,
My heart doth blush, and trembles with a fear,
Lest I should stumble, and again should fall.
But then I cast my gaze upon thy truth,
Thy constant love, that doth my spirit guide;
And find renewal for my faded youth,
As by thy grace, my straying steps abide.
Thou art the anchor 'gainst life's stormy blast,
The steady compass, when my course is lost;
A memory of virtues from the past,
A future hope, whatever be the cost.
So let my errors be by thee forgiven,
And lead my soul along the path to heaven.

###107###
My verse is poor, its numbers do not climb
To reach the height of praise thy worth demands;
It struggles 'gainst the tyranny of Time,
With halting step, through barren, shifting sands.
Yet be not scornful of its humble flight,
Though stronger wings might bear a heavier theme;
For in its weakness lies its truest might,
To capture shadows of a lovely dream.
What cannot words, though finely spun and wrought,
Express of that which lies beyond all speech?
The silent passion, deeply felt and thought,
No tongue can utter, no mere phrase can reach.
So read my lines, not for their outward show,
But for the love that makes their current flow.

###108###
Shall I compare thee to a jewel's gleam?
Or to the silver moon, in beauty bright?
No, for those fading objects are a dream,
While thy true essence burns with constant light.
The diamond may be shorn from earthly dust,
The moon may wane, and hide her gentle face;
But in thy heart I find enduring trust,
A steadfast virtue time cannot efface.
For outward forms are subject to decay,
And beauty's flower will wither on the stem;
But spirit's grace doth never pass away,
A changeless worth, beyond all diadem.
So let the world observe what it holds dear,
My love for thee shall quell all doubt and fear.

###109###
Though absence be a bitter, cruel behest,
To tear apart what love hath closely bound;
And leave the heart with disquiet and unrest,
Where only echoes of past joys are found.
Yet in this void, where shadows seem to cling,
Thy memory shines, a beacon in the night;
And to my soul, sweet consolation bring,
A living presence, banishing all fright.
For true affection knows no bounds of space,
Nor fears the distance that divides us now;
But holds thy image in its sacred place,
A silent promise, sealed by love's own vow.
So though we part, and distant paths we tread,
My heart remains where thy dear form is bred.

###110###
Some say that love is but a summer's sport,
A fleeting fancy, born of idle hours;
A fragile bloom, of too-too short a sort,
That wilts and dies 'neath autumn's frosty showers.
But they know not the depths where true love dwells,
The steadfast root, that braves the bitter blast;
A hidden spring, whose sacred current swells,
And holds its strength, though all else fades too fast.
For love that's built on beauty's changing sand,
Or fickle fortune, soon will come to naught;
But that which springs from honour's firm command,
Is proof 'gainst harm, and cannot be un-wrought.
So let the seasons turn, and ages roll,
My deathless love shall keep its loyal soul.

###111###
When fickle Fortune frowns upon my state,
And envious rumour doth my name defame;
When friends forsake me to a cruel fate,
And mock my sorrow, adding to my shame.
Then do I turn, with a despairing eye,
To recollect the days of brighter cheer;
And wish for death, to lay me down and die,
And end the burden of each passing year.
But then I think upon thy gentle grace,
Thy constant heart, that never will deceive;
And straight my spirit seeks its rightful place,
And learns anew to hope, and to believe.
For in thy love, my riches I behold,
Worth more than silver, or than purest gold.

###112###
Full many a time I marvel at the power
Of cruel deceit, that lurks beneath fair show;
How smooth-tongued treachery, in an evil hour,
Can cause the seeds of bitter hate to grow.
For friendships forged in youth, and sealed by trust,
Are often rent by malice and by spite;
And loyal hearts are trampled into dust,
Extinguished quite, like torches in the night.
O, what a wound, when those we held most dear,
Do turn their faces, and become our foe!
It poisons life, and fills the soul with fear,
And plants within a seed of endless woe.
So let me guard my heart from outward pride,
And trust in few, where constant truths abide.

###113###
If from my verse some flaws do clearly stand,
And rude construction mar its halting gait;
Consider, love, it flows from one whose hand
Is weak, and often trembles 'gainst harsh fate.
The cunning craftsman, with his practised skill,
Can shape his words to catch the public's eye;
But mine, alas, obey a broken will,
And lack the grace of polished poetry.
Yet as a child, with stammering tongue doth try
To tell its mother of its deep desire;
So I, though fumbling, lift my voice on high,
And cast my fervent feelings on love's fire.
So take my lines, despite their fault and strain,
For they are born of truth, and not of gain.

###114###
The fleeting breath, that animates our clay,
Is but a loan, from nature's boundless store;
Which she demands, at some appointed day,
To mingle with the dust, and be no more.
What then of beauty, youth, and all that's fair?
Shall they descend to earth, and perish quite?
And leave no trace, no memory in the air,
But sink forgotten into endless night?
Ah no! For reason doth our minds engage,
To seek a solace 'gainst this certain doom;
And find in art, a timeless, sacred stage,
Where fading glories can regain their bloom.
So live, my verse, when I am laid to rest,
And bear her praises, living, and blessed.

###115###
When first I loved, I thought my passion strong,
A monument against the passing years;
But little did I know, I did it wrong,
To set a limit on my hopes and fears.
For time, that doth all other things consume,
And feeds upon the beauty of the rose,
Hath only served to banish doubt and gloom,
And made my love with fuller current flows.
No longer do I fear what years may bring,
Nor let my heart be troubled by decay;
For every moment doth new solace bring,
And make my devotion brighter every day.
So though my early vows might seem too slight,
My present love burns with a purer light.

###116###
Let me not to the marriage of true minds
Admit impediments. Love is not love
Which alters when it alteration finds,
Or bends with the remover to remove.
O no! it is an ever fixed mark
That looks on tempests and is never shaken;
It is the star to every wandering bark,
Whose worth's unknown, although his height be taken.
Love's not Time's fool, though rosy lips and cheeks
Within his bending sickle's compass come;
Love alters not with his brief hours and weeks,
But bears it out even to the edge of doom.
If this be error and upon me proved,
I never writ, nor no man ever loved.

###117###
Why should my muse, whose habit is to praise,
Be silent now, when most it should be heard?
And let the moments of these fleeting days
Pass by, without a single loving word?
Perhaps my heart, too full of what it feels,
Doth lose its cunning, and forgets to speak;
As over-flowing spring its current seals,
And finds no channel for the words it seeks.
Or else, perchance, I fear to spoil thy grace,
With common phrase, or praise that falls too low;
And rather choose to hide thy beauteous face,
Than let its lustre in my weak lines show.
Forgive my fault, if silence seems offence,
My love's too deep for outward eloquence.

###118###
The weary sun, when he hath run his course,
And drawn his golden chariot from the sky,
Leaves all the world to sleep's refreshing force,
And bids the toiling sons of men to lie.
So too my spirit, vexed by daily strife,
Doth yearn for rest, when shadows longer grow;
To find sweet solace from the cares of life,
And let the troubled thoughts forgotten go.
Then comes the night, with soft and silent tread,
And spreads her velvet mantle o'er the ground;
While in my dreams, by purest fancy led,
My truest comforts, sleeping, I have found.
So let me yield to slumber's kind embrace,
And find in dreams thy sweet and gentle face.

###119###
As in a glass, I see the passing years,
The fleeting moments, swift on silent wing;
And mark the havoc, mingled with my tears,
That Time's relentless scythe doth ever bring.
The youthful brow, once smooth and free from care,
Is etched with lines, where sorrows take their hold;
The raven locks, now bleached to silver hair,
A tale of autumn, sadly to be told.
What then avails our beauty, or our might?
When envious age doth steal what youth hath lent?
And plunges all to everlasting night,
The fleeting pleasures, and the moments spent.
But in my verse, thy youth shall ever bloom,
Defying Time, and conquering the tomb.

###120###
Though others flatter, with a honeyed tongue,
And build their praise on falsehood's brittle base;
And dedicate to thee a poem sung,
Whose empty words hold not a truth's embrace.
My simple lines, though lacking artful skill,
Are forged in fire, from my heart's own flame;
And echo forth my firm, unwavering will,
To celebrate thy virtue, and thy name.
For truth needs not the paint of gilded lie,
To show its worth, or make its beauty known;
It shines more bright beneath an honest sky,
Than any jewel falsely overgrown.
So take my love, unvarnished and sincere,
A faithful offering, held forever dear.

###121###
Shall I compare thee to a summer's day?
Thou art more lovely and more temperate:
Rough winds do shake the darling buds of May,
And summer's lease hath all too short a date:
Sometime too hot the eye of heaven shines,
And often is his gold complexion dimm'd;
And every fair from fair sometime declines,
By chance or nature's changing course untrimm'd;
But thy eternal summer shall not fade,
Nor lose possession of that fair thou ow'st;
Nor shall Death brag thou wander'st in his shade,
When in eternal lines to time thou grow'st:
So long as men can breathe or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###122###
How heavy do I journey on the way,
When what I seek, my heart hath left behind?
A tiresome trip, to pass each lagging day,
For with my thoughts thy image doth I find.
My eyes do draw thy semblance from my mind,
Which makes them wan and dull with pining gaze,
But when I shut them, then thy form I bind,
And through the darkness thy bright beauty plays.
For then my soul's imaginary sight
Presents thy shape, which takes my breath away;
But ah! this bliss is banished by the light,
And I awake to an unhappy day.
So sleep and dreams my dearest comfort prove,
To bring thee near, whom waking I remove.

###123###
Look in thy glass, and tell the face thou viewest,
Now is the time that face should form another,
Whose fresh repair if now thou not renewest,
Thou dost beguile the world, unbless a mother.
For where is she so fair whose unear'd womb
Disdains the tillage of thy husbandry?
Or who is he so fond will be the tomb
Of his self-love, to stop posterity?
Thou art thy mother's glass, and she in thee
Calls back the lovely April of her prime:
So thou through windows of thine age shalt see,
Despite of wrinkles, this thy golden time.
But if thou live, remember'd not to be,
Die single, and thine image dies with thee.

###124###
Unthrifty loveliness, why dost thou spend
Upon thyself thy beauty's legacy?
Nature's bequest gives nothing, but doth lend,
And being frank she lends to those are free.
Then, beauteous niggard, why dost thou abuse
The bounteous largess given thee to give?
Profitless usurer, why dost thou use
So great a sum of sums, yet canst not live?
For having traffic with thyself alone,
Thou of thyself thy sweet self dost deceive:
Then how, when Nature calls thee to be gone,
What audit canst thou make, to leave?
No love toward others in thy bosom dwells,
But for thyself thy beauty feeds and swells.

###125###
Those hours, that with gentle work did frame
The lovely gaze where every eye doth dwell,
Will play the tyrants to the very same,
And the sweet beauty which no power can quell.
For never-resting time leads summer on
To hideous winter and confounds him there;
Sap checked with frost, and lusty leaves quite gone,
Beauty o'ersnowed and bareness every where:
Then, were not summer's distillation left,
A liquid prisoner pent in walls of glass,
Beauty's effect with beauty were bereft,
Nor it, nor no remembrance what it was.
But flowers distill'd, though with winter meet,
Lose but their show; their substance still is sweet.

###126###
Then let not winter's ragged hand deface
In thee thy summer, ere thou be distill'd:
Make sweet some vial, ere time brings disgrace,
With precious liquor, by thyself fulfill'd.
For ere thou view'st this withering in thy prime,
Piteous to thee, that fair should fade so fast,
Thine own dear beauty might outlive harsh time,
By having thy sweet likeness ever last.
That use this treasure to enrich the world,
Not for thyself, but to give it to all;
By duty bound, let thy true love be hurl'd
On one who will, thy graces not let fall.
Be not self-will'd, for beauty's end is waste,
And beauty's use with no less bounty graced.

###127###
Lo, in the orient when the gracious light
Lifts up his burning head, each eye doth gaze
On his new-risen majesty and might,
Adoring with the universal praise.
When, high above, he doth alight the air,
With golden face to freshly gild the skies,
All eyes, by his effect, still fixed are,
But when from highmost pitch he starts to rise,
Downward to west, his weary carriage bends,
Then eyes, that looked on his noontide glory,
Now track him from the steep, as he descends,
Concluding him in age's common story.
So thou, thyself outgoing in thy noon,
Unlook'd upon, unless thou get a son.

###128###
Music to hear, why hear'st thou music sadly?
Sweets with sweets war not, joy delights in joy.
Why lov'st thou that which thou receiv'st not gladly,
Or else receiv'st with pleasure thine annoy?
If the true concord of well tuned sounds,
By unions married, do offend thine ear,
They do but sweetly chide thee, who confounds
In singleness the parts that thou shouldst bear.
Mark how one string, sweet husband to another,
Strikes each in each by mutual ordering;
Resembling sire and child and happy mother,
Which all in one, distinctively do sing.
Whose speechless song, being many, seeming one,
Sings this to thee: 'Thou single wilt prove none.'

###129###
The expense of spirit in a waste of shame
Is lust in action; and till action, lust
Is perjured, murderous, bloody, full of blame,
Savage, extreme, rude, cruel, not to trust;
Enjoy'd no sooner but despised straight,
Past reason hunted; and no sooner had,
Past reason hated, as a swallow'd bait,
