On purpose laid to make the taker mad.
Mad in pursuit, and in possession so;
Had, having, and in quest to have, extreme;
A bliss in proof, and proved, a very woe;
Before, a joy proposed; behind, a dream.
All this the world well knows; yet none knows well
To shun the heaven that leads men to this hell.

###130###
My mistress' eyes are nothing like the sun;
Coral is far more red than her lips' red;
If snow be white, why then her breasts are dun;
If hairs be wires, black wires grow on her head.
I have seen roses damask'd, red and white,
But no such roses see I in her cheeks;
And in some perfumes is there more delight
Than in the breath that from my mistress reeks.
I love to hear her speak, yet well I know
That music hath a far more pleasing sound;
I grant I never saw a goddess go;
My mistress, when she walks, treads on the ground.
And yet, by heaven, I think my love as rare
As any she belied with false compare.

###131###
As fast as thou shalt wane, so fast thou grow'st
In one of thine, from what thou dost consume;
And that fresh blood which youngly thou bestow'st
Thou mayst call thine, when thou art in thy tomb.
For from thyself thou gav'st thy vital heat,
Which, being spent, no fresh supply can gain;
So shall thy beauty, though in part complete,
In winter's cold and barren state remain.
Thou art a fool to make thy beauty's waste,
Which is for use, not to be put to scorn,
Lest nature call thee to her final taste,
And from the world thy fairest form be torn.
Then hadst thou issue of thy self, so good,
And beauty's essence in a sweeter brood.

###132###
When I do count the clock that tells the time,
And see the brave day sunk in hideous night;
When I behold the violet past prime,
And sable curls all silver'd o'er with white;
When lofty trees I see barren of leaves,
Which erst from heat did canopy the herd,
And summer's green all girded up in sheaves,
Borne on the bier with white and bristly beard;
Then of thy beauty do I question make,
That thou among the wastes of time must go,
Since sweets and beauties do themselves forsake
And die as fast as they see others grow.
And nothing 'gainst Time's scythe can make defence
Save breed, to brave him when he takes thee hence.

###133###
How can I then be merry when I part
From thee, the best of dearest company?
Since in a prison vile thou hold'st my heart,
No word of comfort can be spoke to me.
For if I suffer in a living hell,
And thou the cause of my tormenting pain,
How can I look for aught that may fare well,
When every hope is blasted by thy disdain?
My bosom's lord, that sits upon my throne,
Is captive led by thy imperious eye;
No freedom have I, save to make my moan,
And wish for death, that I might gladly die.
The selfsame wound which makes my spirit bleed,
Doth force my tongue to utter thee with need.

###134###
When I survey the heavens' eternal frame,
And mark the course of each celestial light,
How nature's wisdom doth herself proclaim,
From whose fair influence day derives her right;
When I consider how the wondrous earth
In constant order moves in her estate,
And how the stars, by their harmonious birth,
All do their fixèd destinies await;
Then do I gaze upon thy face, my love,
Which holds a grace beyond all starry spheres,
And swear that thou art sent from powers above,
To still my doubts and banish all my fears.
For in thy beauty dwells a truth so rare,
That heaven's own wonders can with thee compare.

###135###
When I consider everything that grows
Holds in perfection but a little moment,
That this huge stage presenteth nought but shows
Whereon the stars in secret influence comment;
When I perceive that men as plants do grow,
Cheered and check'd even by the selfsame sky,
Vaunt in their youthful sap, at height they go,
And then are felled by winter's tyranny;
Then the conceit of this inconstant stay
Sets you most rich in youth before my sight,
Where wasteful time debateth with decay,
To change your day of beauty to a night.
And all in war with time for love of you,
As he takes from you, I engraft you new.

###136###
But wherefore do not you a mightier way
Make war upon this bloody tyrant, Time?
And fortify yourself in your decay
With means more blessed than my barren rhyme?
Now stand you on the top of happy hours,
And many maiden gardens, yet unset,
With virtuous wish would bear your living flowers,
Ere that your date, your very life, be met.
But were it seen, that in this time of woes,
Your image should in canvas fairly stand,
A painted emblem, that the world well knows,
And hold your likeness in a stranger's hand.
This were to conquer time, to be your friend,
And make you live, when you yourself must end.

###137###
Who will believe my verse in time to come,
If it were fill'd with your most perfect praise?
Though yet heaven knows it is but as a tomb,
Which hides your life, and dims your glorious days.
If I could write the beauty of your eyes,
And in fresh numbers number all your graces,
The age to come would say, 'This poet lies;
Such heavenly touches ne'er touch'd earthly faces.'
So should my papers, yellowed with their age,
Be scorn'd, like old men's tales, and held unmeet
For truth, of beauty, by the wise and sage,
Only because my verse could not be sweet.
But were your living merit caught by mind,
Then truth might live, and leave my verse behind.

###138###
When my love swears that she is made of truth,
I do believe her, though I know she lies,
That she might think me some untutor'd youth,
Unlearnèd in the world's false subtleties.
Thus vainly thinking that she thinks me young,
Although I know my days are past the best,
Simply I credit her false-speaking tongue:
On both sides thus is simple truth suppress'd.
But wherefore says she not she is unjust?
And wherefore say not I that I am old?
O, love's best habit is in seeming trust,
And age in love loves not to have years told.
Therefore I lie with her and she with me,
And in our faults by lies we flatter'd be.

###139###
Devouring Time, blunt thou the lion's paws,
And make the earth devour her own sweet brood;
Pluck the keen teeth from the fierce tiger's jaws,
And burn the long-lived phoenix in her blood;
Make glad and sorry seasons as thou fleet'st,
And do whate'er thou wilt, swift-footed Time,
To the wide world and all her fading sweets;
But I forbid thee one most heinous crime:
O, carve not with thy hours my love's fair brow,
Nor draw no lines there with thine antique pen;
Him in thy course untainted do allow
For beauty's pattern to succeeding men.
Yet do thy worst, old Time: despite thy wrong,
My love shall in my verse ever live young.

###140###
Thou art so fair, so sweet, and so divine,
That in thy face all heaven's glories meet;
No star in all the firmament doth shine
With such a grace, or essence half so sweet.
Thy lips, like rubies, promise untold joys,
Thy breath, a fragrance from the morning dew;
Thy voice, a charm that all my heart employs,
Each look of thine doth make my life anew.
But oh, thine eyes, like two bright suns at noon,
Eclipse all other lights with their bright ray;
They steal my soul, and leave me swooning soon,
And hold my spirit captive to their sway.
So cruel sweet, so powerfully they bind,
My very self within thy gaze I find.

###141###
When I behold the swift decay of youth,
And see how beauty doth with time contend,
How fleeting is the grace, the simple truth,
That even the fairest things must have an end.

Then think I on thy most resplendent face,
Which Time's fell hand will cruelly deface,
And steal from thee each charm and lovely grace,
Leaving but wrinkles where joy held its place.

But in this verse, thy spirit shall reside,
Immortal lines, where none can thee impair,
Beyond Time's ravage, and beyond his tide,
Thy youthful essence shall forever fare.

So live, fair love, though earth do claim thy dust,
My sacred lines shall hold thy beauty fast.

###142###
Let not my love be deem'd a fickle flame,
That doth depend on Fortune's shifting tide,
Nor let my truth be measured by a name,
Which shallow wits do carelessly deride.

For though the seasons change, and flowers fade,
And fleeting clouds obscure the sun's bright ray,
My constant heart, no falsehood hath betrayed,
But holds thy image firm, by night and day.

No outward show, nor Time's remorseless hand,
Shall alter this affection I possess,
Deeper than oceans, firmer than the land,
It knows no bounds, nor can it e'er grow less.

So rests my faith, immutable and deep,
While in my soul thy hallow'd form I keep.

###143###
When first thy glorious eyes did meet mine own,
A sudden light did pierce my very soul,
As if a star from heaven had been thrown,
To make my dark and troubled spirit whole.

So potent is the magic in thy gaze,
That reason flees, and passion takes its seat,
And all my former, dull and weary days,
Are banish'd quite, made instantly complete.

No sun, no moon, no fire of mortal art,
Can cast a lustre that with thine may vie,
Thou art the compass of my throbbing heart,
The living heaven reflected in thine eye.

Blest be that glance, which did my life renew,
For in thy sight, I find my truest view.

###144###
How heavy hangs the time when thou art gone,
And absence doth a cruel torment bring,
Each weary hour prolongs itself alone,
And silence listens for no voice to sing.

The sun doth shine, but lacks its former grace,
The birds make music, but I hear it not,
For joy itself hath fled from every place,
And left my spirit in a sorrowful plot.

My thoughts, like restless pilgrims, ever stray,
To seek thy form in every passing shade,
Longing for that blest moment of the day,
When thou, my star, shalt once again invade.

Return, dear love, and chase away my night,
And fill my longing soul with thy sweet light.

###145###
When I perceive the year in constant round,
From tender bud to winter's stark decay,
How summer's glory fades from verdant ground,
And youthful vigour yields to age's sway.

The blossoming rose, in beauty's briefest reign,
Is quickly pluck'd, or falls by envious frost,
A mirror true of man's ephemeral pain,
Whose precious moments are so quickly lost.

Yet nature's heart doth evermore renew,
Though outward forms may perish and decline,
So should our spirits hold a constant hue,
And not lament, but seek a grace divine.

For even as the seed gives future bloom,
So hope outlives the shadow of the tomb.

###146###
So deeply rooted is my present grief,
That no bright solace can my heart command,
Nor time, nor counsel, bringeth sweet relief,
While thou art far, in some unmeasur'd land.

My spirit droops, as flowers in the sun,
When parching heat doth steal their vital dew,
And all the joyful hours, ere thou wert gone,
Are but a memory, painted falsely true.

I walk in shadows, where no comfort dwells,
And list to whispers of a bitter moan,
My aching breast a piteous story tells,
Of joys departed, and of hope o'erthrown.

Come, gentle peace, and banish this despair,
Or let me sleep, beyond all earthly care.

###147###
If virtue's form could speak in mortal tongue,
And show herself to man in perfect grace,
Then surely she, from thy fair soul had sprung,
And took her dwelling in thy lovely face.

For truth and honour, with a gentle mien,
Do guide thy steps, and in thy counsels shine,
No base deceit, no malice can be seen,
But all is pure, and utterly divine.

Thy spirit's light doth banish every shade,
A steadfast beacon, for all those who stray,
No earthly fault hath ever thee betrayed,
Thou art a star that points the better way.

So may thy worth forever be renown'd,
Where honest hearts and loyal souls are found.

###148###
Alas, what poor deserts do I possess,
To claim a portion in thy noble thought?
My merit small, my worthiness much less,
Scarce fit to be by gentle fancy sought.

For every grace that in thy person shines,
Doth make my own defects appear more vast,
And in thy beauty, where no blemish pines,
My frailties are to all the world recast.

So doth my spirit, laden with its shame,
Shrink from the light thy peerless virtues shed,
And dares not whisper, even thy dear name,
Lest my rude breath should sully thee instead.

Yet grant me pity, though I ask for naught,
And be my love, by silent suffering caught.

###149###
Insatiate Time, whose ravenous swift flight,
Devours all beauty, and all strength lays low,
Why dost thou hasten on thy endless night,
And bid the fairest forms to fade and go?

Thy scythe spares not the babe, nor ancient tree,
But sweeps them down, to silent dust transform'd,
Yet why should love, so pure and strong and free,
By thy cold hand, be cruelly disarm'd?

O, spare my love, whose spirit knows no stain,
And let her youth escape thy jealous rage,
Or if thou must inflict thy bitter pain,
Let these my lines preserve her age to age.

For whilst the world endures, and men draw breath,
My verse shall triumph o'er thy cruel death.

###150###
Full many a mask doth hide a hollow soul,
And outward glitter oft conceals decay,
For painted cheeks, and words beyond control,
Do lead the simple, honest heart astray.

The shallow stream may loudly boast its might,
While silent rivers run both deep and wide,
And gaudy plumes may mock the eye with light,
But lack the substance that true birds provide.

So too in man, where much appears so fair,
A secret fault may fester at the core,
But thou, dear friend, whose virtues none compare,
Art honest gold, and covet'st nothing more.

Let outward show deceive the common eye,
Thy inner grace doth truly dignify.

###151###
What folly makes me dote on thee so much,
That reason's voice can find no place to dwell?
And yields my judgement to thy lightest touch,
As if by some enchanting, potent spell?

My waking thoughts, my dreams by night are thine,
My weary footsteps follow where thou goest,
And all my being, by a power divine,
To serve thy will, its every strength bestowest.

I know thou art not perfect, nor unblemish'd,
And can discern thy faults with sober eye,
Yet by this passion, my poor soul is ravish'd,
And cannot choose but love thee till I die.

O love, thou art a tyrant, strong and blind,
That holds in thrall both body, soul, and mind.

###152###
When silent night doth draw her sable veil,
And weary men do yield to soft repose,
My watchful spirit doth its grief bewail,
And counts the sorrows which my bosom knows.

The moon, pale empress, through the clouds doth peep,
And stars, like diamonds, glimmer in the sky,
But all their beauty cannot make me sleep,
Nor turn from thee my long and weeping eye.

For in the darkness, memory doth play,
And brings thy image to my waking sight,
Rehearsing charms that graced a brighter day,
And fills my lonely chamber with thy light.

Thus watch I through the dark hours, till the morn,
And wish the day, that thou might'st be new-born.

###153###
If nature's hand did ever strive to frame
A model fair of all that's good and true,
And sought to put perfection in a name,
That name, my love, she surely gave to you.

For in thy form, all graces do combine,
No single fault doth mar thy outward show,
A soul as pure as any work divine,
A mind where sweetest virtues ever grow.

Thy gentle voice, a music to mine ear,
Thy modest mien, a lesson to behold,
Dispels all doubt, and conquers every fear,
A tale of goodness, centuries old.

So perfect art thou, in each part and whole,
The very essence of a noble soul.

###154###
My reason strives to hold its rightful sway,
And bids my heart from foolish thoughts refrain,
To cast thy image from my mind away,
And seek a peace it cannot now attain.

But passion, like a tyrant, holds its ground,
And laughs to scorn what sober sense may preach,
No firm decree, no argument is found,
To curb the longing that my soul doth reach.

For when I think to quell this wild desire,
And call forth wisdom to regain control,
It burns anew, with an intenser fire,
And takes complete possession of my soul.

Thus am I torn, by love's relentless war,
And yield to that which I should most abhor.

###155###
When I behold the furrows on my brow,
And mark the traces Time hath left behind,
How swiftly doth my youthful vigour bow,
To shadows born of an aging, weary mind.

My once bright locks, now silver'd by the years,
My strength diminish'd, and my eyesight dim,
Each mirror shows a tale of rising fears,
A fading image, standing at life's brim.

And then I think upon thy beauty's bloom,
Which yet doth hold its unimpaired grace,
And wish that Time, who speeds me to my doom,
Would grant to thee a more enduring space.

But 'tis in vain; his scythe doth spare no man,
And all must yield unto his cruel plan.

###156###
When heavy thoughts oppress my troubled breast,
And dark despair doth vex my weary soul,
I find in thee my solace and my rest,
Thou art the haven where my sorrows roll.

For then thy image rises in my sight,
A gentle balm, to soothe my inner pain,
And banishes the shadows of the night,
Making my spirit bright with joy again.

The world outside, with all its bitter strife,
And fortune's blows, which often make me sigh,
Seem but as trifles, in this precious life,
When thy sweet presence doth my heart draw nigh.

So bless'd am I, by this dear love of thine,
Which makes my darkest hours brightly shine.

###157###
When first thy voice, like music from above,
Did steal its sweetness to my listening ear,
I felt a rapture, born of purest love,
And banished every doubt, and every fear.

So clear the tones, so gentle and so deep,
As if a spirit did within them dwell,
That in my heart, I strive their echo keep,
And find therein a magic, potent spell.

No instruments, though masterfully played,
Can stir the soul with such divine accord,
Nor truest poet, by his art convey'd,
A melody more perfectly adored.

O, let me hear that blessed sound once more,
And ever stand enchanted, and adore.

###158###
What fiend is this, that haunts my troubled sleep,
And fills my waking hours with bitter dread?
That bids my jealous heart its vigil keep,
And sows suspicion in my anxious head?

It whispers tales of falsehood in mine ear,
And paints strange phantoms to delude my sight,
And makes me doubt the love I hold so dear,
Transforming day to an eternal night.

O, why should shadows mar a truth so bright?
Why should my mind give lodging to such ill?
When thou, my love, art purity and light,
And dost my trusting, faithful spirit fill.

Depart, black monster, from my soul's fair stage,
And let true love a righteous battle wage.

###159###
The lingering night, with all its solemn gloom,
At last gives way to morning's gentle ray,
And from the darkness of a silent room,
I greet the promise of another day.

The mist doth rise from meadows wet with dew,
And sleepy birds begin their joyful tune,
A world reborn, refreshed and fair and new,
Beneath the pale light of the fading moon.

So may my spirit, from its sorrow's hold,
Awaken swift, and cast its grief aside,
Let every tear, a story to be told,
Be wash'd away, by hope's returning tide.

For with the dawn, new possibilities gleam,
And life renews itself, a vibrant dream.

###160###
So long as breath shall animate my clay,
Or sight remain to view the sun's bright gleam,
So long as thoughts within my bosom play,
Or memory can hold a cherished dream.

So long as springtime brings the tender bud,
And winter's chill gives way to warmer air,
So long as ocean rolls its mighty flood,
And earth endures, with all its beauty fair.

So long, dear love, shall my affection last,
A flame unquenched, defying Time's decay,
No fleeting fancy, but a love held fast,
Until the judgment of the final day.

And when at last my mortal course is run,
My love for thee shall only be begun.

###161###
When envious Time with cruel hand doth seize
The fleeting grace that doth thy youth display,
And steal away thy beauty, and thine ease,
Leaving but wrinkles where thy smiles once lay;
When golden locks turn white with age's frost,
And vibrant spirits yield to failing breath,
And all the bloom of spring seems surely lost,
Consumed by winter's cold and silent death;
Then shall my verse, which seeks thy praise to keep,
Defy the maw of Time's consuming greed,
And though the world in endless slumber sleep,
Thy memory shall plant a living seed.
So long as men can breathe, or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###162###
Shall I compare thee to a summer's day?
Thou art more lovely and more temperate:
Rough winds do shake the darling buds of May,
And summer's lease hath all too short a date:
Sometime too hot the eye of heaven shines,
And often is his gold complexion dimmed;
And every fair from fair sometime declines,
By chance, or nature's changing course, untrimmed;
But thy eternal summer shall not fade,
Nor lose possession of that fair thou ow'st;
Nor shall Death brag thou wander'st in his shade,
When in eternal lines to time thou grow'st:
So long as men can breathe, or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###163###
The eye of heaven, when it doth most delight,
Doth oft perceive the world through clouded glass,
And fancy's hues obscure what's truly bright,
As fleeting shadows o'er green meadows pass.
So doth my heart, when it beholds thy face,
Doubt if its love be true, or but a dream,
Lost in the labyrinth of time and space,
A ship adrift upon a restless stream.
Yet when thy voice doth charm mine ear with sound,
And gentle touch doth banish every fear,
Within my soul a steady truth is found,
That thou art ever precious, ever dear.
Then let the world revolve in doubt and strife,
My love for thee is anchor to my life.

###164###
When I consider everything that grows
Holds in perfection but a little moment,
That this huge stage of earth on which it shows
Holds its brief forth with every fresh adornment;
When I perceive that men as plants do grow,
Cheered and checked even by the self-same sky,
Then green in youth, then sapless on the bough,
And nothing 'gainst Time's scythe can stand thereby;
Then of thy beauty do I question make,
That thou among the wastes of time must go,
Since sweets and beauties do themselves forsake
And die as fast as they see others grow;
And nothing 'gainst Time's might can then endure,
Save my sweet verse, which shall thy fame secure.

###165###
Full many a glorious morning have I seen
Flatter the mountain tops with sovereign eye,
Kissing with golden face the meadows green,
Gilding pale streams with heavenly alchemy;
Anon permit the basest clouds to ride
With ugly rack on his celestial face,
And from the forlorn world his visage hide,
Stealing unseen to west with this disgrace:
Even so my sun one early morn did shine
With all triumphant splendor on my brow;
But out, alack, he was but one hour mine,
The region cloud hath masked him from me now.
Yet him for this my love no whit disdaineth;
Suns of the world may stain when heaven's sun staineth.

###166###
My love is as a fever, longing still
For that which longer nurseth the disease,
Feeding on that which doth preserve the ill,
The self-same foe that doth my spirit appease.
My reason, the physician to my love,
Angry that his prescriptions are not kept,
Hath left me, and I desperate now approve
Desire is health, that was in sickness wept.
My thoughts and my discourse to thy fair eyes
Are of thy beauty, which doth me torment;
So all my senses have at thy command
No other function but to gaze and sigh.
For what I need, I do not know, nor see,
Save but to lose myself, and find in thee.

###167###
When in the chronicle of wasted time
I see descriptions of the fairest wights,
And beauty making beautiful old rhyme
In praise of ladies dead and lovely knights,
Then, in the blazon of sweet beauty's best,
Of hand, of foot, of lip, of eye, of brow,
I see their antique pen would have expressed
Even such a beauty as thou dost wear now.
So all their praises are but prophecies
Of this our time, all you prefiguring;
And, for they looked but with divining eyes,
They had not skill enough your worth to sing:
For we, which now behold these present days,
Have eyes to wonder, but lack tongues to praise.

###168###
My mistress' eyes are nothing like the sun;
Coral is far more red than her lips' red;
If snow be white, why then her breasts are dun;
If hairs be wires, black wires grow on her head.
I have seen roses damasked, red and white,
But no such roses see I in her cheeks;
And in some perfumes is there more delight
Than in the breath that from my mistress reeks.
I love to hear her speak, yet well I know
That music hath a far more pleasing sound;
I grant I never saw a goddess go;
My mistress, when she walks, treads on the ground.
And yet, by heaven, I think my love as rare
As any she belied with false compare.

###169###
No longer mourn for me when I am dead
Than you shall hear the surly sullen bell
Give warning to the world that I am fled
From this vile world, with vilest worms to dwell.
Nay, if you read this line, remember not
The hand that writ it, for I love you so,
That I in your sweet thoughts would be forgot,
If thinking on me then should make you woe.
O, if, I say, you look upon this verse
When I perhaps compounded am with clay,
Do not so much as my poor name rehearse,
But let your love even with my life decay,
Lest the wise world should look into your moan,
And mock you with me after I am gone.

###170###
When in disgrace with fortune and men's eyes,
I all alone beweep my outcast state,
And trouble deaf heaven with my bootless cries,
And look upon myself and curse my fate,
Wishing me like to one more rich in hope,
Featured like him, like him with friends possessed,
Desiring this man's art, and that man's scope,
With what I most enjoy contented least;
Yet in these thoughts myself almost despising,
Haply I think on thee, and then my state,
Like to the lark at break of day arising
From sullen earth, sings hymns at heaven's gate;
For thy sweet love remembered such wealth brings,
That then I scorn to change my state with kings.

###171###
Lo, in the orient when the gracious light
Lifts up his burning head, each under eye
Doth homage to his new-appearing sight,
Serving with looks his sacred majesty;
And having climbed unto the highest height,
Even at the furthest inch of his bright way,
Declines his glowing splendor, and then night
Doth his dim region wholly oversway.
Even so the world's large course an example makes
To thee, my loving boy, for thee to muse;
When thy sweet youth converts to yellowing flakes,
And all thy glory doth its lustre lose:
Then, if thou hast not made a child of thine,
Thou sacrific'st to time, and not to mine.

###172###
Weary with toil, I haste me to my bed,
The dear repose for limbs with travel tired;
But then begins a journey in my head,
To work my mind, when body's work's expired.
For then my thoughts, from far where I abide,
Intend a zealous pilgrimage to thee,
And keep my drooping eyelids open wide,
Looking on darkness which the blind do see:
Save that my soul's imaginary sight
Presents thy shadow to my tired eye,
Which, like a jewel hung in ghastly night,
Makes black night beauteous, and her old face new.
Lo, thus, by day my limbs, by night my mind,
For thee and for myself no quiet find.

###173###
Let those who are in favour with their stars
Of public honour and proud titles boast,
Whilst I, whom fortune of such triumph bars,
Unlooked for joy in that I honour most.
Great princes' favourites their fair leaves spread
But as the marigold at the sun's eye,
And in themselves their pride lies buried,
For at a frown they in their glory die.
The painful warrior famousèd for might,
After a thousand victories once foiled,
Is from the book of honour razed quite,
And all his 'scapes are with oblivion soiled.
Then happy I, that love and am beloved,
Where I may not remove nor be removed.

###174###
When I do count the clock that tells the time,
And see the brave day sunk in hideous night;
When I behold the violet past prime,
And sable curls all silvered o'er with white;
When lofty trees I see barren of leaves,
Which erst from heat did canopy the herd,
And summer's green all girded up in sheaves,
Borne on the bier with white and bristly beard:
Then of thy beauty do I question make,
That thou among the wastes of time must go,
Since sweets and beauties do themselves forsake
And die as fast as they see others grow;
And nothing 'gainst Time's might can then endure,
Save my sweet verse, which shall thy fame secure.

###175###
To me, fair friend, you never can be old,
For as you were when first your eye I eyed,
Such seems your beauty still. Three winters cold
Have from the forests shook three summers' pride,
Three beauteous springs to yellow autumn turned
In process of the seasons have I seen,
Three April perfumes in three hot Junes burned,
Since first I saw you fresh, which yet are green.
Ah, yet doth beauty, like a dial hand,
Steal from his figure, and no pace perceived;
So your sweet semblance, which I hold in hand,
Hath motion, and mine eye may be deceived.
For fear of which, hear this, thou age unbred:
Ere you were born was beauty's summer dead.

###176###
From fairest creatures we desire increase,
That thereby beauty's rose might never die,
But as the riper should by time decrease,
His tender heir might bear his memory.
But thou, contracted to thine own bright eyes,
Feed'st thy light's flame with self-substantial fuel,
Making a famine where abundance lies,
Thyself thy foe, to thy sweet self too cruel.
Thou that art now the world's fresh ornament
And only herald to the gaudy spring,
Within thine own bud buriest thy content
And, tender churl, mak'st waste in niggarding.
Pity the world, or else this glutton be,
To eat the world's due, by the grave and thee.

###177###
Mine eye and heart are at a mortal war
How to divide the conquest of thy sight;
Mine eye my heart thy picture would bar,
My heart mine eye the freedom of that right.
My heart doth plead that thou in him dost lie,
A closet never pierced with crystal eyes,
But in his own pure thoughts thy image fly,
And makes of that thy beauty's form arise.
My eye doth scoff, and says that thou dost there
In his most perfect shape at all times rest.
Then calls my heart a liar, and doth swear
Thou art not in the chamber of his breast.
To 'cide this strife, my heart such means doth find
That upon thee on both sides still I gaze.

###178###
When my love swears that she is made of truth,
I do believe her, though I know she lies,
That she might think me some untutored youth,
Unlearnèd in the world's false subtleties.
Thus vainly thinking that she thinks me young,
Although she knows my days are past the best,
Simply I credit her false-speaking tongue:
On both sides thus is simple truth suppressed.
But wherefore says she not she is unjust?
And wherefore say not I that I am old?
O, love's best habit is in seeming trust,
And age in love loves not to have years told.
Therefore I lie with her, and she with me,
And in our faults by lies we flattered be.

###179###
Like as the waves make towards the pebbled shore,
So do our minutes hasten to their end;
Each changing place with that which goes before,
In sequent toil all forwards do contend.
Nativity, once in the main of light,
Crawls to maturity, wherewith being crowned,
Crooked eclipses 'gainst his glory fight,
And Time that gave doth now his gift confound.
Time doth transfix the flourish set on youth,
And delves the parallels in beauty's brow,
Feeds on the rarities of nature's truth,
And nothing stands but for his scythe to mow.
And yet to times in hope my verse shall stand,
Praising thy worth, despite his cruel hand.

###180###
Accusing heaven, I curse my bitter fate,
That I was born with a too eager mind,
To love so deeply, and to soon abate
The gentle pleasures that my soul doth find.
For often do I grasp at fleeting joys,
And hold them close, as if they were my own,
But like a child who breaks his fragile toys,
I find their brief enchantment quickly flown.
Then in the ruins of my own desire,
I seek a solace that I cannot name,
A hidden spark within a dying fire,
To fan again the passion's fading flame.
But all in vain, for sorrows ever creep,
And leave me nothing but to watch and weep.

###181###
When forty winters shall besiege thy brow,
And dig deep trenches in thy beauty's field,
Thy youthful garment, which doth grace thee now,
Shall then a tattered, worn-out raiment yield.
Then being ask'd where all thy beauty lies,
Where all the treasure of thy lusty days,
To say within thine own deep-sunken eyes,
Were an all-eating shame and thriftless praise.
How much more praise deserv'd thy beauty's use,
If thou couldst answer "This my fair child is,"
To silence that which doth thy youth abuse,
And prove thy beauty by succession's bliss!
This were to be new made when thou art old,
And see thy blood warm when thou feel'st it cold.

###182###
Shall I compare thee to a summer's day?
Thou art more lovely and more temperate:
Rough winds do shake the darling buds of May,
And summer's lease hath all too short a date:
Sometime too hot the eye of heaven shines,
And often is his gold complexion dimm'd;
And every fair from fair sometime declines,
By chance, or nature's changing course, untrimm'd;
But thy eternal summer shall not fade,
Nor lose possession of that fair thou ow'st;
Nor shall Death brag thou wander'st in his shade,
When in eternal lines to time thou grow'st:
So long as men can breathe or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###183###
When in disgrace with fortune and men's eyes,
I all alone beweep my outcast state,
And trouble deaf heaven with my bootless cries,
And look upon myself and curse my fate,
Wishing me like to one more rich in hope,
Featur'd like him, with friends possess'd,
Desiring this man's art and that man's scope,
With what I most enjoy contented least;
Yet in these thoughts myself almost despising,
Haply I think on thee, and then my state,
Like to the lark at break of day arising
From sullen earth, sings hymns at heaven's gate;
For thy sweet love rememb'red such wealth brings
That then I scorn to change my state with kings.

###184###
When to the sessions of sweet silent thought
I summon up remembrance of things past,
I sigh the lack of many a thing I sought,
And with old woes new wail my dear time's waste:
Then can I drown an eye, unus'd to flow,
For precious friends hid in death's dateless night,
And weep afresh love's long since cancell'd woe,
And moan the expense of many a vanish'd sight:
Then can I grieve at grievances foregone,
And heavily from woe to woe tell o'er
The sad account of fore-bemoaned moan,
Which I new pay as if not paid before.
But if the while I think on thee, dear friend,
All losses are restor'd and sorrows end.

###185###
Not marble, nor the gilded monuments
Of princes, shall outlive this powerful rhyme;
But you shall shine more bright in these contents
Than unswept stone besmear'd with sluttish time.
When wasteful war shall statues overturn,
And broils root out the work of masonry,
Nor Mars his sword nor war's quick fire shall burn
The living record of your memory.
'Gainst death and all-oblivious enmity
Shall you pace forth; your praise shall still find room
Even in the eyes of all posterity
That wear this world out to the ending doom.
So, till the judgement that yourself arise,
You live in this, and dwell in lovers' eyes.

###186###
My mistress' eyes are nothing like the sun;
Coral is far more red than her lips' red;
If snow be white, why then her breasts are dun;
If hairs be wires, black wires grow on her head.
I have seen roses damask'd, red and white,
But no such roses see I in her cheeks;
And in some perfumes is there more delight
Than in the breath that from my mistress reeks.
I love to hear her speak, yet well I know
That music hath a far more pleasing sound;
I grant I never saw a goddess go;
My mistress, when she walks, treads on the ground.
And yet, by heaven, I think my love as rare
As any she belied with false compare.

###187###
Devouring Time, blunt thou the lion's paws,
And make the earth devour her own sweet brood;
Pluck the keen teeth from the fierce tiger's jaws,
And burn the long-liv'd phoenix in her blood;
Make glad and sorry seasons as thou fleet'st,
And do whate'er thou wilt, swift-footed Time,
To the wide world and all her fading sweets;
But I forbid thee one most heinous crime:
O, carve not with thy hours my love's fair brow,
Nor draw no lines there with thine antique pen;
Him in thy course untainted do allow
For beauty's pattern to succeeding men.
Yet do thy worst, old Time: despite thy wrong,
My love shall in my verse ever live young.

###188###
When I do count the clock that tells the time,
And see the brave day sunk in hideous night;
When I behold the violet past prime,
And sable curls all silver'd o'er with white;
When lofty trees I see barren of leaves,
Which erst from heat did canopy the herd,
And summer's green all girded up in sheaves,
Borne on the bier with white and bristly beard,
Then of thy beauty do I question make,
That thou among the wastes of time must go,
Since sweets and beauties do themselves forsake
And die as fast as they see others grow;
And nothing 'gainst Time's scythe can make defence
Save breed, to brave him when he takes thee hence.

###189###
Who will believe my verse in time to come,
If it were fill'd with your most high deserts?
Though yet, heaven knows, it is but as a tomb
Which hides your life and shows not half your parts.
If I could write the beauty of your eyes,
And in fresh numbers number all your graces,
The age to come would say "This poet lies;
Such heavenly touches ne'er touch'd earthly faces."
So should my papers, yellow'd with their age,
Be scorn'd, like old men of less truth than boast,
And your true rights be term'd a poet's rage
And stretch'd metre of an antique praise.
But were some child of yours alive that time,
You should live twice, in it and in my rhyme.

###190###
From fairest creatures we desire increase,
That thereby beauty's rose might never die,
But as the riper should by time decrease,
His tender heir might bear his memory:
But thou, contracted to thine own bright eyes,
Feed'st thy light's flame with self-substantial fuel,
Making a famine where abundance lies,
Thyself thy foe, to thy sweet self too cruel.
Thou that art now the world's fresh ornament
And only herald to the gaudy spring,
Within thine own bud buriest thy content
And, tender churl, mak'st waste in niggarding.
Pity the world, or else this glutton be,
To eat the world's due, by the grave and thee.

###191###
Let me not to the marriage of true minds
Admit impediments. Love is not love
Which alters when it alteration finds,
Or bends with the remover to remove:
O no! it is an ever-fixed mark
That looks on tempests and is never shaken;
It is the star to every wand'ring bark,
Whose worth's unknown, although his height be taken.
Love's not Time's fool, though rosy lips and cheeks
Within his bending sickle's compass come;
Love alters not with his brief hours and weeks,
But bears it out even to the edge of doom.
If this be error and upon me prov'd,
I never writ, nor no man ever lov'd.

###192###
My love is as a fever, longing still
For that which longer nurseth the disease;
Feeding on that which doth preserve the ill,
Th' uncertain sickly appetite to please.
My reason, the physician to my love,
Angry that his prescriptions are not kept,
Hath left me, and I desperate now approve
Desire is death, which physic did except.
Past cure I am, now reason is past care,
And frantic-mad with evermore unrest;
My thoughts and my discourse as madmen's are,
At random from the truth vainly express'd;
For I have sworn thee fair, and thought thee bright,
Who art as black as hell, as dark as night.

###193###
No more be griev'd at that which thou hast done:
Roses have thorns, and silver fountains mud;
Clouds and eclipses stain both moon and sun,
And loathsome canker lives in sweetest bud.
All men make faults, and even I in this,
Authorizing thy trespass with compare,
Myself corrupting, salving thy amiss,
Excusing thy sins more than thy sins are;
For to compare them with unspotted innocents,
You do but slander them and foul deface,
And by their taint unhallow their contents,
Whose purity might help thy blame to chase.
Ah, but my love, though you in faults abound,
My love doth hourly on your virtues found.

###194###
When I have seen by Time's fell hand defaced
The rich proud cost of outworn buried age;
When sometime lofty towers I see down-razed,
And brass eternal slave to mortal rage;
When I have seen the hungry ocean gain
Advantage on the kingdom of the shore,
And the firm soil win of the watery main,
Increasing store with loss, and loss with store;
When I have seen such interchange of state,
Or state itself confounded to decay,
Ruin hath taught me thus to ruminate,
That Time will come and take my love away.
This thought is as a death, which cannot choose
But weep to have that which it fears to lose.

###195###
Tired with all these, for restful death I cry,
As, to behold desert a beggar born,
And needy nothing trim in jollity,
And purest faith unhappily forsworn,
And gilded honour shamefully misplaced,
And maiden virtue rudely strumpeted,
And right perfection wrongfully disgraced,
And strength by limping sway disabled,
And art made tongue-tied by authority,
And folly doctor-like controlling skill,
And simple truth miscall'd simplicity,
And captive good attending captain ill:
Tired with all these, from these would I be gone,
Save that, to die, I leave my love alone.

###196###
How like a winter hath my absence been
From thee, the pleasure of the fleeting year!
What freezings have I felt, what dark days seen!
What old December's bareness everywhere!
And yet this time remov'd was summer's time,
The teeming autumn, big with rich increase,
Bearing the wanton burden of the prime,
Like widow'd wombs after their lords' decease:
Yet this abundant issue seem'd to me
But an unseason'd winter, dark and dull,
As if the world were all compris'd in thee,
The pleasure of the time indeed being full.
The birds are mute, the trees are deck'd with white,
And nature's joys have taken sudden flight.

###197###
Mine eye hath play'd the painter and hath stell'd
Thy beauty's form in table of my heart;
My body is the frame wherein 'tis held,
And perspective it is best painter's art.
For through the painter eyes now see his skill,
To find where your true image pictur'd lies,
Which in my bosom's shop is hanging still,
That hath his windows glazed with thine eyes.
Now see what good of this thy art can make,
Thy picture in my sight, but in my heart;
Mine eyes would draw, and thy fair form would take,
But this much less of the sweet love impart.
So I, nor eyes, nor heart, can profit gain,
For either's loss is the other's pain.

###198###
Not from the stars do I my judgement pluck;
And yet methinks I have astronomy,
But not to tell of good or evil luck,
Of plagues, of dearths, or seasons' quality;
Nor can I fortune to brief minutes tell,
Pointing to each his thunder, rain, and wind,
Or say with princes if it shall go well
By oft predict that I in heaven find:
But from thine eyes my knowledge I derive,
And, constant stars, in them I read such art
As truth and beauty shall together thrive,
If from thyself thou dost not start apart.
Then know, my love, that heaven's great eye doth see
Thy heart is pure, and therefore blesseth thee.

###199###
Being your slave, what should I do but tend
Upon the hours and times of your desire?
I have no precious time at all to spend,
Nor services to do, till you require.
Nor dare I chide the world-without-end hour
Whilst I, my sovereign, watch the clock for you,
Nor think the bitterness of absence sour
When you have bid your servant once adieu;
Nor dare I question with my jealous thought
Where you may be, or your affairs suppose,
But, like a sad slave, stay and think of nought
Save, where you are, how happy you make those.
So true a fool is love, that in your will,
Though you do anything, he thinks no ill.

###200###
O, lest the world should task you to recite
What beauty was ere it was born to you,
In dusty old oblivion and dread night,
The fresh remembrance of it doth subdue.
Then take the praise, thine own dear praise away,
Which all for want of praise doth daily die;
And to thy father's grave give that which may
Be buried there, and there unlov'd may lie.
For who is he whose beauty is not lost,
Unless he seeks his beauty to renew?
As quick declines the rose as it doth boast,
Unless it by a child be born anew.
For in thyself a fresher youth will grow,
If thou dost give thy beauty to bestow.

###201###
When forty winters shall besiege thy brow,
And dig deep trenches where thy beauty lies,
Thy youthful garment, though 'tis worn somehow,
Shall mock the gaze of ever-watchful eyes.
Then if thou ask'st where all thy beauty's gone,
Where all thy youthful vigour finds its hold,
To say that in thy soul it lingers on,
Were but to mock the world and make it cold.
But say thy love hath bred a child most fair,
Whose youthful pulse beats in thy aging heart,
Then in that issue may thy grace appear,
And keep thy name from Time's destructive dart.
So shalt thou live, when thou art past thy prime,
And show thy beauty 'gainst devouring Time.

###202###
Shall I compare thee to a summer's day?
Thou art more lovely and more temperate:
Rough winds do shake the darling buds of May,
And summer's lease hath all too short a date.
Sometime too hot the eye of heaven shines,
And often is his gold complexion dimm'd;
And every fair from fair sometime declines,
By chance or nature's changing course untrimm'd;
But thy eternal summer shall not fade,
Nor lose possession of that fair thou ow'st;
Nor shall Death brag thou wand'rest in his shade,
When in eternal lines to time thou grow'st.
So long as men can breathe or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###203###
When in disgrace with fortune and men's eyes,
I all alone beweep my outcast state,
And trouble deaf heaven with my bootless cries,
And look upon myself and curse my fate,
Wishing me like to one more rich in hope,
Featured like him, like him with friends possessed,
Desiring this man's art, and that man's scope,
With what I most enjoy contented least;
Yet in these thoughts myself almost despising,
Haply I think on thee, and then my state,
Like to the lark at break of day arising
From sullen earth, sings hymns at heaven's gate;
For thy sweet love remember'd such wealth brings,
That then I scorn to change my state with kings.

###204###
Mine eye hath play'd the painter and hath stell'd
Thy beauty's form in table of my heart;
My body is the frame wherein 'tis held,
And perspective it is best painter's art.
For through the painter eyes now hath his light,
His fair device to draw thy image true,
He only doth to that most perfect sight
The inward working of my soul imbue.
But eyes, indeed, to limn can little do,
And of thy outward parts the best can trace;
My heart itself in counsel with my view
Hath sought to make thee dwell in this poor place.
So I, by love, within thee ever dwell,
And thou in me, this story doth oft tell.

###205###
No longer mourn for me when I am dead
Than you shall hear the surly sullen bell
Give warning to the world that I am fled
From this vile world, with vilest worms to dwell.
Nay, if you read this line, remember not
The hand that writ it, for I love you so,
That I in your sweet thoughts would be forgot,
If thinking on me then should make you woe.
O, if, I say, you look upon this verse,
When I perhaps compounded am with clay,
Do not so much as my poor name rehearse,
But let your love even with my life decay,
Lest the wise world should look into your moan,
And mock you with me after I am gone.

###206###
When I do count the clock that tells the time,
And see the brave day sunk in hideous night;
When I behold the violet past prime,
And sable curls all silver'd o'er with white;
When lofty trees I see barren of leaves,
Which erst from heat did canopy the herd,
And summer's green all girded up in sheaves,
Borne on the bier with white and bristly beard,
Then of thy beauty do I question make,
That thou among the wastes of time must go,
Since sweets and beauties do themselves forsake
And die as fast as they see others grow;
And nothing 'gainst Time's scythe can make defence,
Save breed, to brave him when he takes thee hence.

###207###
My mistress' eyes are nothing like the sun;
Coral is far more red than her lips' red;
If snow be white, why then her breasts are dun;
If hairs be wires, black wires grow on her head.
I have seen roses damask'd, red and white,
But no such roses see I in her cheeks;
And in some perfumes is there more delight
Than in the breath that from my mistress reeks.
I love to hear her speak, yet well I know
That music hath a far more pleasing sound;
I grant I never saw a goddess go;
My mistress, when she walks, treads on the ground.
And yet, by heaven, I think my love as rare
As any she belied with false compare.

###208###
When in the chronicle of wasted time
I see descriptions of the fairest wights,
And beauty making beautiful old rhyme
In praise of ladies dead and lovely knights,
Then, in the blazon of sweet beauty's best,
Of hand, of foot, of lip, of eye, of brow,
I see their antique pen would have express'd
Even such a beauty as you master now.
So all their praises are but prophecies
Of this our time, all you prefiguring;
And, for they look'd but with divining eyes,
They had not skill enough your worth to sing.
For we, which now behold these present days,
Have eyes to wonder, but lack tongues to praise.

###209###
Let me not to the marriage of true minds
Admit impediments. Love is not love
Which alters when it alteration finds,
Or bends with the remover to remove.
O, no! it is an ever-fixed mark
That looks on tempests and is never shaken;
It is the star to every wand'ring bark,
Whose worth's unknown, although his height be taken.
Love's not Time's fool, though rosy lips and cheeks
Within his bending sickle's compass come;
Love alters not with his brief hours and weeks,
But bears it out even to the edge of doom.
If this be error and upon me proved,
I never writ, nor no man ever loved.

###210###
Weary with toil, I haste me to my bed,
The dear repose for limbs with travel tired;
But then begins a journey in my head,
To work my mind, when body's work's expired:
For then my thoughts, from far where I abide,
Intend a zealous pilgrimage to thee,
And keep my drooping eyelids open wide,
Looking on darkness which the blind do see:
Save that my soul's imaginary sight
Presents thy shadow to my tired view,
Which, like a jewel hung in ghastly night,
Makes black night beauteous and her old face new.
Lo, thus, by day my limbs, by night my mind,
For thee and for myself no quiet find.

###211###
When my love swears that she is made of truth,
I do believe her, though I know she lies,
That she might think me some untutor'd youth,
Unlearned in the world's false subtleties.
Thus vainly thinking that she thinks me young,
Although I know my days are past the best,
Simply I credit her false-speaking tongue:
On both sides thus is simple truth suppress'd.
But wherefore says she not she is unjust?
And wherefore say not I that I am old?
Oh, love's best habit is in seeming trust,
And age in love loves not to have years told:
Therefore I lie with her and she with me,
And in our faults by lies we flatter'd be.

###212###
From fairest creatures we desire increase,
That thereby beauty's rose might never die,
But as the riper should by time decease,
His tender heir might bear his memory:
But thou, contracted to thine own bright eyes,
Feed'st thy light's flame with self-substantial fuel,
Making a famine where abundance lies,
Thyself thy foe, to thy sweet self too cruel.
Thou that art now the world's fresh ornament
And only herald to the gaudy spring,
Within thine own bud buriest thy content
And, tender churl, mak'st waste in niggarding.
Pity the world, or else this glutton be,
To eat the world's due, by the grave and thee.

###213###
Some glory in their birth, some in their skill,
Some in their wealth, some in their body's force,
Some in their garments, though new-fangled ill,
Some in their hawks and hounds, some in their horse;
And every humour hath his adjunct pleasure,
Wherein it finds a joy above the rest:
But these particulars are not my measure;
All these I better in my love possess'd.
But as the sun doth light the world with rays,
So doth my love illuminate my days;
And as the moon the ocean doth command,
My love doth guide the motions of my hand.
For in her grace, all virtues do reside,
And by her will, my truest joys abide.

###214###
Since brass, nor stone, nor earth, nor boundless sea,
But sad mortality o'er-sways their power,
How with this rage shall beauty hold a plea,
Whose action is no stronger than a flower?
O, how shall summer's honey breath hold out
Against the wreckful siege of battering days,
When rocks impregnable are not so stout,
Nor gates of steel so strong, but Time decays?
O fearful meditation! where, alack,
Shall Time's best jewel from Time's chest lie hid?
Or what strong hand can hold his swift foot back?
Or who his spoil of beauty can forbid?
O, none, unless this miracle have might,
That in black ink my love may still shine bright.

###215###
Thy glass will show thee how thy beauties fade,
Thy dial give thee warning of thy end,
And of thy outward parts in glory laid,
The wrinkles which thy youthful brow shall bend.
Then look to it, and be not over-wise,
To think thy perfect days can e'er endure;
For when thy blood shall creep through wintry skies,
No art of youth can ever make thee pure.
What then remains but for thee to repair
Thy perishing grace, through means of issue given?
To make thy beauty in thy child appear,
Ere all thy freshness into age is driven.
For if thou dost not this, then dost thou die,
And with thee goes thy beauty to the sky.

###216###
When I have seen by Time's fell hand defaced
The rich proud cost of outworn buried age;
When sometime lofty towers I see down-razed,
And brass eternal slave to mortal rage;
When I have seen the hungry ocean gain
Advantage on the kingdom of the shore,
And the firm soil win of the watery main,
Increasing store with loss, and loss with store;
When I have seen such interchange of state,
Or state itself confounded to decay,
Ruin hath taught me thus to ruminate,
That Time will come and take my love away.
This thought is as a death, which cannot choose
But weep to have that which it fears to lose.

###217###
How like a winter hath my absence been
From thee, the pleasure of the fleeting year!
What freezings have I felt, what dark days seen!
What old December's bareness everywhere!
And yet this time removed was summer's time,
The teeming autumn, big with rich increase,
Bearing the wanton burden of the prime,
Like widowed wombs after their lords' decease:
Yet this abundant issue seemed to me
But hope of orphans and unfathered fruit;
For summer and his pleasures wait on thee,
And without thee what beauty can bear root?
Then is my summer's time an empty stage,
Where grief attends to fill my weary age.

###218###
The expense of spirit in a waste of shame
Is lust in action; and till action, lust
Is perjured, murderous, bloody, full of blame,
Savage, extreme, rude, cruel, not to trust,
Enjoy'd no sooner but despised straight;
Past reason hunted; and no sooner had,
Past reason hated, as a swallow'd bait
On purpose laid to make the taker mad:
Mad in pursuit and in possession so;
Had, having, and in quest to have, extreme;
A bliss in proof, and proved, a very woe;
Before, a joy proposed; behind, a dream.
All this the world well knows; yet none knows well
To shun the heaven that leads men to this hell.

###219###
My love is as a fever, longing still
For that which longer nurseth the disease;
Feeding on that which doth preserve the ill,
The uncertain sickly appetite to please.
My reason, the physician to my love,
Angry that his prescriptions are not kept,
Hath left me, and I desperate now approve
Desire is health's decay, which makes me wept.
Past cure I am, now reason is past care,
And frantic-mad with evermore unrest;
My thoughts and my discourse as madmen's are,
At random from the truth vainly express'd;
For I have sworn thee fair, and thought thee bright,
Who art as black as hell, as dark as night.

###220###
O, never say that I was false of heart,
Though absence seemed my flame to cool and kill;
As easy might I from myself depart
As from my soul, which in thy breast doth dwell:
That love is merchandised whose rich esteem
The owner's tongue doth publish everywhere.
Our love was new, and then but in the dream,
When we like to a pair of gods did appear.
Though blunting Time with rasure of oblation
Hath made my days of joy and hopes decay,
My fixed heart, which in thee finds foundation,
Shall never from thy perfect image stray.
For come what sorrow, or what joys befall,
My truest love doth answer to thy call.

###221###
When I behold the fleeting grace of day,
And how the cruel hand of Time doth haste,
To steal the bloom from youth, and beauty's sway,
My thoughts on thy dear loveliness are placed.
For like the summer's flower, thou art so fair,
Yet subject to the winter's frosty breath,
Which doth ere long consume all things that bear,
And gives them over to eternal death.
But in these lines, my love shall never fade,
Nor shall oblivion snatch thy gentle name,
For thou art here in deathless verse arrayed,
And brighter burn'st than any mortal flame.
So long as men can breathe, or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###222###
Shall I compare thee to a summer's day?
Thou art more lovely and more temperate:
Rough winds do shake the darling buds of May,
And summer's lease hath all too short a date.
Sometime too hot the eye of heaven shines,
And often is his gold complexion dimmed;
And every fair from fair sometime declines,
By chance, or nature's changing course, untrimmed:
But thy eternal summer shall not fade,
Nor lose possession of that fair thou ow'st;
Nor shall Death brag thou wander'st in his shade,
When in eternal lines to time thou grow'st:
So long as men can breathe, or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###223###
My love is as a fever, longing still
For that which longer nurseth the disease;
Feeding upon the hope which doth me kill,
And playing with the pain that gives me ease.
For then I have sworn thee fair, more fair than truth,
And for thy beauty set my reason by;
When my old eyes were dimmed with error's youth,
And knew not what to praise, nor what deny.
But now I see, though too late in the day,
That beauty's veil did hide thy false heart's guile;
And though I loved, I knew not how to say
My true complaint, held captive by thy smile.
Yet love, though blind, sees not the faults it bears,
And so I love thee still, despite my tears.

###224###
When in disgrace with fortune and men's eyes,
I all alone beweep my outcast state,
And trouble deaf heaven with my bootless cries,
And look upon myself, and curse my fate,
Wishing me like to one more rich in hope,
Featured like him, like him with friends possessed,
Desiring this man's art, and that man's scope,
With what I most enjoy contented least;
Yet in these thoughts myself almost despising,
Haply I think on thee, and then my state,
Like to the lark at break of day arising
From sullen earth, sings hymns at heaven's gate;
For thy sweet love remembered such wealth brings,
That then I scorn to change my state with kings.

###225###
The weary world, in endless cycle bound,
Doth oft present a glass to man's keen eye,
Where fleeting joys and sorrows can be found,
And Time's swift passage bids all beauty die.
The budding rose, with promise full and bright,
Is but a moment's wonder, then it fades;
And e'en the sun, that lends the day its light,
Must yield its glory to the evening's shades.
So too the grace that decks thy youthful brow,
Shall feel the touch of age, and be consumed;
Unless these verses, penned by fortune's plough,
Preserve thy fairness, from oblivion doomed.
Then let my ink preserve what life doth send,
That thy dear form may know no bitter end.

###226###
O, never say that I was false of heart,
Though absence seemed my love to cool and freeze;
But think that self came not in any part,
Between our hearts, which true love doth appease.
For I to thee am bound, and thou to me,
As closely as the ivy to the wall;
No space can part us, no harsh destiny,
Nor e'en the greatest tempest make us fall.
My love for thee is fixed, a constant star,
Whose steady light no worldly cloud can dim;
Though seas may roll, and travails keep me far,
My thoughts unto thy dwelling ever skim.
So trust my faith, which time can not remove,
For absence only doth refine my love.

###227###
When I do count the clock that tells the time,
And see the brave day sunk in hideous night;
When I behold the violet past prime,
And sable curls all silvered o'er with white;
When lofty trees I see barren of leaves,
Which erst from heat did canopy the herd,
And summer's green all girded up in sheaves,
Borne on the bier with white and bristly beard,
Then of thy beauty do I question make,
That thou among the wastes of time must go,
Since sweets and beauties do themselves forsake
And die as fast as they see others grow;
And nothing 'gainst Time's scythe can make defence,
Save breed, to brave him when he takes thee hence.

###228###
Mine eye hath played the painter and hath steeled
Thy outward shape, so deeply in my heart;
My body is the frame wherein 'tis held,
And for a picture is no better art.
My body is the frame, my heart the paint,
Where every hue and feature is expressed;
My soul the witness, free from all constraint,
Doth gaze upon thy form, forever blessed.
But yet the inward worth it cannot see,
Nor can my brush portray thy spirit's grace;
For that which makes thy truest beauty be,
Hath no dimension, time, nor earthly space.
So though my art may show thy face so fair,
Thy soul's deep truth, no canvas can declare.

###229###
The little love-god, lying once asleep,
Laid by his torch and arrows in the wood;
A maid of Dian's train, the while to steep
Her innocent heart, for beauty understood,
Came tripping by; but in her heart a fire,
Lit by his brand, did make her straight to burn;
She took the torch, to quench the fierce desire,
And cast it in a cold and crystal urn.
But from the heat, the water came to boil,
And ever since, it warms the bathing love;
So that the sweet, from out the bitter toil,
Can make a heart to higher pleasures move.
Thus Love's hot fire, by chance to water thrown,
Doth still in women's bosoms make his own.

###230###
Let those who are in favour with their stars
Of public honour and proud titles boast,
Whilst I, whom fortune of such triumph bars,
Unlooked for joy in that I honour most.
Great princes' favourites their fair leaves spread
But as the marigold at the sun's eye,
And in themselves their pride lies buried,
For at a frown they in their glory die.
The painful warrior famousèd for fight,
After a thousand victories once foiled,
Is from the book of honour razed quite,
And all the rest forgot for which he toiled:
Then happy I, that love and am beloved,
Where I may not remove nor be removed.

###231###
When forty winters shall besiege thy brow,
And dig deep trenches in thy beauty's field,
Thy youth's proud livery, so gazed on now,
Will be a tattered weed, of small worth held:
Then being asked where all thy beauty lies,
Where all the treasure of thy lusty days,
To say within thine own deep-sunken eyes,
Were an all-eating shame, and thriftless praise.
How much more praise deserved thy beauty's use,
If thou couldst answer "This my fair child of mine
Shall sum my count, and make my old excuse,"
Proving his beauty by succession thine!
This were to be new made when thou art old,
And see thy blood warm when thou feel'st it cold.

###232###
From fairest creatures we desire increase,
That thereby beauty's rose might never die,
But as the riper should by time decease,
His tender heir might bear his memory:
But thou, contracted to thine own bright eyes,
Feed'st thy light's flame with self-substantial fuel,
Making a famine where abundance lies,
Thyself thy foe, to thy sweet self too cruel.
Thou that art now the world's fresh ornament
And only herald to the gaudy spring,
Within thine own bud buriest thy content
And, tender churl, makest waste in niggarding.
Pity the world, or else this glutton be,
To eat the world's due, by the grave and thee.

###233###
No more be grieved at that which thou hast done:
Roses have thorns, and silver fountains mud;
Clouds and eclipses stain both moon and sun,
And loathsome canker lives in sweetest bud.
All men make faults, and even I in this,
Authorizing thy trespass with compare,
Myself corrupting, salving thy amiss,
Excusing thy foul deeds with words more fair.
For to thy sensual fault I bring in sense—
Thy beauty's form, and nature's charming grace,
As I vouchsafe to thee, in my defence,
My pure and constant love, no time can chase.
But say that now, with reason for my guide,
I will not let my passion be denied.

###234###
My love is strengthened, though more weak in show,
Than that which love is by the eye deemed true;
For I, before thee, can no longer grow,
Whilst thou by time dost every day renew.
For my behalf, to thy most fair effect,
With what thy eyes have made, I must content;
My love, though it seem aged, doth not defect,
But like a spirit, waits a new event.
The world hath seen my love's fair countenance,
And heard the praises that my verse hath brought;
But time, that makes the best of things advance,
Hath nothing new, save what from thee is caught.
So thou art all my theme, and thou art all
My verse doth give, and what my thoughts enthrall.

###235###
When in the chronicle of wasted time
I see descriptions of the fairest wights,
And beauty making beautiful old rhyme
In praise of ladies dead and lovely knights,
Then, in the blazon of sweet beauty's best,
Of hand, of foot, of lip, of eye, of brow,
I see their antique pen would have expressed
Even such a beauty as thou master'st now.
So all their praises are but prophecies
Of this our time, all you prefiguring;
And, for they looked but with divining eyes,
They had not skill enough your worth to sing:
For we, which now behold these present days,
Have eyes to wonder, but lack tongues to praise.

###236###
Against my love shall be as I am now,
With wan, with withered, and with age outworn,
When thy sweet youth and freshest grace shall grow,
And all thy beauties shall be new up-born:
When thou shalt be as fresh as morning rose,
And I as faded as the flower of night,
Then love to thee shall newer vows disclose,
And tell thee how I loved thee, full of light.
For though my body shall decay to dust,
My spirit, ever young, shall dwell in thee;
And by my verse, preserved from time's foul rust,
Thy beauty and my passion shall agree.
So live thou ever, in my lines made strong,
While all the world shall read and praise thy song.

###237###
How like a winter hath my absence been
From thee, the pleasure of the fleeting year!
What freezings have I felt, what dark days seen!
What old December's bareness every where!
And yet this time removed was summer's time;
The teeming autumn, big with rich increase,
Bearing the wanton burden of the prime,
Like widowed wombs after their lords' decease:
Yet these had not been winter's storms, my dear,
Had thy sweet presence warmed my desolate state.
The birds sang not, the leaves did not appear,
But every thing did mourn thy long wait.
For either buds, or fruits, or happy shows,
They all did die, that were not in thy close.

###238###
When I consider every thing that grows
Holds in perfection but a little moment,
That this huge stage presenteth nought but shows
Whereon the stars in secret influence comment;
When I perceive that men as plants increase,
Cheered and checked even by the self-same sky,
Then proud-pied April dressed in all his trim
Hath put a spirit of youth in every thing.
And youth and folly doth thy brow adorn,
So that thy beauty shines in all its might;
But knowing this, I sorrow and I mourn,
That such rare grace must yield to darkling night.
Then though all things to Time must make their end,
My love for thee shall never time transcend.

###239###
Poor soul, the centre of my sinful earth,
These rebel powers that thee array, why dost thou pine
Within and suffer thy fair house's dearth,
Buying a fading outward with so dear a clime?
What is the cost of such a mortal grace,
That thou so fondly lavishest thy store?
Shall worms, inheritors of this excess,
Eat up thy charge? Is this thy body's lore?
Then, soul, live thou upon thy servant's loss,
And let that pine to aggravate thy store;
Buy terms divine in selling hours of dross,
Within be fed, without be rich no more:
So shalt thou feed on Death, that feeds on men,
And Death once dead, there's no more dying then.

###240###
Let me not to the marriage of true minds
Admit impediments. Love is not love
Which alters when it alteration finds,
Or bends with the remover to remove.
O no! it is an ever-fixèd mark
That looks on tempests and is never shaken;
It is the star to every wandering bark,
Whose worth's unknown, although his height be taken.
Love's not Time's fool, though rosy lips and cheeks
Within his bending sickle's compass come;
Love alters not with his brief hours and weeks,
But bears it out even to the edge of doom.
If this be error and upon me proved,
I never writ, nor no man ever loved.

###241###
When summer's glory fades from verdant day,
And chilling winds do whisper in the tree,
So doth thy youthful beauty pass away,
Which time's swift hand shall take from thee and me.
No artifice can stay its cruel hold,
Nor paint anew the fading summer's light,
For wrinkles come when stories have been told,
And dim the eyes that once did shine so bright.
Yet in these lines thy memory shall be found,
Though earthly beauty shall be seen no more,
When dust returns to mingle with the ground,
My verse shall praise what time could not lay o'er.
So long as men can breathe or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###242###
Shall I compare thee to a summer's day?
Thou art more lovely and more temperate:
Rough winds do shake the darling buds of May,
And summer's lease hath all too short a date.
Sometime too hot the eye of heaven shines,
And often is his gold complexion dimm'd;
And every fair from fair sometime declines,
By chance, or nature's changing course, untrimm'd;
But thy eternal summer shall not fade,
Nor lose possession of that fair thou ow'st;
Nor shall Death brag thou wander'st in his shade,
When in eternal lines to time thou grow'st:
So long as men can breathe or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###243###
When forty winters shall besiege thy brow,
And dig deep trenches in thy beauty's field,
Thy youth's proud livery, so gazed on now,
Will be a tatter'd weed, of small worth held.
Then being ask'd where all thy beauty lies,
Where all the treasure of thy lusty days,
To say, within thine own deep-sunken eyes,
Were an all-eating shame and thriftless praise.
How much more praise deserved thy beauty's use,
If thou couldst answer 'This fair child of mine
Shall sum my count and make my old excuse,'
Proving his beauty by succession thine!
This were to be new made when thou art old,
And see thy blood warm when thou feel'st it cold.

###244###
From fairest creatures we desire increase,
That thereby beauty's rose might never die,
But as the riper should by time decrease,
His tender heir might bear his memory:
But thou, contracted to thine own bright eyes,
Feed'st thy light's flame with self-substantial fuel,
Making a famine where abundance lies,
Thyself thy foe, to thy sweet self too cruel.
Thou that art now the world's fresh ornament
And only herald to the gaudy spring,
Within thine own bud buriest thy content
And, tender churl, mak'st waste in niggarding.
Pity the world, or else this glutton be,
To eat the world's due, by the grave and thee.

###245###
My mistress' eyes are nothing like the sun;
Coral is far more red than her lips' red;
If snow be white, why then her breasts are dun;
If hairs be wires, black wires grow on her head.
I have seen roses damask'd, red and white,
But no such roses see I in her cheeks;
And in some perfumes is there more delight
Than in the breath that from my mistress reeks.
I love to hear her speak, yet well I know
That music hath a far more pleasing sound;
I grant I never saw a goddess go;
My mistress, when she walks, treads on the ground:
And yet, by heaven, I think my love as rare
As any she belied with false compare.

###246###
When in disgrace with fortune and men's eyes,
I all alone beweep my outcast state,
And trouble deaf heaven with my bootless cries,
And look upon myself and curse my fate,
Wishing me like to one more rich in hope,
Featured like him, with friends possess'd,
Desiring this man's art and that man's scope,
With what I most enjoy contented least;
Yet in these thoughts myself almost despising,
Haply I think on thee, and then my state,
Like to the lark at break of day arising
From sullen earth, sings hymns at heaven's gate;
For thy sweet love remember'd such wealth brings
That then I scorn to change my state with kings.

###247###
Let those who are in favour with their stars
Of public honour and proud titles boast,
Whilst I, whom fortune of such triumph bars,
Unlook'd for joy in that I honour most.
Great princes' favourites their fair leaves spread
But as the marigold at the sun's eye,
And in themselves their pride lies buried,
For at a frown they in their glory die.
The painful warrior famoused for fight,
After a thousand victories once foil'd,
Is from the book of honour razed quite,
And all his former glory lost and spoil'd:
Then happy I, that love and am beloved,
Where I may not be changed, nor overcome.

###248###
Not marble, nor the gilded monuments
Of princes, shall outlive this powerful rhyme;
But you shall shine more bright in these contents
Than unswept stone, besmear'd with sluttish time.
When wasteful war shall statues overturn,
And broils root out the work of masonry,
Nor Mars his sword nor war's quick fire shall burn
The living record of your memory.
'Gainst death and all-oblivious enmity
Shall you pace forth; your praise shall still find room
Even in the eyes of all posterity
That wear this world out to the ending doom.
So, till the judgement that yourself arise,
You live in this, and dwell in lovers' eyes.

###249###
When to the sessions of sweet silent thought
I summon up remembrance of things past,
I sigh the lack of many a thing I sought,
And with old woes new wail my dear time's waste:
Then can I drown an eye, unused to flow,
For precious friends hid in death's dateless night,
And weep afresh love's long since cancell'd woe,
And moan the expense of many a vanish'd sight:
Then can I grieve at grievances foregone,
And heavily from woe to woe tell o'er
The sad account of fore-bemoaned moan,
Which I new pay as if not paid before.
But if the while I think on thee, dear friend,
All losses are restored and sorrows end.

###250###
Full many a glorious morning have I seen
Flatter the mountain-tops with sovereign eye,
Kissing with golden face the meadows green,
Gilding pale streams with heavenly alchemy;
Anon permit the basest clouds to ride
With ugly rack on his celestial face,
And from the forlorn world his visage hide,
Stealing unseen to west with this disgrace:
Even so my sun one early morn did shine
With all triumphant splendour in my brow;
But out, alack, he was but one hour mine,
The region cloud hath mask'd him from me now.
Yet him for this my love no whit disdaineth;
Suns of the world may stain when heaven's sun staineth.

###251###
My love is as a fever, longing still
For that which longer nurseth the disease;
Feeding on that which doth preserve the ill,
The uncertain sickly appetite to please.
My reason, the physician to my love,
Angry that his prescriptions are not kept,
Hath left me, and I desperate now approve
Desire is health's decay, which my love hath wept.
Past cure I am, now reason is past care,
And frantic-mad with evermore unrest;
My thoughts and my discourse as madmen's are,
At random from the truth vainly express'd;
For I have sworn thee fair, and thought thee bright,
Who art as black as hell, as dark as night.

###252###
Like as the waves make towards the pebbled shore,
So do our minutes hasten to their end;
Each changing place with that which goes before,
In sequent toil all forwards do contend.
Nativity, once in the main of light,
Crawls to maturity, wherewith being crown'd,
Crooked eclipses 'gainst his glory fight,
And Time that gave doth now his gift confound.
Time doth transfix the flourish set on youth,
And delves the parallels in beauty's brow,
Feeds on the rarities of nature's truth,
And nothing stands but for his scythe to mow:
And yet to times in hope my verse shall stand,
Praising thy worth, despite his cruel hand.

###253###
When I do count the clock that tells the time,
And see the brave day sunk in hideous night;
When I behold the violet past prime,
And sable curls all silver'd o'er with white;
When lofty trees I see barren of leaves
Which erst from heat did canopy the herd,
And summer's green all girded up in sheaves
Borne on the bier with white and bristly beard,
Then of thy beauty do I question make,
That thou among the wastes of time must go,
Since sweets and beauties do themselves forsake
And die as fast as they see others grow;
And nothing gainst Time's scythe can make defence,
Save breed, to brave him when he takes thee hence.

###254###
Poor soul, the centre of my sinful earth,
These rebel powers that thee array do fight,
Why dost thou pine within and suffer dearth,
Painting thy outward walls so costly bright?
Why so large cost, having so short a lease,
Dost thou upon thy fading mansion spend?
Shall worms, inheritors of this excess,
Eat up thy charge? Is this thy body's end?
Then, soul, live thou upon thy servant's loss,
And let that pine to aggravate thy store;
Buy terms divine in selling hours of dross;
Within be fed, without be rich no more:
So shalt thou feed on Death, that feeds on men,
And Death once dead, there's no more dying then.

###255###
My love is strengthen'd, though more weak in seeming;
I love not less, though less the show appear:
That love is merchandized whose rich esteeming
The owner's tongue doth publish everywhere.
Our love was new, and then but in the spring,
When I was wont to greet it with my lays;
As Philomel in summer's front doth sing,
And stops her pipe in growth of riper days:
Not that the summer is less pleasant now
Than when her mournful hymns did hush the night,
But that wild music burthens every bough,
And sweets grown common lose their dear delight.
Therefore, like her, I sometimes hold my tongue,
Because I would not dull you with my song.

###256###
No more be grieved at that which thou hast done:
Roses have thorns, and silver fountains mud;
Clouds and eclipses stain both moon and sun,
And loathsome canker lives in sweetest bud.
All men make faults, and even I in this,
Authorizing thy trespass with compare,
Myself corrupting, salving thy amiss,
Excusing thy sins more than thy sins are;
For to thy sensual fault I bring in sense,
Thy adverse party is thy advocate,
And 'gainst myself a lawful plea commence:
Such civil war is in my love and hate,
That I an accessary needs must be
To that sweet thief which sourly robs from me.

###257###
When in the chronicle of wasted time
I see descriptions of the fairest wights,
And beauty making beautiful old rhyme
In praise of ladies dead and lovely knights,
Then, in the blazon of sweet beauty's best,
Of hand, of foot, of lip, of eye, of brow,
I see their antique pen would have express'd
Even such a beauty as you master now.
So all their praises are but prophecies
Of this our time, all you prefiguring;
And, for they look'd but with divining eyes,
They had not skill enough your worth to sing:
For we, which now behold these present days,
Have eyes to wonder, but lack tongues to praise.
