A treasury of wisdom, rich and old.
For though the body crumbles into dust,
And fading breath doth leave its earthly cell,
The spirit's light, preserved by pen and trust,
Through ages dark, its wondrous tale shall tell.
So let my verse, though humble, play its part,
To keep thy memory deep within the heart.

###386###
Upon thy brow, no wrinkle yet appears,
Nor time's cruel hand hath stolen any grace,
Thy youthful eye doth banish all my fears,
And in thy smile, I see a tranquil place.
Thy cheek, a rose where blushing beauty dwells,
Thy voice, a music sweeter than the spring,
More captivating than a siren's spells,
The solace that my troubled heart doth bring.
Let cynics scoff at beauty's swift decay,
And preachers rail 'gainst earthly, vain delight,
I find in thee a perfect, sunlit day,
That casts a radiance on the darkest night.
Such heavenly form, a wonder to behold,
A treasure richer far than gems or gold.

###387###
Why do mine eyes, once bright with cheerful hue,
Now shed such bitter tears without control?
What unseen sorrow doth my peace subdue,
And cast a shadow on my inmost soul?
Perhaps the past, with spectres grim and deep,
Returns to haunt the quiet of my rest,
Or future fears, their silent vigil keep,
And press with leaden weight upon my breast.
Oh, would that I could banish every grief,
And bid despair to leave my weary sight,
To find in slumber some profound relief,
And wake refreshed to dawn's returning light.
But till that day, my spirit must contend,
With woes whose bitter pangs do never end.

###388###
When thou art far, a dreary silence reigns,
And joy itself seems banished from my door,
My heart doth echo with a lover's pains,
And yearns to see thy cherished face once more.
The sun shines bright, the birds sing sweet and clear,
But all these pleasures seem a hollow show,
For absence chills the spirit with its fear,
And doth a constant, silent sorrow sow.
I walk alone, where once we used to stray,
And every spot recalls thy gentle tread,
A thousand memories light the lonely way,
And whisper softly of the words we said.
Return, my love, and banish all my strife,
For thou alone canst bring my heart to life.

###389###
Behold the lily, in its purest white,
That doth with grace adorn the verdant field,
Or evening stars, that gem the velvet night,
To whom all other earthly glories yield.
The lark, with song, doth greet the rising sun,
The gentle stream doth murmur on its way,
Each wondrous marvel, 'neath the heaven's run,
Doth praise its Maker with each passing day.
Yet none of these, though beautiful and grand,
Can match the grace that in thy form I find,
A perfect master-piece from nature's hand,
Designed to charm the eye and bless the mind.
In thee, all earthly beauties seem to meet,
A vision perfect, wondrous, and complete.

###390###
The fairest face may hide a false design,
And polished words conceal a wicked heart,
A poisoned chalice often gleams divine,
And with sweet show doth play a treacherous part.
For outward grace doth often lead astray,
And tempt the eye with beauty's hollow guise,
While truth itself may choose a plainer way,
And shun the flattery that beguiles the wise.
Let not thy judgment be by show misled,
Nor trust the surface of a painted scene,
For often virtue's path is humbly spread,
And honesty doth wear a sober mien.
Seek not the glitter, which doth soon decay,
But steadfast worth, that lasts beyond a day.

###391###
From fairest creatures we desire increase,
That thereby beauty's rose might never die,
But as the riper should by time decrease,
His tender heir might bear his memory.
But thou, contracted to thine own bright eyes,
Feed'st thy light's flame with self-substantial fuel,
Making a famine where abundance lies,
Thyself thy foe, to thy sweet self too cruel.
Thou art thy mother's glass, and she in thee
Calls back the lovely April of her prime;
So see thyself, when old, if thou shouldst be
Unlook'd for joy, to pass the latter time.
Pity the world, or else this glutton be,
To eat the world's due, by the grave and thee.

###392###
When I behold the swift decay of things,
And how the strongest walls must turn to dust,
The proudest towers that aspiration brings,
Beneath time's slow and conquering rust;
When verdant spring gives way to summer's heat,
And summer's glory fades to autumn's chill,
And wintry blasts the year's last hopes defeat,
I ponder on the fate that waits us still.
The fleeting breath, the body's fragile form,
The certain end that every mortal knows,
How transient life, enduring but a storm,
Before to quiet earth its spirit goes.
This thought alone doth chill my very bone,
That all we cherish, soon will be outgrown.

###393###
Let envious tongues their venom freely pour,
And whisper falsehoods in the listening ear,
Let slander's breath assail my love's pure core,
And strive to plant the seeds of doubt and fear.
They know not of the bond that ties our souls,
A sacred knot that cannot be undone,
Nor of the strength which every test controls,
A steadfast love beneath the burning sun.
For what is rumour but an idle breeze,
That stirs the leaves, then swiftly dies away?
It cannot shake the deep-rooted trees,
Nor dim the light of truth's eternal ray.
So let them speak; their malice I defy,
My constant heart shall never know a lie.

###394###
My verse is rude, my muse doth lack the fire
To praise thy worth as worthily it may,
My humble pen doth struggle to aspire,
To cast a glory on thy perfect day.
For when I strive to frame some lofty line,
And bid my words thy excellence express,
They falter, weak, and seem of small design,
And fail to capture half thy loveliness.
Perhaps 'tis best that silence guards thy fame,
Lest clumsy praise should do thy beauty wrong,
And simple truth should whisper forth thy name,
More eloquent than any polished song.
Yet for thy sake, my spirit cannot rest,
But pours its all, though poor, at thy behest.

###395###
Not marble, nor the gilded monuments
Of princes, shall outlive this powerful rhyme;
But you shall shine more bright in these contents
Than unswept stone, besmear'd with sluttish time.
When wasteful war shall statues overturn,
And broils root out the work of masonry,
Nor Mars his sword nor war's quick fire shall burn
The living record of your memory.
'Gainst death and all-oblivious enmity
Shall you pace forth; your praise shall still find room
Even in the eyes of all posterity
That wear this world out to the ending doom.
So, till the judgment that yourself arise,
You live in this, and dwell in lovers' eyes.

###396###
When sorrows strike, and fortune proves unkind,
And heavy burdens press upon my soul,
To thee, my friend, my weary steps I find,
And in thy presence, feel my spirit whole.
For thou dost share my griefs with gentle grace,
And lend an ear to every whispered fear,
A comfort shining from thy honest face,
That doth dispel each dark and bitter tear.
No flatterer art thou, with specious art,
But true as steel, a steadfast, loyal guide,
Who holds a cherished place within my heart,
Where trust and truth forevermore abide.
Such friendship rare, a gift from heaven above,
A bond unbreakable, a constant love.

###397###
My restless thoughts, like birds with weary wing,
Do seek thy presence through the fading light,
And find no solace that the world can bring,
When thou art absent from my longing sight.
Each moment passed, an age of bitter pain,
Each silent hour, a burden to endure,
My soul doth thirst, like fields for gentle rain,
And yearns for joys that only thou canst cure.
The very air seems heavy with despair,
The colours dim, the music cold and strange,
For all delight is fled beyond compare,
When cruel distance doth our lives estrange.
Come quickly back, and end this mournful plea,
Restore my heart, and set my spirit free.

###398###
Let others boast of beauty, riches, might,
And seek their praise in transient, earthly show,
I value truth, which shines with purest light,
And doth a lasting, inner peace bestow.
For outward glitter often doth deceive,
And painted masks conceal a shallow mind,
While honest hearts have no need to conceive
A subtle web of falsehoods intertwined.
The noble spirit, clad in simple grace,
Doth greater worth than any king command,
And leaves a legacy no time can chase,
A beacon shining brightly through the land.
So let my life reflect this sacred rule,
To be sincerely true, and no man's fool.

###399###
My gentle muse, from whence thy power flows,
To stir my heart and bid my spirit sing?
A subtle magic that my being knows,
A silent voice that doth its comfort bring.
When heavy clouds obscure the mind's clear sight,
And inspiration hides its sacred flame,
Thou dost return, a vision pure and bright,
And softly whisper forth thy cherished name.
The words unbidden rise, in rhythmic flow,
The thoughts take wing, like doves into the air,
A tapestry of beauty starts to grow,
Dispelling shadows, banishing despair.
So bless me still, with thy celestial art,
And let thy music dwell within my heart.

###400###
Though cruel time may plough furrows in thy face,
And youth's bright summer yield to winter's cold,
Though fleeting beauty find no lasting place,
And all that's cherished slowly turns to old;
My steadfast love shall mock at such decay,
And hold thee precious, though thy bloom be past,
For in thy spirit, dwells a brighter day,
A noble essence, meant for aye to last.
Let worldly eyes perceive a fading grace,
And turn their gaze to novel, fresher forms,
My loyal heart shall find in thy embrace,
A refuge safe from life's tempestuous storms.
For what I love, is not a transient show,
But depth of soul, where endless virtues grow.

###401###
When I do muse on beauty's fleeting show,
And see the spring adorn with blossoms gay,
How summer's pride doth to the autumn go,
And all fair things must fade and pass away.
Then think I on thy grace, which now doth bloom,
As fresh and young as morning's gentle light,
How soon 'twill yield unto the common doom,
And darkness gather round thy day so bright.
Unless some art preserve thy vibrant hue,
Or living lines engrave thy sweet renown,
To hold thy essence, ever fresh and new,
When time's rude hand hath cast all beauty down.
So live in verse, where envious age defies,
And shine eternal in admiring eyes.

###402###
Shall I compare thee to a summer's day?
Thou art more lovely and more temperate:
Rough winds do shake the darling buds of May,
And summer's lease hath all too short a date.
Sometime too hot the eye of heaven shines,
And often is his gold complexion dimmed;
And every fair from fair sometime declines,
By chance, or nature's changing course, untrimmed.
But thy eternal summer shall not fade,
Nor lose possession of that fair thou ow'st,
Nor shall death brag thou wander'st in his shade,
When in eternal lines to time thou grow'st.
So long as men can breathe, or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###403###
Lo, in the heaven a fiery chariot speeds,
Bearing the sun from slumber's drowsy hold,
And gilding eastern clouds with golden beads,
A wondrous sight for mortal to behold.
Yet oft that bright orb, 'ere his course be run,
Is veiled by clouds or sudden tempest's frown,
And sets at eve, his daily labour done,
Leaving the world to darkness, still and brown.
So too thy beauty, though it shine so fair,
May face some storm, or shadowed be by woe,
And fleeting joys dissolve into thin air,
As swift as melted flakes of winter snow.
But truth and virtue hold a lasting sway,
And banish sorrow from thy brighter day.

###404###
When I behold the ocean's vast expanse,
Whose restless waves forever seek the shore,
Or mark the moon's serene and silent dance,
That pulls the tides and rules the watery roar;
Then think I on the fleeting state of man,
Whose brief existence passes like a dream,
A brittle vessel, built on nature's plan,
Borne on life's ever-swift and changing stream.
For what is strength, or honour, or great fame,
Against the winds of time's remorseless flight?
They are but whispers, an ephemeral name,
Extinguished swiftly in the coming night.
Yet love, alone, defies the common lot,
And lives enduring, though all else be rot.

###405###
My love is like a melody, soft and deep,
Whose sweet accords do soothe my troubled breast,
A gentle tune, that lulls my soul to sleep,
And grants my weary spirit peace and rest.
No jarring notes disturb this harmony,
No bitter discords mar its pure design,
But every chord rings true and full for thee,
A sacred music, beautifully divine.
Though worldly cares may fret and life may strive,
And fortune's fickle wheel may turn and turn,
Within this haven, doth my heart revive,
And lessons of enduring passion learn.
So let this song of love forever play,
And guide my footsteps through each passing day.

###406###
The gentle comfort of thy loving eye,
Doth chase away the shadows of my fear,
And when I feel that sorrow lingers nigh,
Thy steadfast gaze doth bid my spirit cheer.
Like morning dew upon a drooping flower,
Thy sweet affection lifts my heavy soul,
And lends me strength to face each trying hour,
Making my broken, wounded spirit whole.
What solace then, could greater fortune bring,
Than to possess a treasure such as thine?
A constant spring, from which new hopes do spring,
A sacred bond, eternally divine.
So let my heart, enslaved by thy dear sight,
Forever dwell within thy loving light.

###407###
My verse, too plain, doth want a richer dress,
To praise thy worth, which doth my skill exceed,
For words fall short, however I express,
The boundless grace and truth thou dost indeed.
My halting lines cannot thy beauty show,
Nor paint thy virtues with a worthy hand,
But as a faint reflection, pale and low,
They strive to tell what few can understand.
Perchance hereafter, when my dust is laid,
And bolder bards shall seize the tuneful lyre,
Some richer genius, mightier arrayed,
Shall set thy praises to a nobler fire.
Till then, accept these labours, poor and slight,
That fain would capture thee in deathless light.

###408###
When nature's cunning hand hath wrought with care,
A form so fair, that marvel fills all eyes,
And graced with beauty, precious and so rare,
A wondrous work beneath the boundless skies.
Then envy's tongue doth whisper venomous lies,
And malice aims its dart with bitter sting,
To dim the lustre that within thee lies,
And drag thy goodness down on broken wing.
But truth, though oft obscured by clouds of spite,
Shall pierce the gloom and make its presence known,
And justice, armed with pure and noble might,
Shall crush the ill that envy's seeds have sown.
So let them rail, whose hearts are black with dread,
Thy virtue shines when all their lies are dead.

###409###
That I do love thee, let no man deny,
For in my heart thou holdest sovereign sway,
And every thought that from my soul doth fly,
Doth bear thy name unto the coming day.
Though other beauties may their charms display,
And fleeting glances tempt my wandering gaze,
My faithful spirit never strays away,
But keeps its fealty through all life's maze.
For constancy is rooted deep within,
A sturdy oak, that storms cannot defeat,
And loyalty, a virtue free from sin,
Makes every bitter hardship truly sweet.
So trust my truth, as sun doth follow moon,
My love for thee shall never wane too soon.

###410###
The fleeting hours, with silent, winged speed,
Do steal away the glory of the morn,
And life's brief lease, a fragile, tender weed,
Is plucked by time ere harvest can be born.
When I reflect upon this hasty flight,
And see how youth doth wither to decay,
How beauty fades and loses all its light,
And strength gives way to weakness, day by day.
Then do I strive to seize each moment fast,
And fill its measure with a worthy deed,
Lest life's rich promise be too quickly past,
And barren plains be found where blossoms bleed.
For in this brief sojourn, 'tis wise and true,
To pluck the bloom, ere winter's icy hue.

###411###
When in the sessions of sweet silent thought,
I summon up remembrance of things past,
I sigh for many a thing I sought and naught,
And with old woes new wail my dear time's waste.
Then can I drown an eye, unused to flow,
For precious friends hid in death's dateless night,
And weep afresh love's long since cancelled woe,
And moan the expense of many a vanished sight.
Then can I grieve at grievances foregone,
And heavily from woe to woe tell o'er
The sad account of fore-bemoanèd moan,
Which I new pay as if not paid before.
But if the while I think on thee, dear friend,
All losses are restored, and sorrows end.

###412###
My muse is dumb, when I should make thee hear
The silent praises of my heart's true lay,
For fear of faulty lines, that might appear
To mar thy worth with words of common clay.
I hold my tongue, lest I should speak amiss,
And by my weakness, dim thy shining grace,
Preferring silence to a tasteless kiss,
That would betray the beauty of thy face.
Yet in this hush, my spirit doth proclaim
A deeper reverence than verse can find,
A burning adoration, void of shame,
That leaves all earthly eloquence behind.
So judge me not for what I fail to tell,
But by the love that in my bosom dwell.

###413###
If ever soul on earth knew perfect woe,
Or heart did ache with sorrow's sharpest dart,
Then surely I, whose grief doth overflow,
Have felt the pangs that tear a loving heart.
For cruel absence, with its chilling breath,
Hath snatched thee from my fond and eager sight,
And left my days to mourn a living death,
And clothed my spirit in eternal night.
No solace can my weary mind appease,
No fleeting joy can ease this heavy pain,
But thoughts of thee, that drift upon the breeze,
Do bring thy cherished image back again.
So I do wait, with patience calm and deep,
Till fate shall grant the vows that true loves keep.

###414###
The fickle world, with all its vain delight,
Doth tempt the soul from paths of steadfast truth,
And draws the unwary into endless night,
Consuming passion, joy, and tender youth.
How many fall before its painted shows,
Its hollow promises and gilded lures,
And reap the bitter fruit of thorny woes,
While virtue's gentle guiding light obscures.
But let not folly lead thy steps astray,
Nor seek renown in riches or in power,
For these shall vanish with the passing day,
As fading blossoms of a summer hour.
Embrace the good, and hold to grace divine,
For lasting peace and lasting joys are thine.

###415###
When that I look upon the starry sky,
And mark the planets in their measured dance,
Or trace the comets swiftly passing by,
Whose fiery tails gleam with a fleeting glance;
Then think I on the vastness of our fate,
How small we are within the boundless span,
And how our lives, however grand and great,
Are but a moment in creation's plan.
What then is man, that he should dream of sway,
Or seek to conquer time with earthly might?
His little reign doth vanish in a day,
Consumed by darkness of eternal night.
Yet love alone, a beacon ever bright,
Can pierce the gloom and lead us to the light.

###416###
Let others praise the beauty of the rose,
Or sing the lily's pure and spotless grace,
Or marvel how the gentle violet grows,
And lends sweet fragrance to its secret place.
My heart beholds a beauty far more true,
A loveliness that blossoms from within,
A spirit pure, whose goodness shines anew,
And drives away the shadows of all sin.
For outward shows, though pleasing to the eye,
Are but a mask that time can soon deface,
And fleeting charms will vanish and will die,
Leaving no trace of their once fair embrace.
But inward worth, a treasure to behold,
Grows ever brighter, as the days unfold.

###417###
How oft my heart, oppressed with worldly care,
Doth seek a refuge from the strife and noise,
And findeth comfort in the silent air,
Amidst the solace of life's simpler joys.
Not in the halls where gilded pomp resides,
Nor in the chase of honour's fleeting gleam,
But where tranquility serenely glides,
As calm reflections in a peaceful stream.
A quiet walk beneath the ancient trees,
A moment's thought, a book of worthy lore,
The gentle murmur of the evening breeze,
These are the riches I do most adore.
For in such peace, my spirit finds its rest,
And banishes the turmoil from my breast.

###418###
From fairest creatures we desire increase,
That thereby beauty's rose might never die,
But as the riper should by time decrease,
His tender heir might bear his memory.
But thou, contracted to thine own bright eyes,
Feed'st thy light's flame with self-substantial fuel,
Making a famine where abundance lies,
Thyself thy foe, to thy sweet self too cruel.
Thou that art now the world's fresh ornament
And only herald to the gaudy spring,
Within thine own bud buriest thy content
And, tender churl, mak'st waste in niggarding.
Pity the world, or else this glutton be,
To eat the world's due, by the grave and thee.

###419###
When that my life to fleeting days is bound,
And slow decay begins its subtle art,
And silver threads among my locks are found,
To mark the passage of a youth departed;
Then shall I gaze upon thy youthful prime,
And in thy vigour find a sweet release,
A blest defiance to the tyrant time,
That steals our moments and denies us peace.
For in thy form, my fading hopes revive,
And in thy spirit, strength anew I find,
A vibrant image, keeping love alive,
A living solace for my weary mind.
So let me cling unto thy radiant light,
And find my dawn within thy endless night.

###420###
My love for thee doth mock at fickle chance,
And scorns the tempests of adversity,
It stands unmoved by fortune's wayward dance,
A steadfast beacon for eternity.
Though winter's grasp may chill the verdant land,
And icy winds may strip the trees of green,
My constant passion holds a firmer hand,
More deeply rooted than all that is seen.
For true affection knows no ebb nor flow,
No sudden change, no fading of its fire,
But like the sun, its steady rays do glow,
And burn with pure and unquenched desire.
So let the world its changing seasons prove,
My heart remains eternally in love.

###421###
When lovely youth doth fade and lose its light,
And silvered age doth dim the shining eye,
Then envious Time, with all his cruel might,
Doth steal the grace whereon our joys rely.
But from the root of beauty's fair increase,
Some tender scion may itself uprear,
To give to future days a sweet release,
And banish thence the shadow of all fear.
So shall thy form, though turned to common dust,
Live in the image of a child so fair,
A pledge against the worm of bitter rust,
That doth the fairest monuments impair.
Then yield thee not to sorrow's chill embrace,
But find thy solace in a younger face.

###422###
My love shall live, though kingdoms fall to dust,
And monuments of brass themselves decay,
For in these lines, a faithful, sacred trust
Shall keep thy memory from Time's foul sway.
When marble tombs are worn by wind and rain,
And gilded statues crumble to the ground,
Thy gentle name shall brightly still remain,
In every verse, eternally renowned.
Nor shall grim Death, with all his cruel sting,
Obscure thy worth from future ages' view,
But in this measured song, thy praise I sing,
To make thy beauty ever fresh and new.
So long as men can breathe, or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###423###
Shall I compare thee to a summer's day?
Thou art more lovely and more temperate:
Rough winds do shake the darling buds of May,
And summer's lease hath all too short a date.
Sometime too hot the eye of heaven shines,
And often is his gold complexion dimmed;
And every fair from fair sometime declines,
By chance, or nature's changing course untrimmed.
But thy eternal summer shall not fade,
Nor lose possession of that fair thou ow'st,
Nor shall Death brag thou wander'st in his shade,
When in eternal lines to time thou grow'st.
So long as men can breathe, or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###424###
When in the chronicle of wasted time
I see descriptions of the fairest wights,
And beauty making beautiful old rhyme
In praise of ladies dead and lovely knights,
Then, in the blazon of sweet beauty's best,
Of hand, of foot, of lip, of eye, of brow,
I see their forms are but a shadowed test
Of that which living in thy face lives now.
Theirs was but beauty's faded copy then,
A prologue to the volume of thy grace;
Thou art the master-mold, the first of men,
A perfect pattern for a future race.
So all their praises are but prophecies,
Of thee, who art now fresh, and fair, and wise.

###425###
My love is as a fever, longing still
For that which longer nurseth the disease,
Feeding on that which doth preserve the ill,
The dangerous philtre that my soul doth please.
My reason, the physician to my love,
Angry that his prescriptions are not kept,
Hath left me, and I desperate now approve
Desire is death, which physic did accept.
Past cure I am, now reason is past care,
And frantic-mad with evermore unrest;
My thoughts and my discourse as madmen's are,
At random from the truth vainly expressed.
For I have sworn thee fair; more perjured eye,
Than to swear white is black, or day is night.

###426###
O, never say that I was false of heart,
Though absence seemed my flame to cool and chill,
But think that self-same heaven was my part,
Where thy bright star doth guide my wand'ring will.
I know the subtle changes of the moon,
And how the seasons shift from spring to fall,
Yet in my breast, thy image holds its noon,
And stands unchanging, answering duty's call.
No tide of fortune, no tempestuous blast,
No envious tongue can ever move my mind,
For love like mine was made to ever last,
And seeks no new, when it the best doth find.
Then doubt not, though my steps afar may roam,
My truest heart still finds with thee its home.

###427###
When I do count the clock that tells the time,
And see the brave day sunk in hideous night;
When I behold the violet past its prime,
And sable curls all silvered o'er with white;
When lofty trees I see barren of leaves,
Which erst from heat did canopy the herd,
And summer's green all girded up in sheaves,
Borne on the bier with white and bristly beard:
Then of thy beauty do I question make,
That thou among the wastes of time must go,
Since sweets and beauties do themselves forsake,
And die as fast as they see others grow.
And nothing 'gainst Time's scythe can make defence,
Save breed, to brave him when he takes thee hence.

###428###
How oft, when thou, my music, music play'st,
Upon that blessed wood whose motion sounds
With thy sweet fingers, when thou gently sway'st
The wiry concord that mine ear confounds,
Do I envy those jacks that nimble leap
To kiss the tender inward of thy hand,
Whilst my poor lips, which should that harvest reap,
At the wood's boldness by thee blushing stand!
To be so tickled, they would change their state
And gesture, just as those proud jacks do so,
Whose fingers' ends, when thou dost touch, elate,
More than my lips, their happiness they show.
Since saucy jacks so happy are in this,
Give them thy fingers, me thy lips to kiss.

###429###
The expense of spirit in a waste of shame
Is lust in action; and till action, lust
Is perjured, murderous, bloody, full of blame,
Savage, extreme, rude, cruel, not to trust;
Enjoy'd no sooner but despised straight;
Past reason hunted; and no sooner had,
Past reason hated, as a swallowed bait,
On purpose laid to make the taker mad:
Mad in pursuit and in possession so;
Had, having, and in quest to have, extreme;
A bliss in proof, and proved, a very woe;
Before, a joy proposed; behind, a dream.
All this the world well knows; yet none knows well
To shun the heaven that leads men to this hell.

###430###
Let me not to the marriage of true minds
Admit impediments. Love is not love
Which alters when it alteration finds,
Or bends with the remover to remove.
O no! it is an ever-fixed mark
That looks on tempests and is never shaken;
It is the star to every wand'ring bark,
Whose worth's unknown, although his height be taken.
Love's not Time's fool, though rosy lips and cheeks
Within his bending sickle's compass come;
Love alters not with his brief hours and weeks,
But bears it out even to the edge of doom.
If this be error and upon me proved,
I never writ, nor no man ever loved.

###431###
When my love swears that she is made of truth,
I do believe her, though I know she lies,
That she might think me some untutored youth,
Unlearned in the world's false subtleties.
Thus vainly thinking that she thinks me young,
Although I know my days are past the best,
Simply I credit her false-speaking tongue:
On both sides thus is simple truth suppressed.
But wherefore says she not she is unjust?
And wherefore say not I that I am old?
Oh, love's best habit is in seeming trust,
And age in love loves not to have years told.
Therefore I lie with her and she with me,
And in our faults by lies we flatter'd be.

###432###
Poor soul, the center of my sinful earth,
These rebel powers that thee array, why dost thou pine,
And faint with toil, and run to seek a dearth,
Eating the crust of bread, but not the wine?
Why dost thou lavish out thy store's cost clear,
Upon thy fading mansion so untrue,
That must within short time be all laid bare,
And lose possession of its outward hue?
Buy terms divine in selling hours of dross;
The canker will not eat thee from the core;
Invest thy stock in spirit, 'gainst Time's cross,
And give thyself the wealth that endures evermore.
So shalt thou feed on Death, that feeds on men,
And Death once dead, there's no more dying then.

###433###
No more be grieved at that which thou hast done:
Roses have thorns, and silver fountains mud;
Clouds and eclipses stain both moon and sun,
And loathsome canker lives in sweetest bud.
All men make faults, and even I in this,
Authorizing thy trespass with compare,
Myself corrupting, salving thy amiss,
Excusing thy sins more than thy sins are.
For to thy sensual fault I bring in sense—
Thy adverse party is thy advocate—
And 'gainst myself a lawful plea commence:
Such civil war is in my love and hate,
That I an accessary needs must be
To that sweet thief which sourly robs from me.

###434###
Full many a glorious morning have I seen
Flatter the mountain tops with sovereign eye,
Kissing with golden face the meadows green,
Gilding pale streams with heavenly alchemy;
Anon permit the basest clouds to ride
With ugly rack on his celestial face,
And from the forlorn world his visage hide,
Stealing unseen to west with this disgrace.
Even so my sun one early morn did shine
With all triumphant splendor on my brow;
But out, alack, he was but one hour mine,
The region cloud hath masked him from me now.
Yet him for this my love no whit disdaineth;
Suns of the world may stain when heaven's sun staineth.

###435###
When that I was and you were in your prime,
When youth and folly made us both so bold,
Ere wrinkle came, or fell diseases' rhyme,
And story's breath was fresher to be told;
Then did we sport beneath a summer's tree,
And count the stars that glimmered in the night,
Believing then that time would ever flee,
And never bring an end to our delight.
But now the shadows lengthen on the grass,
And colder winds begin to stir the air,
The mirrored glass reflects how moments pass,
And shows the traces of consuming care.
Yet in my heart, the olden flame doth burn,
And for thy love, my spirit still doth yearn.

###436###
Mine eye hath played the painter and hath steeled
Thy beauty's form in table of my heart;
My body is the frame wherein 'tis held,
And perspective it is best painter's art.
For through the painter no where canst thou look,
Not at thy living image in my eye,
Yet in my heart, where he hath pencilled took,
His working many a curious thing doth lie.
But still, in him, thy fair appears obscured,
For it the inward print hath made so deep,
That by the outward surface, 'tis secured,
And from all vulgar gaze doth safely keep.
To find the lively colour of thy cheek,
I need no further than my heart to seek.

###437###
To me, fair friend, you never can be old,
For as you were when first your eye I eyed,
Such seems your beauty still. Three winters cold
Have from the forests shook three summers' pride,
Three beauteous springs to yellow autumn turned
In process of the seasons have I seen,
Three April perfumes in three hot Junes burned,
Since first I saw you fresh, which yet are green.
Ah! yet doth beauty, like a dial-hand,
Steal from his figure and no pace perceived;
So your sweet semblance, which I understand,
Hath made no step, as it were still received.
For fear of which, hear this, thou age unbred:
Ere you were born, was beauty's summer dead.

###438###
When in disgrace with fortune and men's eyes,
I all alone beweep my outcast state,
And trouble deaf heaven with my bootless cries,
And look upon myself and curse my fate,
Wishing me like to one more rich in hope,
Featured like him, like him with friends possessed,
Desiring this man's art and that man's scope,
With what I most enjoy contented least;
Yet in these thoughts myself almost despising,
Haply I think on thee, and then my state,
Like to the lark at break of day arising
From sullen earth, sings hymns at heaven's gate;
For thy sweet love remembered such wealth brings
That then I scorn to change my state with kings.

###439###
They that have power to hurt and will do none,
That do not do the thing they most do show,
Who, moving others, are themselves as stone,
Unmoved, cold, and to temptation slow;
They rightly do inherit heaven's graces,
And husband nature's riches from expense;
They are the lords and owners of their faces,
Others but stewards of their excellence.
The summer's flower is to the summer sweet,
Though to itself it only live and die,
But if that flower with base infection meet,
The basest weed outbraves his dignity:
For sweetest things turn sourest by their deeds;
Lilies that fester smell far worse than weeds.

###440###
Not marble, nor the gilded monuments
Of princes, shall outlive this powerful rhyme;
But you shall shine more bright in these contents
Than unswept stone, besmeared with sluttish time.
When wasteful war shall statues overturn,
And broils root out the work of masonry,
Nor Mars his sword nor war's quick fire shall burn
The living record of your memory.
'Gainst death and all-oblivious enmity
Shall you pace forth; your praise shall still find room
Even in the eyes of all posterity
That wear this world out to the ending doom.
So, till the judgment that yourself arise,
You live in this, and dwell in lovers' eyes.

###441###
When rosy morn doth wake the slumbering day,
And youthful beauty paints the cheek so fair,
Doth cruel Time then steal its grace away,
And plant his furrows with a harsh despair?
The vibrant bloom, which now in glory shines,
Shall fade anon, when winter winds do blow,
And swift oblivion all its worth inclines,
To dust and ashes, where no beauties grow.
Yet in these lines, thy precious form shall live,
When monuments of brass are quite decayed,
A lasting memory this verse shall give,
'Gainst Time's fell hand, though all the world be swayed.
So shalt thou shine, in this my lasting rhyme,
Immortalized, beyond the grasp of Time.

###442###
My love is as a star, whose steady light
Doth guide my vessel on the stormy main;
Though tempest rage, and darkness shroud the night,
Her constant beam doth banish every pain.
No shifting gale can move her fixed intent,
Nor fleeting clouds obscure her visage bright;
On faith's firm rock her precious soul is bent,
And in her truth I find my sole delight.
Let envious tongues pronounce her worth to cease,
Let slander's breath defile her spotless name;
My heart holds fast, and finds in her its peace,
Unshaken by the world's malicious blame.
For she is all that virtue can define,
A sacred love, eternally divine.

###443###
Shall I compare thee to a summer's day?
Thou art more lovely and more temperate:
Rough winds do shake the darling buds of May,
And summer's lease hath all too short a date.
Sometime too hot the eye of heaven shines,
And often is his gold complexion dimmed;
And every fair from fair sometime declines,
By chance, or nature's changing course, untrimmed.
But thy eternal summer shall not fade,
Nor lose possession of that fair thou ow'st;
Nor shall Death brag thou wander'st in his shade,
When in eternal lines to time thou grow'st.
So long as men can breathe, or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###444###
When I behold the fading of the rose,
Which flaunts its beauty in the morning sun,
Then droops its head, as twilight softly grows,
Its brief and lovely pilgrimage now done;
I think upon the swiftly passing hours,
How youth and prime do vanish from our sight,
And how the strength of unassailed powers
Is swallowed by oblivion's dark night.
Then do I praise thy beauty, rare and true,
Which holds its sway against time's cruel decree,
And wish these verses might prolong for you
The grace that mortal eyes may cease to see.
So shall thy merit live, beyond compare,
Preserved by art from death's engulfing snare.

###445###
My heavy heart, with sorrow overcast,
Doth seek its solace in thy gentle gaze,
Where troubles vanish, and where shadows cast
No gloom upon my soul in these dark days.
For when I see thy face, so pure and bright,
A sudden joy within my breast doth spring,
Dispelling all the clouds of pain and night,
And to my spirit, blessed comfort bring.
Though worldly cares may vex my weary mind,
And fortunes frown with harsh and cold disdain,
In thy dear presence, true relief I find,
A balm to soothe each agonizing pain.
Thus art thou heaven, by my side to be,
A guiding star for my infirmity.

###446###
The fleeting hours, that whirl the years away,
Do steal the beauty from the youthful brow,
And make the locks, once black, turn silver-grey,
And ancient furrows on the forehead plough.
Where once the rose did bloom in blushing hue,
Now winter's chill hath set its icy hand;
And fading lustre doth the eyes subdue,
As life's brief candle flickers through the land.
But love, true love, doth mock at time's swift flight,
And scoffs at age, and laughs at all decay;
It burns unquenched, a pure and steady light,
And brighter shines, as all things pass away.
So let us cherish, ere our moments cease,
This bond of hearts, that finds eternal peace.

###447###
Full many a thought doth stir within my breast,
When I consider all that life bestows;
From morning's light, to evening's peaceful rest,
And how each moment swiftly comes and goes.
The pride of kings, the wisdom of the sage,
The merchant's wealth, the soldier's martial might,
All fade at last upon time's cruel stage,
Consuming darkness swallowing the light.
Yet in thy grace, a solace I do find,
A steadfast haven from the worldly strife;
A comfort for my troubled, weary mind,
A promise of a more enduring life.
For while this frame decays, and breath doth part,
Thy image dwells eternal in my heart.

###448###
When nature's art doth paint the lily white,
And crimson blush upon the rose impart,
I see the wonders of her glorious might,
But praise thy beauty, which transcends all art.
No flower that blooms, however fair its grace,
Can rival hues that in thy features blend;
No morning star, that lights the ethereal space,
Can with thy radiant countenance contend.
For in thy eyes, a heaven I behold,
Where virtue dwells, and truth doth make its shrine;
A story that no language can unfold,
A love that speaks of something more divine.
So let the world admire its fading show,
While in my heart, thy deathless beauties grow.

###449###
Oft have I mused on Fortune's fickle wheel,
How swiftly it doth lift men to the sky,
Then with a turn, their happiness conceal,
And cast them down to misery, to sigh.
The rich man's hoard, the proud man's brief command,
The scholar's lore, the victor's loud acclaim,
All yield at last to time's relentless hand,
And vanish into an unhonoured name.
But true affection, from the spirit born,
Disdains the blows that outward fortune deals;
It greets each dawn, unwearied and unshorn,
And all its precious truth at last reveals.
Such love have I for thee, beyond all price,
A steadfast balm, 'gainst fate's cold prejudice.

###450###
My verse is poor, unworthy of thy praise,
And cannot paint thy merit as it ought;
It struggles with a thousand humble ways,
To capture beauty, which no skill hath caught.
For when I strive to make thy virtues clear,
My halting words do falter and grow weak,
And all my efforts fill my soul with fear,
Lest my rude lines thy perfect grace bespeak.
Yet know, dear friend, that though my pen may fail,
And lack the power to do thy honour right,
My heart's true feeling shall at last prevail,
And hold thy image ever in its light.
So take this offering, though it be but small,
My love for thee, which doth surpass them all.

###451###
The weary sun, when he hath run his race,
Doth seek his rest within the western wave;
And shadows lengthen, with a solemn pace,
As daylight yields to night, a silent grave.
So passes life, its labours and its dreams,
Each fleeting breath a moment quickly flown;
A transient sparkle, as a river gleams,
Then in the vast unknown, forever gone.
But though the body shall return to dust,
And earthly joys dissolve like morning dew,
The soul's pure essence, built on faith and trust,
Doth seek a realm eternally renewed.
And in that hope, our spirits find release,
A journey toward everlasting peace.

###452###
Let others sing of battles bravely fought,
Or praise the conqueror's triumphant might;
Let bards describe the treasures finely wrought,
Or beauty's charm, that shimmers in the light.
My simple muse doth seek a humbler theme,
To celebrate the truth that I possess;
A love that wakes me from a troubled dream,
And fills my days with joy and happiness.
For in thy smile, I find a richer prize
Than all the jewels in Golconda's store;
A deeper wisdom in thy gentle eyes,
Than sages learn from ancient, dusty lore.
So let them boast of greatness, wealth, and fame,
While I hold dear thy sweet and precious name.

###453###
When sorrow's cloud doth overcast my mind,
And bitter thoughts within my spirit dwell,
No earthly comfort can I hope to find,
To break the bondage of this gloomy spell.
I wander forth, beneath the silent moon,
And seek a solace in the midnight air;
But even stars withhold their kindly boon,
And mock my heart, consumed by deep despair.
Then memory recalls thy gentle face,
Thy soothing words, that banish every fear;
And suddenly, a warmth doth fill this place,
And all the shadows swiftly disappear.
Thus art thou heaven, sent from realms above,
To light my darkness with thy tender love.

###454###
The world's a stage, where fickle actors play
Their varied parts, then vanish from our view;
A fleeting show, that lasts but for a day,
Where falsehood oft doth mask the honest hue.
The flattering tongue, the smile that hides deceit,
The gilded promise, broken ere it's made,
Such empty shadows do our senses cheat,
And leave the trusting heart forlorn and swayed.
But in thy spirit, pure and undefiled,
No guile resides, nor any subtle art;
Thou art as gentle as a new-born child,
With truth engraven deeply in thy heart.
So let the false world play its hollow game,
While I hold fast to thy unblemished name.

###455###
Mine eyes have seen the rising of the sun,
And watched him set, in fiery western skies;
Beheld the race of summer swiftly run,
And winter's breath, that freezes ere it dies.
I've marked the trees, how green their leaves appear,
Then turn to gold, and flutter to the ground;
And heard the birds, when spring doth draw them near,
Then fall to silence, where no songs are found.
All these do preach the lesson of decay,
That beauty's bloom is but a fading dream;
And all that lives, must pass from light away,
As swift as currents in a flowing stream.
Yet in thy form, a timeless grace I see,
My only hope 'gainst frail mortality.

###456###
What bitter pangs do seize my trembling soul,
When I consider Time's relentless sway!
How youth and vigour yield to his control,
And strength and beauty softly melt away.
The vibrant flush that colours childhood's cheek,
The shining eye, that speaks of joy and mirth,
Grow dim and pale, as seasons onward seek,
And claim their tribute from the yielding earth.
Then do I long for some enduring art,
To hold thy grace beyond the common doom;
To seal thy image deep within my heart,
And shield thy beauty from the hungry tomb.
So in these lines, thy gentle light shall shine,
And bid defiance to all things malign.

###457###
If all the world were stripped of every grace,
And beauty's form did vanish from our sight;
If nature hid her wonders from this place,
And darkness reigned, where once there shone a light;
Still in my mind, thy image would reside,
A perfect dream, that time could not erase;
My constant comfort, and my faithful guide,
A hidden heaven, in this lonely space.
For thou hast taught my spirit to discern
A deeper truth, beyond mere outward show;
A love that ages never can unlearn,
A sacred flame, whose light doth ever glow.
So let the world its fading pleasures mourn,
Thy deathless essence shall my heart adorn.

###458###
My thoughts are restless, like a troubled sea,
When absence keeps thy presence from my view;
No peace I find, no solace comes to me,
Until thy gentle image I renew.
Each passing hour doth seem a tedious age,
Each moment stretched beyond its rightful span;
And sorrow's hand doth turn life's joyful page,
To scenes of bleakness for a lonely man.
I count the minutes, measure every day,
And sigh for moments that refuse to haste;
Longing to chase these dreary clouds away,
And know again thy love, I may not waste.
Come swiftly, love, and banish all my pain,
And bring my weary spirit joy again.

###459###
The wisdom of the ancients, grave and deep,
Hath taught mankind of life's uncertain stay;
How fleeting joys their brief communion keep,
Then swiftly fade, like shadows of the day.
The strongest fort, the tower built so high,
At length must yield to time's eroding hand;
The mightiest empire, 'neath the spacious sky,
At last will crumble, and forgotten stand.
But love, true love, a firmer fabric weaves,
Than stone or brass, or glory's transient gleam;
Its root is deep, its fruit the heart receives,
A waking truth, and not a fading dream.
So let all else succumb to slow decay,
Our bond shall mock the passing of the day.

###460###
When in the throes of sadness I do pine,
And heavy burdens weigh upon my soul,
I think of thee, and then my spirit's shrine
Is filled with comfort, making my heart whole.
For thy dear name, a balm to every wound,
Doth charm away the shadows from my sight;
And in thy memory, my peace is found,
A guiding star, throughout the darkest night.
Let others seek their pleasure in the chase
Of worldly honours, or of fleeting gold;
My joy is fixed upon thy gentle grace,
A treasure richer far than can be told.
So blessèd be the hour when first we met,
Thy love's sweet solace, I shall ne'er forget.

###461###
When I do gaze upon thy youthful light,
And mark the fading of each tender grace,
I mourn to think how time with cruelest might,
Shall mar the beauty of thy gentle face.
The roses sweet that bloom in summer's field,
Must soon give way to winter's icy store,
No lasting treasure can their blossoms yield,
But fade and vanish, to be seen no more.
Yet in my verse, thy memory I keep,
Against the ravage of consuming age,
So shall thy virtues from oblivion creep,
And live forever on this lasting page.
Thus love's true essence doth transcend all art,
And finds its dwelling in a constant heart.
###462###
From thine eyes, dear love, a wondrous light,
That doth before my waking spirit stand,
Dispelling shadows of the darkest night,
A guiding star held firmly in thy hand.
No sunbeam could with such pure beauty gleam,
Nor fairer vision ever bless my true
And fondest hope, a sweet and endless dream,
Reflected brightly in my loving view.
What potent magic dost thou cast on me,
That fills each moment with such potent power?
No joy like this could ever bloom for thee,
To light my spirit in its darkest hour.
So in my heart thy image I shall find,
The dearest treasure to my constant mind.
###463###
When Nature calls thee forth to make thy prime,
And bids thee store thy beauty, passing fair,
Why dost thou waste the richness of thy time,
And keep thy glory from a hopeful heir?
For as the rose, when its sweet life doth fade,
Leaves in its bud a promise to replace,
So shouldst thou, lest thy loveliness be made
A fleeting shadow, leaving not a trace.
Let not thy beauty perish with thy breath,
But in a child, let thy fair image live,
Against the conquests of insatiate death,
That to the world a fairer self might give.
So shalt thou conquer time, and make it blind,
And in thy offspring, thy true self shalt find.
###464###
The worldly goods that mortal men do hold,
Are but a lease that hastens to decay;
No lasting solace can their worth unfold,
They vanish swift, as shadows from the day.
What profit hath the man whom riches steal,
When all his golden moments softly fade?
Before grim death, all earthly powers kneel,
No lasting fortress by his wealth is made.
Then why should man his fleeting wealth pursue,
When bitter anguish is its final woe?
Let virtue guide his spirit, firm and true,
For transient glories do but falsely show.
Hold not thy riches with a grip so fast,
For only virtue doth forever last.
###465###
The fleeting beauty of a tender age,
Doth quickly pass, a transient, fading show;
And time, the churl, doth turn each youthful page,
To quench the spark that made thy spirit glow.
No earthly bloom can stand against his might,
Nor golden treasure can his grasp withstand,
But all must yield unto eternal night,
And vanish swift, like dust within the hand.
Yet shall my verse thy dearest image give,
A lasting monument, beyond all breath,
Where through my lines thy loveliness shall live,
Defying ruin, and the sting of death.
So shall thy form still gladden every eye,
And conquer all that seeks to bring thee nigh.
###466###
Though envious tongues with venomous spite may rage,
And slander seek to wound my spirit true,
My steadfast love, though time doth turn each page,
Shall ever hold thee in its constant view.
Let seasons change, and summer's warmth decrease,
And winter's frost o'er nature's stillness creep,
My firm devotion brings my spirit peace,
As silent stars their nightly watches keep.
For I have vowed my faith, and it shall stand
Against all woes that seek to tear apart,
No foreign shore, nor any distant land,
Can break the bonds that link my loyal heart.
No earthly power can divide my soul,
For thou and I art fashioned to be whole.
###467###
Behold the sun, when in its youthful light,
It doth ascend and paint the morn with gold,
And draws all eyes, a marvel to the sight,
A kingly progress, glorious to behold.
But when its journey hath its zenith passed,
And dims the glory of its morning bright,
Its fading beams no longer hold so fast,
As shadows deepen into coming night.
So thou, whose beauty doth now charm each eye,
And drawest homage from each gazing mind,
Shalt feel the stealthy hand of time draw nigh,
And leave thy youthful splendor far behind.
Then wisely use this fleeting prime of thine,
Lest fading moments cause thy light to pine.
###468###
When soulful music fills the air so sweet,
And doth inspire each dull and heavy ear,
What discord then, when lovers do not meet,
And joy is banished by a single tear?
For instruments, when tuned in perfect grace,
Do chime together in a pleasing sound,
But if one string be found in faulty place,
Then all the harmony cannot be found.
So too our lives, if love doth bind them fast,
Should join in concord, free from jealous strife,
And build a union that shall ever last,
A gentle echo through the span of life.
Then let our spirits in sweet measure run,
For in true love, two hearts become as one.
###469###
When thou, dear friend, from my fond sight dost part,
And distance bears thy cherished form afar,
A heavy sorrow settles in my heart,
And dims my spirit, like a setting star.
I watch the clouds that dim the summer view,
And think of thee, throughout the fading light,
My thoughts to thee eternally are true,
And wish thee near, through shadows of the night.
For in thine absence, solace hath been gone,
And heavy sighs my watchful moments keep,
Until thy face returns with morning's dawn,
My weary eyes shall but lament and weep.
Then come, sweet comfort, ere my heart grows cold,
And let our loving story be retold.
###470###
When fleeting moments steal thy youthful bloom,
And hungry time doth hasten thy decay,
What earthly power can reverse thy doom,
Or keep thy beauty from its fading day?
Not costly jewels, nor the proudest name,
Can long endure beyond their transient hour,
But all must yield unto oblivion's flame,
And wither swift, like some frail summer flower.
Yet in these lines, thy gentle virtues live,
A deathless essence, pure and ever new,
Such lasting glory can my passion give,
To tell thy story, constant, strong, and true.
So shalt thou conquer time, and make it blind,
The fairest image for a loving mind.
###471###
Let fickle Fortune shift her painted scene,
And turn her wheel with ever-changing hand,
Let storms arise where skies were once serene,
And scatter blessings o'er a barren land.
My constant spirit shall not feel her sway,
Nor shall her tempests move my firm intent,
For in thy love, I find my guiding day,
A solace sweet, from heaven gently sent.
Though worldly goods may crumble to the dust,
And fading glories vanish from the sight,
In thy true heart, my steadfast soul doth trust,
A beacon shining through the darkest night.
So let the world around me ebb and flow,
My love for thee shall never cease to grow.
###472###
When icicles hang down and winds do blow,
And all the fields are clad in hoary white,
No tender flower doth on the meadow grow,
And nature sleeps through many a cheerless night.
The summer's bloom and autumn's fleeting grace,
Are vanished quite, their colours flown away;
No verdant leaf upon the bough doth trace
The memory of a warmer, brighter day.
Yet in this bleakness, hope doth softly creep,
A promise whispered on the northern breeze,
That 'neath the snow, where seeds in silence sleep,
New life shall rise and banish all disease.
For spring shall come, with sunshine bright and grand,
And bring new beauty to the waiting land.
###473###
As plants do lean upon the sun's warm light,
To draw their sustenance from golden ray,
So doth my spirit, through the darkest night,
Depend upon thy presence, night and day.
When thou art absent, all my joys are flown,
And heavy gloom doth fill my weary mind,
No greater sadness have I ever known,
Nor comfort else can my poor spirit find.
Then let no shadow dim thy radiance clear,
Nor cruel distance hold thee far apart,
But ever be, my comfort, ever near,
To cheer the dwelling of my longing heart.
For without thee, my life doth lose its goal,
Thou art the anchor of my very soul.
###474###
Not from the stars do I my judgment pluck,
Nor can I tell of fortunes good or ill,
I have no skill to read what fate hath stuck
Upon the wheel, to work its wondrous will.
But from thy virtues, which do shine so clear,
I draw my prescience, of a happier state,
That truth and goodness banish every fear,
And overcome the frowns of cruelest fate.
For though thy beauty fade, as all things must,
And time's cold hand doth mar thy youthful face,
Thy constant spirit, free from worldly rust,
Shall hold its glory, in its destined place.
So shalt thou live, when outward charms are past,
For only virtue doth forever last.
###475###
When all the world doth turn from thee away,
And fleeting friendship proves a hollow sound,
My loyal spirit shall forever stay,
Where true devotion ever can be found.
Though envious time may steal thy youthful grace,
And mar the beauty of thy tender face,
My steadfast verse shall hold thy treasured place,
And keep thy memory safe from all disgrace.
For while I live, and breath doth fill my frame,
Thy cherished image in my soul shall dwell,
And every line shall echo forth thy name,
A loving truth, that time can never quell.
So in these rhymes, thy essence I impart,
The deathless treasure of my constant heart.
###476###
The fleeting moments of our mortal day,
Are but a shadow, transient and brief;
They swiftly pass, and vanish quite away,
Like autumn's glory, or a summer's leaf.
Then let us not in idle slumber lie,
Nor waste the riches of our precious youth,
But seize the joys that swiftly pass us by,
And fill our spirits with life's vibrant truth.
For when old age with frosty hand doth creep,
And steals the vigor from our failing frame,
Then shall we find that regrets gather deep,
And naught remains but sorrow and dull shame.
Live then thy life with passion, bold and fast,
For every moment quickly hastens past.
###477###
Those gentle eyes, that shine with inward grace,
Are windows to a soul both pure and deep,
Reflecting kindness from their tender space,
Where tender thoughts in silent vigil keep.
No hidden malice doth within them dwell,
Nor any falsehood cloud their honest gleam,
But crystal truth, a story they do tell,
More fair than waking thought or pleasant dream.
When I behold their calm and quiet light,
My troubled spirit finds its comfort true,
Dispelling shadows of the darkest night,
And making all my weary being new.
So in their depths, my truest self I see,
My dearest love, eternally in thee.
###478###
When I no more thy gentle face can see,
And distance keeps thee from my longing gaze,
A heavy sadness settles over me,
And shadows lengthen in my weary days.
My spirit pines, and doth its comfort mourn,
Like one bereft of sunshine's golden ray,
And waits impatient for thy swift return,
To banish gloom and usher in bright day.
The very stars that twinkle in the night,
Seem dim and cold, without thy cheering gleam,
And all the world, deprived of thy dear light,
Is but a shadow of a vanished dream.
Then come, sweet friend, and banish all my pain,
And make my heart rejoice in thee again.
###479###
Devouring Time, that doth all things consume,
And with thy scythe doth mow down strongest towers,
Thou makest beauty hasten to its tomb,
And stealest fragrance from the sweetest flowers.
Let not thy hand upon my loved one prey,
Nor mar the fairness of that gentle face,
But let her beauty hold its sovereign sway,
And youth abide within its destined place.
Though pyramids may crumble into dust,
And ancient kingdoms vanish from the earth,
My loyal love, in which I ever trust,
Shall bear witness to her lasting worth.
For in my verse, she shall forever live,
The purest joy that love can truly give.
###480###
When first I gaze upon thy radiant face,
Where every virtue shines in beauty clear,
Such perfect form, such universal grace,
Doth banish from my spirit every fear.
Thy eyes, like twin bright stars in clearest sky,
Hold such a wonder, as the heavens send;
No painter's skill could catch their lustre high,
And hail thee as my truest, dearest friend.
What sculptor's skill, or poet's wondrous art,
Could capture half the beauty that is thine?
It moves the spirit, and expands the heart,
A joy immortal, exquisitely divine.
Such perfect form doth my deep passion prove,
And bind my spirit with eternal love.

###481###
When I consider how my days do flee,
And how the spring of youth doth swiftly pass,
I muse upon thy perfect grace to see,
Which time's stern hand shall turn to dust and glass.
But in this verse, thy beauty shall remain,
A gilded monument 'gainst death's decay,
Though envious ages rage with cruel pain,
And steal all other glory clean away.
For whilst the world in darkness finds its close,
And earthly splendours crumble to the dust,
My humble lines shall bloom a living rose,
To hold thy memory in sacred trust.
So live thou ever in this written praise,
And mock the scythe of death through endless days.

###482###
The subtle thief of time doth steal our youth,
And plough deep furrows on the fairest brow,
Consuming beauty, health, and steadfast truth,
As swiftly as the moments vanish now.
What solace then for fragile mortal kind,
Against the ruin that doth all consume?
No balm exists, no refuge we can find,
To 'scape the shadow of a timeless tomb.
Unless, my love, in thee, a power reside,
To stand unconquered 'gainst oblivion's might,
And in this verse, thy spirit shall abide,
To shine eternal, banishing the night.
So shall our bond, defying death's harsh call,
Outlive the ages and transcend them all.

###483###
My tongue doth falter when I would commend
The matchless grace that in thy person dwells,
So poor a verse cannot aspire to mend
The silent stories that thy beauty tells.
My words are faint, unworthy of thy sight,
A humble tribute from a troubled breast,
Yet pure affection lends my spirit light,
And sets my fervent labours to the test.
For what I lack in art, I gain in heart,
A true devotion, constant and sincere,
Which plays its fond and unpretending part,
And holds thy image ever shining clear.
So take this gift, though simple it may seem,
A lover's truth, more potent than a dream.

###484###
When chill December doth usurp July,
And summer's blossom yields to winter's frost,
When golden leaves from lofty branches fly,
And all the transient beauty then is lost;
So too the world doth change its fickle face,
And fortunes turn with every passing breeze,
No steadfast hope, no firm abiding place,
But swift decay in all that nature sees.
Yet in my heart, one constant fire burns,
A sacred flame that time cannot abate,
To thee, my loyal spirit ever turns,
Defying ruin, destiny, and fate.
For true affection knows no ebb nor flow,
But stronger grows, the more the tempests blow.

###485###
How swift the moments pass, how brief our stay,
A fleeting breath upon the ocean's foam,
As blossoms fair that flourish for a day,
Then fade to nothing, finding nature's home.
What art can hold the rose when it is plucked?
Or stay the shadow ere it turns to night?
By time's decree, all earthly things are sucked
Into oblivion, hid from human sight.
But beauty's essence, by a poet's hand,
May live anew in lines of potent verse,
Enduring more than any shifting sand,
Defying death, and time's relentless curse.
So grant thy grace, to make my labours known,
That in these rhymes, thy spirit may be shown.

###486###
When doubts assail and shadows fill my mind,
And dark forebodings whisper of despair,
I seek thy image, solace there to find,
A star of hope that banishes all care.
For in thy gaze, a steadfast truth I see,
A loyal spirit, constant and profound,
Which drives away the fears that trouble me,
And heals the deepest, most pernicious wound.
Let tempest rage, and fortunes ebb and flow,
Let envious tongues speak falsehoods to the air,
My heart for thee a changeless faith doth show,
Beyond the reach of sorrow or of snare.
So strong my love, it fears no earthly strife,
The very anchor of my fragile life.

###487###
The morning sun, when first it gilds the east,
And paints the sky with hues of rosy light,
Doth hold its beauty, though it soon hath ceased,
To yield to noon, and then to fading night.
No summer's flower, however sweet and rare,
Can boast such grace as in thy form I see,
No moonlit stream, no dawn-besprinkled air,
Can match the mild perfections found in thee.
For thou art fair, beyond compare's embrace,
A living wonder, framed by nature's art,
A radiant marvel, blessing time and place,
Enthroned forever in my loyal heart.
So let the world its lesser beauties show,
Thou art the zenith, whither all must go.

###488###
When wanderers are lost upon the main,
And darkling clouds conceal the guiding star,
They yearn for light, to find their course again,
To know what haven lies beyond afar.
So in life's tempest, tossed by every tide,
My weary soul doth often lose its way,
And seeks a steady beacon to abide,
To lead it safely from the troubled day.
That beacon art thou, shining ever bright,
A constant comfort in this mortal strife,
Dispelling shadows with thy gentle light,
The very compass of my errant life.
For whilst thou shin'st, no darkness can prevail,
Thou art the pilot, I the trusting sail.

###489###
How like a winter is my soul bereft,
When thy dear presence doth no longer gleam,
And joy itself, no vital spark hath left,
But fades away, as in a vanished dream.
The birds are mute, the flowers droop and die,
The sun itself doth wear a paler hue,
No sweet delight can captivate mine eye,
When I am parted, cherished one, from you.
For absence is a keen and bitter foe,
That steals the colour from the brightest day,
And fills the empty hours with silent woe,
Until thy fond return shall chase away.
Come back, my love, and banish all my pain,
And make my desolate spirit bloom again.

###490###
Not marble nor the gilded monuments
Of princes shall outlive this powerful rhyme,
But thou, more bright than all their blandishments,
Shalt shine perpetual, conquering fierce Time.
When wasteful wars shall utterly confound
The strongest structures, built by human hand,
And all the living world shall sleep profound,
As if no life had ever graced the land;
Thy spirit then, by this true verse preserved,
Shall walk triumphant through oblivion's scorn,
And from the jaws of death, by me observed,
A newer being shall for thee be born.
So long as men can breathe, or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###491###
Let others praise the outward show of grace,
The transient beauty that doth soon decay,
And seek their comfort in a fleeting face,
Which time's harsh currents swiftly sweep away.
My truer eye doth pierce the veil of sense,
To find the essence dwelling deep within,
A noble spirit, rich in recompense,
Where lasting virtues constantly begin.
For though the lily's bloom may please the sight,
And roses flaunt their colours to the sun,
Thy inner worth, a more enduring light,
Outshines all earthly glories, one by one.
This deeper love no changing season fears,
But stronger grows with every passing year.

###492###
When Nature's hand, with cunning skill and might,
Did frame thy being, faultless and divine,
She gathered all that's lovely to the light,
And wrought a masterpiece, a perfect sign.
No blemished form, no shadow of defect,
But pure perfection, gloriously arrayed,
Such beauty as the heavens might elect,
A lasting wonder, never to be swayed.
For in thy face, all virtues seem to meet,
All grace and dignity, beyond compare,
A harmony of spirit, softly sweet,
A precious treasure, free from earthly care.
What praise then fit for such a wondrous art?
To hold thee ever in my ravished heart.

###493###
My pen, though humble, labours to express
The boundless reverence my spirit feels,
A deep devotion, born of pure excess,
Which all thy perfect excellence reveals.
For in thy shadow, I would gladly dwell,
Content to serve, and ever to obey,
A loyal subject, bound beneath thy spell,
Through darkest night and brightest shining day.
No worldly honours do I seek to gain,
No fleeting treasures, bought with toil and strife,
But only this: to banish every pain,
And lend some solace to thy precious life.
So take my vows, engraved in lines of truth,
And be the comfort of my fading youth.

###494###
When first thine eyes did meet my gazing sight,
A sudden wonder seized my startled breast,
As if a star had fallen from the night,
And found a dwelling-place within my rest.
No common beauty, but a grace profound,
Did then disclose its magic to my soul,
A harmony in every feature found,
To make my fractured spirit true and whole.
From that blest moment, knew I not my name,
But lived enchanted by thy sovereign sway,
And felt within my heart a burning flame,
Which guides my footsteps, lighting up my way.
So was I born anew, by love's sweet art,
And thou thenceforth didst hold my captive heart.

###495###
Let stormy winds conspire to shake the tree,
And raging floods o'erwhelm the verdant plain,
Let tempests roar across the troubled sea,
And all the fabric of the world sustain;
So let misfortune strike with cruelest hand,
And envious malice seek to do me wrong,
Let fickle fame desert me in the land,
And sorrow's lamentations grow full strong.
Yet in the midst of every bitter strife,
My faithful heart for thee shall constant be,
The only solace in this weary life,
A haven safe, from all adversity.
For greater strength than death itself hath love,
Sent from the timeless dwelling-place above.

###496###
Ah, wherefore did my unavailing tongue
Attempt to utter what the heart concealed?
When no fit praise by mortal can be sung,
To match the beauty that thy face revealed.
My simple words, unworthy of thy name,
But poorly show the fervent zeal I bear,
And in their weakness, bring my soul to shame,
That cannot paint thee fair beyond compare.
For Nature's skill hath wrought thee past all art,
And made thy form a wonder to behold,
Whilst I, with barren craft and failing heart,
Can but a fraction of thy worth unfold.
Forgive, dear love, this unaccomplished strain,
My fault it is, that speaks my love in vain.

###497###
The fairest flowers do but bloom a space,
And wither swiftly, touched by winter's chill,
As transient beauty finds no lasting place,
But yields to ruin, following nature's will.
The stoutest oaks, that brave a thousand years,
At last must fall beneath the axe's blow,
And crumble into dust, amidst our tears,
A silent witness to time's overthrow.
But love like mine, a deeper root doth take,
Than earth's frail growth, or man's constructed pride,
For thy dear sake, my loyal vows I make,
Through endless ages, ever to abide.
So let the world decay, and empires cease,
Our deathless bond shall find eternal peace.

###498###
All mortal things are subject to decay,
And brief the span allotted to our breath,
The brightest sun must yield to fading day,
And every living creature taste of death.
What hope remains for us, who stand so frail,
Against the universal, common doom?
When joy itself doth quickly turn to wail,
And shadows gather in the silent room.
Yet in the steadfast witness of my heart,
A truth unyielding, ever firm, I find,
That love for thee shall play a deathless part,
And leave all transient earthly woes behind.
So let fate frown, and ruin seize the earth,
My love for thee shall prove its endless worth.

###499###
My labouring thoughts, with deep desire do strive
To capture all the beauty in thy face,
That future ages may behold alive,
The matchless wonder of thy form and grace.
But ah, my skill is poor, my art too weak,
To limn the living spirit that I see,
No mortal hand can truly hope to speak
The vibrant glory that resides in thee.
For words are cold, and colours swiftly fade,
When set against thy bright reality,
A dim reflection, by my efforts made,
That scarce can show one half thy majesty.
Forgive the failure of my humble rhyme,
To prison perfect beauty for all time.

###500###
When doubt and fear like spectres haunt the mind,
And courage falters in the face of ill,
When all the world seems harsh and uninclined
To lend its aid, or bend unto my will;
Then do I turn to thee, my constant friend,
A steadfast solace in my deepest need,
Whose loyal love doth freely condescend
To plant within my breast a valiant seed.
For in thy strength, I find my own renewed,
A spirit bright, that darkness cannot quell,
By thy dear presence, every ill subdued,
And banished far, the fears that in me dwell.
So hold me fast, dear heart, and make me strong,
And turn my sighing into joyful song.

###501###
When I behold the ruinous decay,
And see the fairest creatures fade away,
How doth my heart lament the passing day,
To think what beauty vanishes each May.
For as the spring doth yield to summer's heat,
So youth's bright morn doth hasten to its close,
And swift-winged hours our mortal lives defeat,
Ere cruel winter spreads its chilling snows.
Yet in these lines thy memory shall live,
When marble monuments to dust are turned,
A timeless grace these humble words shall give,
By my true verse thy worth shall be discerned.
So shalt thou triumph over Death's cold hand,
And dwell eternal in this written land.

###502###
When forty winters shall besiege thy brow,
And dig deep trenches where thine eyes now shine,
Thy youthful beauty, which adorns thee now,
Shall be a tattered garment, past its prime.
Then if some ask, "Where hides thy beauty's might?"
Thou canst not answer with a whispered sigh,
"It lies within these hollows, lost to night,"
Lest thou be scorned for such a poor reply.
But hadst thou bred, to leave a living heir,
A child whose beauty mirrored thine own face,
Then couldst thou boast, dispelling all despair,
"My beauty lives within this youthful grace."
So to thyself art thou too cruel and kind,
Who leaves no copy of thy lovely mind.

###503###
Shall I compare thee to a summer's day?
Thou art more lovely and more temperate:
Rough winds do shake the darling buds of May,
And summer's lease hath all too short a date:
Sometime too hot the eye of heaven shines,
And often is his gold complexion dimmed;
And every fair from fair sometime declines,
By chance or nature's changing course untrimmed;
But thy eternal summer shall not fade,
Nor lose possession of that fair thou ow'st;
Nor shall Death brag thou wander'st in his shade,
When in eternal lines to time thou grow'st:
So long as men can breathe or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###504###
When in disgrace with fortune and men's eyes,
I all alone beweep my outcast state,
And trouble deaf heaven with my bootless cries,
And look upon myself, and curse my fate,
Wishing me like to one more rich in hope,
Featured like him, like him with friends possessed,
Desiring this man's art, and that man's scope,
With what I most enjoy contented least;
Yet in these thoughts myself almost despising,
Haply I think on thee, and then my state,
Like to the lark at break of day arising
From sullen earth, sings hymns at heaven's gate;
For thy sweet love remembered such wealth brings
That then I scorn to change my state with kings.

###505###
My love is strengthened, though more weak in show,
Than that which daily by exchange is fed;
Love is not love which alters when it ought to go,
Or bends with the remover to be removed.
Oh, no! it is an ever-fixed mark,
That looks on tempests and is never shaken;
It is the star to every wandering bark,
Whose worth's unknown, although his height be taken.
Love's not Time's fool, though rosy lips and cheeks
Within his bending sickle's compass come;
Love alters not with his brief hours and weeks,
But bears it out even to the edge of doom.
If this be error and upon me proved,
I never writ, nor no man ever loved.

###506###
That time of year thou mayst in me behold
When yellow leaves, or none, or few, do hang
Upon those boughs which shake against the cold,
Bare ruined choirs, where late the sweet birds sang.
In me thou seest the twilight of such day
As after sunset fadeth in the west,
Which by and by black night doth take away,
Death's second self, that seals up all in rest.
In me thou seest the glowing of such fire
That on the ashes of its youth doth lie,
As the death-bed whereon it must expire,
Consumed with that which it was nourished by.
This thou perceivest, which makes thy love more strong,
To love that well which thou must leave ere long.

###507###
From fairest creatures we desire increase,
That thereby beauty's rose might never die,
But as the riper should by time decrease,
His tender heir might bear his memory:
But thou, contracted to thine own bright eyes,
Feed'st thy light's flame with self-substantial fuel,
Making a famine where abundance lies,
Thyself thy foe, to thy sweet self too cruel.
Thou that art now the world's fresh ornament
And only herald to the gaudy spring,
Within thine own bud buriest thy content
And, tender churl, makest waste in niggarding.
Pity the world, or else this glutton be,
To eat the world's due, by the grave and thee.

###508###
When I do count the clock that tells the time,
And see the brave day sunk in hideous night;
When I behold the violet past its prime,
And sable curls all silvered o'er with white;
When lofty trees I see barren of leaves,
Which erst from heat did canopy the herd,
And summer's green all girded up in sheaves,
Borne on the bier with white and bristly beard,
Then of thy beauty do I question make,
That thou among the wastes of time must go,
Since sweets and beauties do themselves forsake
And die as fast as they see others grow;
And nothing 'gainst Time's scythe can make defence,
Save breed, to brave him when he takes thee hence.

###509###
Poor soul, the center of my sinful earth,
Fooled by these rebel powers that thee array,
Why dost thou pine within and suffer dearth,
Painting thy outward walls so costly gay?
Why so large cost, having so short a lease,
Dost thou upon thy fading mansion spend?
Shall worms, inheritors of this excess,
Eat up thy charge? Is this thy body's end?
Then, soul, live thou upon thy servant's loss,
And let that pine to aggravate thy store;
Buy terms divine in selling hours of dross;
Within be fed, without be rich no more.
So shalt thou feed on Death, that feeds on men,
And Death once dead, there's no more dying then.

###510###
O, lest the world should task you to recite
What merit in my verse at all is found,
Or what it is that makes me write,
When all my best doth to oblivion bound;
You are my all the argument I write,
The field wherein my spirit doth contend,
The constant star that guides my lonely night,
And to my thoughts doth true perfection lend.
And as the moon doth draw the ocean's tide,
So doth thy beauty draw my very soul,
My heart, in thrall, can nowhere else abide,
But yields to thy most absolute control.
So blame me not if I record your praise,
For you alone give substance to my days.

###511###
Mine eye hath played the painter and hath steeled
Thy lovely form within my heart's design;
My body is the frame wherein 'tis held,
And all the skill of art is used to define.
For through thy windows, now mine eyes, I find
The very treasure of thy soul enshrined,
And by their light, thy beauty's truth confined,
A masterpiece for every viewing mind.
But eyes with eyes so true a quarrel make,
That they accuse each other of their crime;
For while my eyes thy outward beauty take,
My heart doth covet thee through space and time.
So in my heart, thy image pure I keep,
As in a chest where priceless jewels sleep.

###512###
When in the chronicle of wasted time
I see descriptions of the fairest wights,
And beauty making beautiful old rhyme
In praise of ladies dead and lovely knights,
Then, in the blazon of sweet beauty's best,
Of hand, of foot, of lip, of eye, of brow,
I see their antique pen would have expressed
Even such a beauty as you master now.
So all their praises are but prophecies
Of this our time, all you prefiguring;
And, for they looked but with divining eyes,
They had not skill enough your worth to sing.
For we, which now behold these present days,
Have eyes to wonder, but lack tongues to praise.

###513###
Weary with toil, I haste me to my bed,
The dear repose for limbs with travel tired;
But then begins a journey in my head,
To work my mind, when body's work's expired:
For then my thoughts—from far where I abide—
Intend a zealous pilgrimage to thee,
And keep my drooping eyelids open wide,
Looking on darkness which the blind do see.
Save that my soul's imaginary sight
Presents thy shadow to my tired view,
Which, like a jewel hung in ghastly night,
Makes black night beauteous and her old face new.
Lo, thus, by day my limbs, by night my mind,
For thee and for myself no quiet find.

###514###
Let me confess that we two must be twain,
Although our undivided loves are one;
So shall those blots that do with me remain
Without thy help by me be borne alone.
In our two loves there is but one respect,
Though in our lives a separable spite,
Which though it alter not thy dear effect,
Yet doth it steal sweet hours from thy delight.
I may not evermore acknowledge thee,
Lest my bewailèd guilt should do thee shame,
Nor thou with public kindness honour me,
