Unless you take that honour from my name.
But do not so; I love thee in such sort
As thou being mine, mine is thy good report.

###515###
When my love swears that she is made of truth,
I do believe her, though I know she lies,
That she might think me some untutored youth,
Unlearned in the world's false subtleties.
Thus vainly thinking that she thinks me young,
Although she knows my days are past the best,
Simply I credit her false-speaking tongue:
On both sides thus is simple truth suppressed.
But wherefore says she not she is unjust?
And wherefore say not I that I am old?
Oh, love's best habit is in seeming trust,
And age in love loves not to have years told:
Therefore I lie with her and she with me,
And in our faults by lies we flatter'd be.

###516###
Since I left you, mine eye is in my mind,
And that which governs me to go about
Doth here abye so fixed, that it doth find
No object worthy of his inward doubt.
For gracious beauty doth oft give offence,
And what good turns, then, can the heart repay?
The world's wide theatre, its broad expanse,
Doth lose its charm when thou art far away.
So if I wander, it is but to stray
From out myself, a journey from my soul;
For every step that bears me far away
Is but a further part of thy control.
And till I meet with thee, my thoughts do turn
To where thy blessed image still doth burn.

###517###
No more be grieved at that which thou hast done:
Roses have thorns, and silver fountains mud;
Clouds and eclipses stain both moon and sun,
And loathsome canker lives in sweetest bud.
All men make faults, and even I in this,
Authorizing thy trespass with compare,
Myself corrupting, salving thy amiss,
Excusing thy sins more than thy sins are:
For to thy sensual fault I bring in sense—
Thy adverse party is thy advocate—
And 'gainst myself a lawful plea commence:
Such civil war is in my love and hate,
That I an accessary needs must be
To that sweet theft which thee from me hath reft.

###518###
To me, fair friend, you never can be old,
For as you were when first your eye I eyed,
Such seems your beauty still. Three winters cold
Have from the forests shook three summers' pride,
Three beauteous springs to yellow autumn turned
In process of the seasons have I seen,
Three April perfumes in three hot Junes burned,
Since first I saw you fresh, which yet are green.
Ah, yet doth beauty, like a dial-hand,
Steal from his figure, and no pace perceived;
So your sweet semblance, which I here command,
Hath from the world insensibly been heaved.
For fear of which, my love, I praise you now,
Ere you, by time, from memory must bow.

###519###
When thou shalt be dispersed to dust away,
And in the earth thy memory buried deep,
When all thy grace and beauty doth decay,
And silent slumber seals thine eyes in sleep,
Then shall my verse, more lasting than thy breath,
Preserve the spirit of thy youthful prime;
And rescue thee from Time's consuming death,
And raise thy name beyond this fleeting time.
For though the grave may claim thy mortal part,
And worms consume what once was fair to see,
Yet shall thou live immortal in my heart,
And shine eternal, cherished, dear, and free.
Thus, though thy body may dissolve to nought,
Thy deathless praise in these lines shall be wrought.

###520###
O from what power hast thou this powerful might,
With insufficiency to move my mind
To scorn the fair and count the beauty slight
Of all this world besides, yet bear thy kind?
When I compare my state with thine, I find
No cause but thine to make my heart thus swell;
For by thy grace, my spirit is refined,
And every doubt doth in thy presence quell.
So let me not be false, nor thou unkind,
But let our love endure through every test,
A bond of hearts that time can never bind
To fading woes or troubles that infest.
For in thy love, my truest joy I gain,
And all my sorrows turn to peace again.

###521###
When lovely youth doth in its glory bloom,
And nature's hand with cunning art doth grace,
Doth beauty fade, and hasten to its doom,
Ere time's swift foot effaceth every trace?
Ah, cruel question, posed by thoughts of dread,
For fleeting joys do quickly pass away,
And memories of pleasure swiftly fled,
Leave naught behind but sorrow and dismay.
But in these lines, thy virtues I will bind,
A testament against oblivion's sway,
So future ages shall thy beauty find,
And praise thee still, when dust doth thee array.
So long as men can breathe, or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###522###
My love, no frost shall blight thy tender spring,
Nor envious clouds obscure thy morning's light,
For in my verse, thy deathless praise I sing,
To banish shadow with eternal might.
Let Time, the thief, steal beauty from the rose,
And scatter dust where golden tresses lay,
The steadfast spirit that within thee glows
Shall mock his scythe, and fear no dire decay.
For though the world may turn to ash and dust,
And monuments of kings forgotten lie,
My faithful heart, in which I place my trust,
Shall hold thy image till the heavens sigh.
So rest assured, though earthly glories cease,
Thy name shall live, and find eternal peace.

###523###
The morning sun, when from the east it springs,
Doth paint the clouds with hues of fiery gold,
And joyful birds on soaring, feathered wings,
A dulcet, waking melody unfold.
Yet all this pomp, this transient, painted show,
Is but a shadow to thy living grace,
For from thy eyes, a brighter lustre flow,
That doth illuminate this mortal place.
What need have I of jewels, rich and rare,
Or garments spun with thread of finest gleam,
When thy dear presence doth all else compare
To but a faint and unavailing dream?
Thy single glance doth richer wealth impart,
Than all the treasures earth holds in her heart.

###524###
When I consider how my days consume,
And fleeting hours like hurried shadows fly,
I fear that soon, encompassed by the tomb,
My mortal form shall unremembered lie.
But then I turn, dear friend, to thoughts of thee,
Whose gentle spirit doth my soul sustain,
And in thy truth, a lasting hope I see,
A balm to soothe my sorrow and my pain.
For though the Fates may sever earthly ties,
And memory fade with each succeeding year,
The bond we share, beneath these changing skies,
Shall banish every doubt and every fear.
Thus shall my spirit find eternal rest,
Enshrined within the chamber of thy breast.

###525###
My muse, why art thou silent in this hour,
When beauty calls for praises from thy quill?
Doth dull neglect obscure thy former power,
Or slothful ease thy keenest ardour kill?
Awake, my spirit, from this lethargy,
And let thy voice in soaring numbers rise,
To celebrate the grace I find in thee,
Whose worth transcends the praise of mortal eyes.
Lest envious Time, with unrelenting haste,
Should bear away the moments we now keep,
And future ages mourn a beauty waste,
While unrecorded, silent slumbers sleep.
Then rouse thyself, and let thy true light shine,
To make her virtues evermore divine.

###526###
Oft have I watched the seasons make their rounds,
From budding spring to winter's icy hold,
And seen how nature's changing law abounds,
With tales of youth that swiftly turn to old.
The vibrant rose, that once did brightly glow,
Doth lose its blush, and shed its petals wide,
The mighty oak, that winds could not o'erthrow,
At last must fall, by silent worms defied.
So too must I, whose breath doth now respire,
Surrender life unto the common dust,
And quench the flame of this corporeal fire,
Returning to the earth from whence I burst.
But in thy love, my soul finds endless grace,
And time's harsh rule shall never quite efface.

###527###
Shall I compare thee to a summer's day?
Thou art more lovely and more temperate:
Rough winds do shake the darling buds of May,
And summer's lease hath all too short a date:
Sometime too hot the eye of heaven shines,
And often is his gold complexion dimm'd;
And every fair from fair sometime declines,
By chance or nature's changing course untrimm'd;
But thy eternal summer shall not fade,
Nor lose possession of that fair thou ow'st;
Nor shall Death brag thou wander'st in his shade,
When in eternal lines to time thou grow'st:
So long as men can breathe or eyes can see,
So long lives this and this gives life to thee.

###528###
When in disgrace with fortune and men's eyes,
I all alone beweep my outcast state,
And trouble deaf heaven with my bootless cries,
And look upon myself and curse my fate,
Wishing me like to one more rich in hope,
Featured like him, like him with friends possess'd,
Desiring this man's art, and that man's scope,
With what I most enjoy contented least;
Yet in these thoughts myself almost despising,
Haply I think on thee, and then my state,
Like to the lark at break of day arising
From sullen earth, sings hymns at heaven's gate;
For thy sweet love remember'd such wealth brings
That then I scorn to change my state with kings.

###529###
The eye of heaven doth often gaze askance,
Upon the earth, with cloudy, sullen brow,
And changing fortune leads a fickle dance,
To mock the hopes that human hearts avow.
What joy hath man, that doth not swiftly fly?
What treasure gained, that time doth not consume?
All worldly pomp beneath the sun must die,
And sink at last into a silent tomb.
Yet in thy truth, a constant light I find,
A beacon steady 'midst the storm's dark rage,
A lasting comfort to my troubled mind,
A noble worth that doth defy all age.
So let the world its fleeting pleasures spend,
My love for thee shall never know an end.

###530###
My tongue, for shame, doth falter in its plea,
To speak the depth of passion I possess,
For common words would dim the light of thee,
And make thy virtues seem no whit the less.
What artist's brush, or sculptor's cunning hand,
Could capture essence of thy radiant grace?
No mortal wit can truly understand
The gentle beauty beaming from thy face.
So let my silence bear this heavy weight,
A testament to what cannot be told,
For in my heart, thy love doth dominate,
A story greater than was e'er unrolled.
My fervent sighs shall whisper of thy worth,
The dearest treasure given unto earth.

###531###
Lo, in the sky, the sun doth bravely rise,
Dispelling shadows of the fading night,
And with his beams, he opens mortal eyes,
To greet the world with freshness and with light.
So in my soul, when darkness held its sway,
And heavy grief did weigh my spirit down,
Thy presence came, a brighter, fairer day,
And banished sorrow from my weary town.
For in thy smile, a healing power dwells,
To mend the heart that suffered bitter pain,
And from thy lips, a wondrous music wells,
To cheer the soul and make it whole again.
No greater solace can my spirit crave,
Than thy dear love, that doth my being save.

###532###
When I behold the proudest monuments,
That human toil hath raised with might and main,
And know that Time, with conquering intents,
Will level all to common dust again;
When I perceive how fleeting is man's breath,
How quickly fades the bloom of youth and prime,
And how we hasten to the door of death,
Imprisoned by the tyranny of time;
Then do I fret, lest thy sweet beauty too,
Should fall a victim to this tyrant's hand,
And vanish quite, like morning's transient dew,
Before the sun upon the waking land.
But fear not, love, my verse shall keep thee green,
The fairest flower that earth hath ever seen.

###533###
The fickle world, with ever-changing show,
Doth lure men's hearts to seek what cannot last,
And swift desires, like restless waters, flow,
To joys that perish in the fleeting past.
Some court for power, others for mere gain,
And some for praise, that is but empty air,
Their weary lives consumed by toil and pain,
To grasp at shadows, fraught with future care.
But I, dear friend, no longer seek such things,
For in thy truth, a steadfast joy I find,
That brighter shines than crowns of earthly kings,
A lasting comfort to my peaceful mind.
Let fortune frown, and all her gifts withdraw,
Thy constant heart fulfills all nature's law.

###534###
Full many a flower, that doth in beauty bloom,
Is born to blush unseen, and waste its scent,
And many a gem, within the ocean's gloom,
Lies hid from view, its brilliance never lent.
So too, perchance, a spirit pure and bright,
May pass through life, unheeded and unknown,
Its virtues veiled, its gentle, inward light,
A lonely secret, cherished all alone.
But thou, whose grace doth shine for all to see,
Art like the sun, that warms the waking earth,
No hidden jewel, but a spreading tree,
Whose generous branches give abundant birth.
Thy truth and beauty, openly displayed,
Have made my heart eternally remade.

###535###
The heavy clouds that weep their dreary rain,
Do hide the heavens from our longing sight,
And sullen winds sweep o'er the barren plain,
To shroud the world in sorrow and in night.
So too, my soul, when distant from thy face,
Doth sink beneath a weight of grievous woe,
And finds no comfort in this joyless place,
Where former pleasures can no longer glow.
Oh, haste thee back, and banish all my fears,
And let thy presence chase away the gloom,
For only thou canst dry these bitter tears,
And rescue me from this despairing tomb.
Return, my light, and make the darkness flee,
For all my solace doth reside in thee.

###536###
When first I saw thee, youth had set its seal
Upon thy brow, with beauty's tender grace,
And time, the foe, had not begun to steal
The budding promise from thy lovely face.
Now years have passed, and subtly left their mark,
With silver strands among thy raven hair,
And wisdom's lines appear amidst the dark,
A testament to life's incessant care.
Yet in thine eyes, a deeper lustre dwells,
Than ever shone in youth's tempestuous pride,
A richer beauty, that the heart compels,
A spirit true, where gentle virtues hide.
So fear not age, nor what the seasons bring,
Thy worth eternal, I shall ever sing.

###537###
My love is like a star, forever bright,
That guides my ship through tempests of despair,
A constant beacon in the darkest night,
That frees my spirit from each worldly care.
Let cynics scoff, and mock my steadfast heart,
And cold indifference scorn my fervent plea,
No subtle doubt shall tear our souls apart,
Nor bitter envy steal thy grace from me.
For though the world may change from day to day,
And fickle fortunes rise and then decline,
My sacred vows shall never pass away,
But hold thy image in this breast of mine.
So let them speak, who know not truth's embrace,
Our deathless love shall time and fate outface.

###538###
The proudest towers, that pierce the azure sky,
And ancient walls, where warlike banners flew,
Must yield at last, and in oblivion lie,
Their strength forgotten, and their glories few.
No earthly might can Time's sharp scythe withstand,
Nor beauty keep its bloom against his rage,
He lays his hand on all throughout the land,
And turns to dust each fleeting, gilded page.
But in my verse, thy beauty I shall bind,
A lasting challenge to his cruel decree,
So future ages shall thy virtues find,
And marvel at the deathless grace of thee.
Though stones may crumble, and though empires fall,
My loving lines shall triumph over all.

###539###
When to the sessions of sweet silent thought
I summon up remembrance of things past,
I sigh the lack of many a thing I sought,
And with old woes new wail my dear time's waste:
Then can I drown an eye, unused to flow,
For precious friends hid in death's dateless night,
And weep afresh love's long since cancell'd woe,
And moan the expense of many a vanish'd sight:
Then can I grieve at grievances foregone,
And heavily from woe to woe tell o'er
The sad account of fore-bemoaned moan,
Which I new pay as if not paid before.
But if the while I think on thee, dear friend,
All losses are restor'd, and sorrows end.

###540###
Let fickle fate her bitter wrath impart,
And storm of sorrow beat upon my head,
Let treach'rous lies attempt to break my heart,
And envious tongues pronounce my spirit dead.
I shall not yield, nor let my courage fail,
While in my soul thy noble image gleams,
For thy true love, a shield that must prevail,
Doth guard me from all dark and fearful dreams.
What power hath pain, what sting hath harsh regret,
When thy dear presence doth my spirit raise?
All worldly troubles I can well forget,
Lost in the wonder of thy loving gaze.
So come what may, I stand in strength complete,
Thy steadfast love makes every sorrow sweet.

###541###
Whence comes this beauty that adorns thy face?
A fleeting moment, fraught with gentle grace.
Time's cruel hand doth oft erase each trace,
And steal from youth its swift and hopeful pace.
Thy glorious eyes, like stars within the skies,
Reflect a truth that ne'er in slumber lies;
But swiftly as the morning dew-drop dies,
So passeth life, to turn to dust and sighs.
Oh, that some cunning art could hold thy breath,
And shield thy form from Time's impending death,
For nature's common doom to all it saith,
No mortal man such lasting beauty hath.
Yet in these lines, thy lasting form I see,
And grant eternity to dwell with thee.

###542###
My love is like a star, both fixed and bright,
Whose steady beams throughout the darkness stream;
It doth not change with day, nor yield to night,
But holds its course, a constant, guiding gleam.
Though envious clouds may seek to dim its ray,
And fortune's tempest rage with bitter scorn,
My loyal heart shall never turn away,
But faithful stand, as on creation's morn.
Let fleeting pleasures tempt the fickle mind,
And vows be broken with a careless word;
In constancy alone shall true love find
Its perfect peace, by righteous Heaven heard.
For while I breathe, and while my blood doth flow,
My steadfast love for thee shall ever grow.

###543###
Shall summer's bounty fade from off the bough,
And winter's chill embrace its verdant pride?
So doth fair beauty change, as time allows,
And fleeting joys like morning shadows glide.
But these poor lines, by humble pen expressed,
Shall hold thy grace beyond the reach of doom;
When marble crumbles and the ages rest,
Thy radiant form shall conquer every tomb.
For not in stone, nor in the gilded shrine,
But in the poet's verse thou shalt endure;
A living spirit, death can ne'er confine,
Thy memory bright, eternally secure.
Thus, though all else in earth to dust decay,
Thou shalt live on, defying time's swift sway.

###544###
When in the glass I see my wrinkles creep,
And youth's fair blossoms turn to winter's frost,
A sudden tremor through my soul doth sweep,
To think how much by time hath been quite lost.
My fading locks, once dark as raven's wing,
Are now arrayed in silver, pale and grey;
The joyful songs that once my spirit sing,
Are muffled now by life's declining day.
Yet do I find some solace in this plight,
That though my body wastes and doth decay,
My inward spirit gleams with clearer light,
And doth perceive a more enduring way.
For beauty fades, and earthly pleasures cease,
But truth and love can bring the soul release.

###545###
Why doth my verse so seldom show thy worth,
Though all my heart doth worship at thy shrine?
Is't that my spirit finds no words on earth
To match the grace that doth in thee combine?
My tongue, alas, seems fettered by its fear,
Lest praise too faint should do thy beauty wrong;
No gilded phrases make thy virtues clear,
Nor can my humble measure hold thy song.
Others may boast of passion's fervent flame,
And paint their loves with colours bold and bright;
I, silently, but whisper forth thy name,
And find my solace in thy gentle light.
For true affection needs no loud display,
But shines most pure when words are put away.

###546###
When first I saw thy eyes, a heaven did seem
To break upon my dull and weary gaze;
As from a slumber, wakened by a dream,
I felt a warmth that banished all my daze.
Thy gentle voice, a music soft and low,
Did steal into my soul with sweet accord,
And made my captive heart sincerely know
That I had found my cherished, rightful lord.
The world without, with all its harsh alarms,
Did fade away, and left but thee and I;
Enfolded safe within thy loving arms,
I wished that moment never more to die.
For in thy presence, all my sorrows cease,
And troubled thoughts are hushed to perfect peace.

###547###
The weary sun descends to ocean's bed,
And draws a veil of darkness o'er the land;
The world is hushed, as if in slumber led,
And lonely thoughts begin to take command.
Then in my mind, thy image doth arise,
More bright than moon, more radiant than star;
It doth disperse the shadows from my eyes,
And brings thy presence, though thou art afar.
What solace then, to know that thou art true,
And though removed by space, yet ever near;
My heart finds comfort in this tender view,
And banishes each doubt, each rising fear.
So shall I dream until the morn doth break,
And for thy love, each silent night partake.

###548###
My absence from thy sight doth grieve me sore,
As when the sun withdraws its vital heat;
My very being longs for thee the more,
And finds no joy, no pleasure truly sweet.
The hours crawl by with leaden, weary pace,
Each moment stretched, an age of slow despair;
And naught can fill the void of thy dear face,
Nor ease the burden that my spirit bears.
I walk through fields, where spring's fresh colours lie,
And hear the birds their cheerful carols sing;
Yet all seems dim beneath a leaden sky,
For thy bright presence doth no solace bring.
Return, my love, and banish every sigh,
Lest without thee, my longing spirit die.

###549###
Let those who boast of titles, wealth, or power,
Exult in trifles, fading with the light;
I count my treasure in a single hour
Spent in thy company, both day and night.
No kingly crown, no scepter's proud command,
No vast dominion stretched across the sea,
Can yield such joy within this mortal land,
As one sweet moment I do spend with thee.
For outward pomp and fortune's fickle smile
Are but a shadow, transient and vain;
They may deceive the foolish for a while,
But cannot soothe the heart's enduring pain.
My true content, my soul's profound delight,
Is found in thee, my never-setting light.

###550###
When nature's cunning hand hath wrought its best,
And painted flowers with hues beyond compare,
Or set the lark's sweet music in my breast,
To fill the morning with a joyful air;
Then do I think upon thy matchless grace,
And how all beauty doth in thee reside,
No mortal art can mirror forth thy face,
Nor all the world such perfect form can hide.
For thou art fashioned from celestial mold,
A vision sent from heavens high above;
A story that can never be untold,
The living essence of eternal love.
So let the world display its fleeting show,
My heart sees naught but where thy beauties glow.

###551###
The fleeting hours, like shadows, pass us by,
And steal from youth its fresh and tender bloom;
The brightest days beneath a cloudless sky
Are hurried onward to an early tomb.
So doth the rose, in summer's gentle heat,
Unfold its petals, fragrant, soft, and fair;
Then quickly fade, its beauty bittersweet,
As autumn's breath begins to fill the air.
Ah, what avail, this transient, fading grace,
If naught remains to mark where beauty trod?
Unless some verse can fix thy lovely face,
And give thee life beyond the common clod.
Thus in these lines, thy memory shall not die,
But live renewed beneath an endless sky.

###552###
Mine eyes have seen the spring in garments green,
And watched the summer ripen golden grain;
The autumn leaves, in rich array, have been
A glorious sight, ere winter's icy reign.
But ne'er a season, in its full display,
Nor all the wonders nature can unfold,
Hath held my gaze or charmed my heart away,
Like thy dear presence, worth more than fine gold.
For in thy form, all loveliness resides,
A constant spring that never knows decay;
Where beauty's purest, fairest essence hides,
To chase the shadows from my darkest day.
So let the seasons turn and swiftly flee,
My steadfast heart finds endless spring in thee.

###553###
If I could choose what fate should mark my way,
Or bid the stars decree my earthly end,
I would but ask to see thee every day,
And on thy gentle counsel still depend.
No grand ambition stirs my tranquil breast,
No lust for power, nor for worldly fame;
In thy affection I have found my rest,
And cherish only love's enduring flame.
Let others chase the phantom joys of earth,
And build their fortunes on deceit and guile;
My humble lot, from my true love's sweet birth,
Is but to serve thee for a little while.
And when my breath at last begins to fail,
To whisper forth thy name, beyond the veil.

###554###
When I remember how our paths first crossed,
A sudden joy doth thrill my aging breast;
Like one who found a treasure long since lost,
My spirit then was put to blissful test.
Before thy sight, my life was dull and grey,
A barren landscape, withered by the frost;
But thy sweet presence chased the night away,
And all my former sorrows were engrossed.
What power had I, to merit such a prize?
What virtue mine, to claim so fair a friend?
My humble heart, before thy radiant eyes,
Doth simply marvel, till its earthly end.
So let me serve thee, with my final breath,
And love thee truly, e'en beyond all death.

###555###
The sun itself, though sovereign in the sky,
Doth sometimes veil its golden, glorious face;
And earthly kings, how proud soe'er they lie,
Are often humbled from their lofty place.
So human beauty, though it shine so bright,
Is but a fleeting flower, quickly gone;
And fortune's favours, changing with the light,
Are oft withdrawn ere rising of the dawn.
But in thy spirit, truth and grace reside,
A beauty deeper than the outward show;
Which time's relentless current cannot ride,
Nor envious shadows from its essence throw.
Thus, though all else to rust and ruin fall,
Thy inner worth shall stand, surviving all.

###556###
My thoughts, like weary pilgrims, journey far,
To seek thy image in each waking dream;
They follow thee, as sailors seek a star,
Or thirsty travellers a cooling stream.
And when they find thee, though but in my mind,
A sudden sweetness doth my soul imbue;
All worldly cares I leave then far behind,
And dwell awhile in joys both fresh and new.
But oh, what sorrow when the vision fades,
And stark reality resumes its reign!
My spirit then in mournful darkness wades,
And longs for that sweet imagery again.
Thus, caught between what is and what might be,
I live enslaved to absent thoughts of thee.

###557###
How shall I praise thy beauty, passing fair,
Without resort to flattery's false art?
My simple words can scarce thy grace declare,
Nor plumb the depths of thy most noble heart.
The poets of old, with their melodious strain,
Might seek to capture what in thee doth shine;
But all their efforts would prove quite in vain,
To paint a semblance of a love divine.
For thou art built of something more profound
Than mortal phrase or cunning rhyme can show;
A silent truth, where perfect joys are found,
And virtue's gentle spirit doth outflow.
So let my silence speak what words conceal,
The fervent passion that my soul doth feel.

###558###
When I behold the proud and lofty tree,
Whose ancient branches brave the tempest's shock,
Or watch the ocean's wide immensity,
That doth assail the firm and steadfast rock;
Then do I ponder on our human span,
How frail and fleeting is our mortal breath;
A transient moment in God's mighty plan,
From fragile birth unto consuming death.
Yet fear not, love, the tyrant's harsh decree,
Nor dread the swift approach of passing years;
For in this verse, thou shalt forever be
Immune from sorrow, safe from all thy fears.
My lines shall grant thy beauty endless fame,
And speak thy praises with eternal flame.

###559###
The world, with all its bustling, noisy strife,
And busy cares that vex the human breast,
Doth oft encroach upon my inner life,
And rob my weary spirit of its rest.
But then I turn my thoughts to thee, my dear,
And instantly, the tumult fades away;
Thy gentle image brings a solace clear,
And drives the shadows from my troubled day.
As storms subside before the sun's bright ray,
So vanish worries when thy face I see;
Thou art the anchor in life's stormy fray,
The constant comfort that upholds my plea.
So let me keep thy essence ever near,
To banish sorrow, banish every fear.

###560###
No greater love than this can e'er be told,
Than that which binds my fervent heart to thine;
A treasure richer far than gems or gold,
A sacred bond, enduring and divine.
Though time may steal the freshness from thy cheek,
And winter's frost upon thy brow appear,
My steadfast soul shall never cease to seek
The deeper beauty that I hold so dear.
For outward form doth quickly fade away,
And earthly pleasures vanish in the dust;
But loyal love, defying all decay,
Holds firm its truth, in everlasting trust.
So let us journey through life's changing scene,
With hearts entwined, eternally serene.

###561###
When wasteful Time doth ravage beauty's bloom,
And envious Age doth steal youth's gentle grace,
Then summer's glory yields to winter's gloom,
And wrinkles mar the fairest human face.
So swift the hours that lead us to decay,
And steal from us the treasures of our prime,
No art can hold youth's fleeting, bright array,
Nor turn the current of relentless Time.
Yet in these lines, my love, thy image lives,
Though dust to dust thy mortal form descend,
This verse alone a lasting solace gives,
When all beside doth perish in the end.
So long as men can breathe, or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###562###
Shall I compare thee to a summer's day?
Thou art more lovely and more temperate:
Rough winds do shake the darling buds of May,
And summer's lease hath all too short a date;
Sometime too hot the eye of heaven shines,
And often is his gold complexion dimm'd;
And every fair from fair sometime declines,
By chance, or nature's changing course, untrimm'd;
But thy eternal summer shall not fade,
Nor lose possession of that fair thou ow'st;
Nor shall Death brag thou wander'st in his shade,
When in eternal lines to time thou grow'st:
So long as men can breathe or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###563###
From fairest creatures we desire increase,
That thereby beauty's rose might never die,
But as the riper should by time decease,
His tender heir might bear his memory:
But thou, contracted to thine own bright eyes,
Feed'st thy light's flame with self-substantial fuel,
Making a famine where abundance lies,
Thyself thy foe, to thy sweet self too cruel.
Thou that art now the world's fresh ornament,
And only herald to the gaudy spring,
Within thine own bud buriest thy content,
And, tender churl, mak'st waste in niggarding.
Pity the world, or else this glutton be,
To eat the world's due, by the grave and thee.

###564###
Mine eye hath play'd the painter and hath stell'd
Thy beauty's form in table of my heart;
My body is the frame wherein 'tis held,
And perspective it is best painter's art.
For through the painter no where in my mind,
It is the heart wherein all beauty lies,
There stands thy picture, living, sweet, and kind,
Which in my heart's chamber, never dies.
Thus is his cheater of the eye and hand,
For it doth shape my love and doth confine
That which transcends all art, throughout the land,
No earthly pigment could portray thy line.
But for this picture, I can thee define,
As truest love, eternally divine.

###565###
When I do count the clock that tells the time,
And see the brave day sunk in hideous night;
When I behold the violet past prime,
And sable curls all silver'd o'er with white;
When lofty trees I see barren of leaves,
Which erst from heat did canopy the herd,
And summer's green all girded up in sheaves,
Borne on the bier with white and bristly beard,
Then of thy beauty do I question make,
That thou among the wastes of time must go,
Since sweets and beauties do themselves forsake
And die as fast as they see others grow;
And nothing 'gainst Time's scythe can make defence
Save breed, to brave him when he takes thee hence.

###566###
My love is strengthen'd, though more weak in seeming;
I love not less, though less the show appear:
That love is merchandiz'd whose rich esteeming
The owner's tongue doth publish everywhere.
Our love was new, and then but in the spring,
When I was wont to greet it with my lays;
As in the year doth every blessed thing
Come to a truth, and bloom in riper days.
But now, with years, my verse is grown more faint,
And seems to lack the vigour of its youth;
Yet in my soul, no weary, sad complaint,
But deeper roots of constancy and truth.
Then marvel not, though I have told thee less,
My love is more, though I want words to express.

###567###
Not marble, nor the gilded monuments
Of princes, shall outlive this powerful rhyme;
But you shall shine more bright in these contents
Than unswept stone, besmear'd with sluttish time.
When wasteful war shall statues overturn,
And broils root out the work of masonry,
Nor Mars his sword nor war's quick fire shall burn
The living record of your memory.
'Gainst death and all oblivious enmity
Shall you pace forth; your praise shall still find room
Even in the eyes of all posterity
That wear this world out to the ending doom.
So, till the judgment that yourself arise,
You live in this, and dwell in lovers' eyes.

###568###
Poor soul, the centre of my sinful earth,
These rebel powers that thee array,
Why dost thou pine within and suffer dearth,
Painting thy outward walls so costly gay?
Why so large cost, having so short a lease,
Dost thou upon thy fading mansion spend?
Shall worms, inheritors of this excess,
Eat up thy charge? Is this thy body's end?
Then, soul, live thou upon thy servant's loss,
And let that pine to aggravate thy store;
Buy terms divine in selling hours of dross;
Within be fed, without be rich no more:
So shalt thou feed on Death, that feeds on men,
And Death once dead, there's no more dying then.

###569###
When in disgrace with fortune and men's eyes,
I all alone beweep my outcast state,
And trouble deaf heaven with my bootless cries,
And look upon myself and curse my fate,
Wishing me like to one more rich in hope,
Featured like him, like him with friends possess'd,
Desiring this man's art, and that man's scope,
With what I most enjoy contented least;
Yet in these thoughts myself almost despising,
Haply I think on thee, and then my state,
Like to the lark at break of day arising
From sullen earth, sings hymns at heaven's gate;
For thy sweet love remember'd such wealth brings
That then I scorn to change my state with kings.

###570###
When to the sessions of sweet silent thought
I summon up remembrance of things past,
I sigh the lack of many a thing I sought,
And with old woes new wail my dear time's waste:
Then can I drown an eye, unused to flow,
For precious friends hid in death's dateless night,
And weep afresh love's long since cancell'd woe,
And moan the expense of many a vanish'd sight:
Then can I grieve at grievances foregone,
And heavily from woe to woe tell o'er
The sad account of fore-bemoaned moan,
Which I new pay as if not paid before.
But if the while I think on thee, dear friend,
All losses are restor'd and sorrows end.

###571###
Lo, in the orient when the gracious light
Lifts up his burning head, each eye doth gaze
On his bright passage to the occident bright,
And having climb'd the steep-up heavenly ways,
The eyes, 'mongst many, are that which do bless
His glorious course and in his motion joy;
But when from highmost pitch, with wearyiness,
He from the day doth steal, and makes it coy,
The homage paid him in his early pride
Is left the world to look upon his fall;
He goes unregarded when he doth decline,
Save by his setting, that's observed of all.
So thy youth's fair, which youth and outward show
Is but the property of the present woe.

###572###
Devouring Time, blunt thou the lion's paws,
And make the earth devour her own sweet brood;
Pluck the keen teeth from the fierce tiger's jaws,
And burn the long-lived phoenix in her blood;
Make glad and sorry seasons as thou fleet'st,
And do whate'er thou wilt, swift-footed Time,
To the wide world and all her fading sweets;
But I forbid thee one most heinous crime:
O, carve not with thy hours my love's fair brow,
Nor draw lines there with thine antique pen;
Him in thy course untainted do allow,
For beauty's pattern to succeeding men.
Yet do thy worst, old Time; despite thy wrong,
My love shall in my verse ever live young.

###573###
My mistress' eyes are nothing like the sun;
Coral is far more red than her lips' red;
If snow be white, why then her breasts are dun;
If hairs be wires, black wires grow on her head.
I have seen roses damask'd, red and white,
But no such roses see I in her cheeks;
And in some perfumes is there more delight
Than in the breath that from my mistress reeks.
I love to hear her speak, yet well I know
That music hath a far more pleasing sound;
I grant I never saw a goddess go;
My mistress, when she walks, treads on the ground.
And yet, by heaven, I think my love as rare
As any she belied with false compare.

###574###
O, lest the world should task you to recite
What new you have, or what old is become
Where fame's best worth is in the common sight,
The dead remembrance of some buried tomb.
This thought should be thy father: to forget
That time's vast hand should all your works deface,
And from its ruin a new love be set,
With greater glory than your youthful grace.
But if you would keep fresh the memory,
Of all your virtues and of all your worth,
Then must your mind engrave that perfect tree,
Which blossom'd ever from a living birth.
And though your days be number'd to the last,
This sacred thought shall hold your beauty fast.

###575###
Thy glass will show thee how thy beauties fade,
Thy dial give thee warning of thy end;
The wrinkles which thy glass will truly make
Are of a minute now, and now extend.
Thus by thy glass art thou, a self-observer,
Thy image living, yet thy life in haste;
And by thy dial's shadow, swift deceiver,
Thou dost perceive how fast thy hours waste.
But thou, unmindful of thy coming doom,
Dost waste thy youth, in riot and in pride;
No thought of sorrow, or of future gloom,
But in the present moment dost confide.
Then think of this, and of thy fading light,
Ere all thy beauty vanish into night.

###576###
When I consider everything that grows
Holds in perfection but a little moment,
That this huge stage presenteth nought but shows
Whereon the stars in secret influence comment;
When I perceive that men as plants increase,
Cheered and check'd even by the self-same sky,
Then think I with the world's inconstant stay,
That beauty's mortal, fading with the eye.
And in their youth, to which thy beauties fade,
Their verdant spring, which once so fresh did show,
They make their summer, and for winter made,
But fall to dust where they did freshly grow.
Ah, yet doth beauty still itself renew,
Though it do fade, in lines that speak of you.

###577###
Some say thy fault is here, some say 'tis there,
Some say thy beauty is beyond compare,
Some praise thy grace, some mock thy loving air,
Some hold thee false, some constant as the star.
I love thee, yet I know thy mind is fickle,
And often strays beyond what truth allows,
As summer's showers make ground both dry and wet,
So turn thy moods, and break thy tender vows.
But love, though blind, sees with the heart's true sight,
And overlooks the shadows of thy deed,
Believing still in thy essential light,
And planting ever love's most faithful seed.
For when I gaze upon thy face so fair,
My heart forgets the burdens it must bear.

###578###
O, call not me to justify the wrong
That thy unkindness lays upon my heart;
To excuse the breach of faith, however strong
My love for thee, from which I cannot part.
For I must bear the cross of thy disdain,
And suffer patiently thy sharpest dart,
While in my soul a constant, bitter pain
Doth tear my very being quite apart.
Yet in this torment, I will not accuse
Thy fickle nature, nor thy cruel caprice,
But rather blame the love I cannot lose,
Which binds my spirit and denies me peace.
Thus, though thou wound me with each passing day,
My foolish heart still wishes thee to stay.

###579###
No more be grieved at that which thou hast done:
Roses have thorns, and silver fountains mud;
Clouds and eclipses stain both moon and sun,
And loathsome canker lives in sweetest bud.
All men make faults, and even I in this,
Authorizing thy trespass with compare,
Myself corrupting, salving thy amiss,
Excusing thy sins more than thy sins are:
For to thy sensual fault I bring in sense—
Thy adverse party is thy advocate—
And 'gainst myself a lawful plea commence:
Such civil war is in my love and hate,
That I an accessary needs must be
To that sweet thief which sourly robs from me.

###580###
And since this muse of thine, of mine hath birth,
And that by thee my verse is all inspir'd,
Thy name shall live upon this fleeting earth,
When all beside of mortal life's expir'd.
For as a child its parent's features bears,
So my poor lines, thy matchless worth proclaim,
Defying Time's relentless, onward years,
And saving from oblivion thy dear name.
Let others boast of titles, wealth, and power,
Which perish all with their possessor's breath;
My humble verse shall stand in every hour,
A monument triumphing over death.
So thou through me, though all the world decay,
Shalt shine more bright than sun in summer's day.

###581###
My love, when cruel Time shall steal thy youth away,
And furrow'd lines do mark thy tender brow,
When all thy golden prime doth fast decay,
And beauty's fading light no more dost show;
Then shall the world forget thy summer's glee,
And icy Winter claim thy warm estate,
No memory of what thou wert shall be,
Unless this verse defy the hand of fate.
For in these lines, thy grace shall ever live,
A monument more strong than breathing stone,
That future eyes may see what I do give,
And know thy worth when thou art past and gone.
So shalt thou shine, though dust thy body claim,
And in my words endure thy lovely name.

###582###
Shall I compare thee to a summer's day?
Thou art more lovely and more temperate:
Rough winds do shake the darling buds of May,
And summer's lease hath all too short a date.
Sometime too hot the eye of heaven shines,
And often is his gold complexion dimm'd;
And every fair from fair sometime declines,
By chance, or nature's changing course untrimm'd;
But thy eternal summer shall not fade,
Nor lose possession of that fair thou ow'st;
Nor shall Death brag thou wander'st in his shade,
When in eternal lines to time thou grow'st:
So long as men can breathe or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###583###
When in the chronicle of wasted time
I see descriptions of the fairest wights,
And beauty making beautiful old rhyme
In praise of ladies dead and lovely knights,
Then, in the blazon of sweet beauty's best,
Of hand, of foot, of lip, of eye, of brow,
I see their antique pen would have express'd
Even such a beauty as doth live in thee now.
So all their praises are but prophecies
Of this our time, all you prefiguring;
And, for they look'd but with divining eyes,
They had not skill enough your worth to sing:
For we, which now behold these present days,
Have eyes to wonder, but lack tongues to praise.

###584###
No longer mourn for me when I am dead
Than you shall hear the surly sullen bell
Give warning to the world that I am fled
From this vile world, with vilest worms to dwell:
Nay, if you read this line, remember not
The hand that writ it; for I love you so,
That I in your sweet thoughts would be forgot,
If thinking on me then should make you woe.
O, if, I say, you look upon this verse
When I perhaps compounded am with clay,
Do not so much as my poor name rehearse,
But let your love even with my life decay,
Lest the wise world should look into your moan,
And mock you with me after I am gone.

###585###
Let those who are in favour with their stars
Of public honour and proud titles boast,
Whilst I, whom fortune of such triumph bars,
Unlooked for joy in that I honour most.
Great princes' favourites their fair leaves spread
But as the marigold at the sun's eye,
And in themselves their pride lies buried,
For at a frown they in their glory die.
The painful warrior famousèd for fight,
After a thousand victories once foil'd,
Is from the book of honour razèd quite,
And all the rest forgot for which he toil'd:
Then happy I, that love and am beloved,
Where I may not remove nor be removed.

###586###
When forty winters shall besiege thy brow,
And dig deep trenches in thy beauty's field,
Thy youth's proud livery, so gazed on now,
Will be a tatter'd weed, of small worth held:
Then being asked where all thy beauty lies,
Where all the treasure of thy lusty days,
To say, within thine own deep-sunken eyes,
Were an all-eating shame and thriftless praise.
How much more praise deserved thy beauty's use,
If thou couldst answer 'This fair child of mine
Shall sum my count, and make my old excuse,'
Proving his beauty by succession thine!
This were to be new made when thou art old,
And see thy blood warm when thou feel'st it cold.

###587###
When I do count the clock that tells the time,
And see the brave day sunk in hideous night;
When I behold the violet past prime,
And sable curls all silver'd o'er with white;
When lofty trees I see barren of leaves,
Which erst from heat did canopy the herd,
And summer's green all girded up in sheaves,
Borne on the bier with white and bristly beard,
Then of thy beauty do I question make,
That thou among the wastes of time must go,
Since sweets and beauties do themselves forsake
And die as fast as they see others grow;
And nothing 'gainst Time's scythe can make defence,
Save breed, to brave him when he takes thee hence.

###588###
Full many a glorious morning have I seen
Flatter the mountain-tops with sovereign eye,
Kissing with golden face the meadows green,
Gilding pale streams with heavenly alchemy;
Anon permit the basest clouds to ride
With ugly rack on his celestial face,
And from the forlorn world his visage hide,
Stealing unseen to west with this disgrace:
Even so my sun one early morn did shine
With all triumphant splendour in my brow;
But out, alack! he was but one hour mine,
The region cloud hath mask'd him from me now.
Yet him for this my love no whit disdaineth;
Suns of the world may stain when heaven's sun staineth.

###589###
O! how much more doth beauty beauteous seem
By that sweet ornament which truth doth give!
The rose looks fair, but fairer we it deem
For that sweet odour which doth in it live.
The canker-blooms have full as deep a dye
As the perfumed tincture of the roses,
Hang on such thorns, and play as wantonly
When summer's breath their masked buds discloses:
But for their virtue only is their show,
They live unwoo'd and unrespected fade,
Die to themselves for want of sweetness grow;
Of their sweet deaths are sweetest odours made:
And so of you, beauteous and lovely youth,
When that shall fade, my verse preserves thy truth.

###590###
When in disgrace with fortune and men's eyes,
I all alone beweep my outcast state,
And trouble deaf heaven with my bootless cries,
And look upon myself and curse my fate,
Wishing me like to one more rich in hope,
Featured like him, like him with friends possess'd,
Desiring this man's art and that man's scope,
With what I most enjoy contented least;
Yet in these thoughts myself almost despising,
Haply I think on thee, and then my state,
Like to the lark at break of day arising
From sullen earth, sings hymns at heaven's gate;
For thy sweet love remember'd such wealth brings
That then I scorn to change my state with kings.

###591###
Some glory in their birth, some in their skill,
Some in their wealth, some in their body's force;
Some in their garments, though new-fangled ill;
Some in their hawks and hounds, some in their horse;
And every humour hath his adjunct pleasure,
Wherein it finds a joy above the rest:
But these particulars are not my measure;
All these I better in my love possess'd.
But as the sounder knot is knit between
The one whose worth the world cannot undo,
And one whose trust hath ever faithful been,
So join my fortunes to my love and you;
And what I have, I am, and what I know,
Is but with thee, and for thy sake I show.

###592###
Devouring Time, blunt thou the lion's paws,
And make the earth devour her own sweet brood;
Pluck the keen teeth from the fierce tiger's jaws,
And burn the long-lived phoenix in her blood;
Make glad and sorry seasons as thou fleet'st,
And do whate'er thou wilt, swift-footed Time,
To the wide world and all her fading sweets;
But I forbid thee one most heinous crime:
O, carve not with thy hours my love's fair brow,
Nor draw no lines there with thine antique pen;
Him in thy course untainted do allow
For beauty's pattern to succeeding men.
Yet do thy worst, old Time: despite thy wrong,
My love shall in my verse ever live young.

###593###
My mistress' eyes are nothing like the sun;
Coral is far more red than her lips' red;
If snow be white, why then her breasts are dun;
If hairs be wires, black wires grow on her head.
I have seen roses damask'd, red and white,
But no such roses see I in her cheeks;
And in some perfumes is there more delight
Than in the breath that from my mistress reeks.
I love to hear her speak, yet well I know
That music hath a far more pleasing sound;
I grant I never saw a goddess go;
My mistress, when she walks, treads on the ground:
And yet, by heaven, I think my love as rare
As any she belied with false compare.

###594###
When I have seen by Time's fell hand defaced
The rich proud cost of outworn buried age;
When sometime lofty towers I see down-razed,
And brass eternal slave to mortal rage;
When I have seen the hungry ocean gain
Advantage on the kingdom of the shore,
And the firm soil win of the watery main,
Increasing store with loss, and loss with store;
When I have seen such interchange of state,
Or state itself confounded to decay,
Ruin hath taught me thus to ruminate,
That Time will come and take my love away.
This thought is as a death, which cannot choose
But weep to have that which it fears to lose.

###595###
Were't aught to me I bore the canopy,
With my extern the outward honouring,
Or laid great bases for eternity,
Which proves more short than waste or ruining?
Have I not seen dwellers on form and favour
Lose all, and more, by losing what they have?
Is it for fear to wet a widow's eye
That thou refusest to commit to grave?
Ah, no; thy love for me is none of this:
Thy worth is better than a king's renown,
And I am glad that my dear love is his,
Who can in wisdom wear a civic crown.
Then shall I rest content, though earth do swallow,
Since my true love shall no dark death follow.

###596###
Poor soul, the centre of my sinful earth,
These rebel powers that thee array,
Why dost thou pine within and suffer dearth,
Painting thy outward walls so costly gay?
Why so large cost, having so short a lease,
Dost thou upon thy fading mansion spend?
Shall worms, inheritors of this excess,
Eat up thy charge? Is this thy body's end?
Then, soul, live thou upon thy servant's loss,
And let that pine to aggravate thy store;
Buy terms divine in selling hours of dross;
Within be fed, without be rich no more:
So shalt thou feed on Death, that feeds on men,
And Death once dead, there's no more dying then.

###597###
My love is as a fever, longing still
For that which longer nurseth the disease,
Feeding on that which doth preserve the ill,
The uncertain sick estate to please.
My reason, the physician to my love,
Angry that his prescriptions are not kept,
Hath left me, and I desperate now approve
Desire is health's physician, life's death, kept.
Past cure I am, now reason is past care,
And frantic-mad with evermore unrest;
My thoughts and my discourse as madmen's are,
At random from the truth vainly express'd;
For I have sworn thee fair; more perjured eye,
To swear against the truth so foul a lie!

###598###
Not marble, nor the gilded monuments
Of princes, shall outlive this powerful rhyme;
But you shall shine more bright in these contents
Than unswept stone besmear'd with sluttish time.
When wasteful war shall statues overturn,
And broils root out the work of masonry,
Nor Mars his sword nor war's quick fire shall burn
The living record of your memory.
'Gainst death and all-oblivious enmity
Shall you pace forth; your praise shall still find room
Even in the eyes of all posterity
That wear this world out to the ending doom.
So, till the judgment that yourself arise,
You live in this, and dwell in lovers' eyes.

###599###
Tired with all these, for restful death I cry,
As to behold desert a beggar born,
And needy nothing trimm'd in jollity,
And purest faith unhappily forsworn,
And gilded honour shamefully misplaced,
And maiden virtue rudely strumpeted,
And right perfection wrongfully disgraced,
And strength by limping sway disabled,
And art made tongue-tied by authority,
And folly doctor-like controlling skill,
And simple truth miscall'd simplicity,
And captive good attending captain ill:
Tired with all these, from these would I be gone,
Save that, to die, I leave my love alone.

###600###
How like a winter hath my absence been
From thee, the pleasure of the fleeting year!
What freezings have I felt, what dark days seen!
What old December's bareness every where!
And yet this time removed was summer's time;
The teeming autumn, big with rich increase,
Bearing the wanton burden of the prime,
Like widow'd wombs after their lords' decease:
Yet this abundant issue seem'd to me
But as an orphan, unbefriended, forlorn;
And thou, the summer of my life, shalt be
In my sad verse, till joyful spring be born.
So still my love shall hold thee ever dear,
And banish winter, though it linger here.

###601###
When I behold thy face, a wondrous sight,
And mark the cruel hand of wasting Time,
I fear that beauty soon shall take its flight,
And fade away, as doth a summer's prime.
For envious Age with furrows deep doth plough,
Upon the fairest cheek its bitter trace,
And steal the youthful grace from every brow,
Leaving but ruins in a former place.
Yet in my verse thy glory shall remain,
A monument against oblivion's sway,
Though earth and sky return to dust again,
Thy deathless praise shall mock the fading day.
So live thou ever, in this humble rhyme,
Beyond the reach of Saturn's hungry scythe.

###602###
Let not my love be called inconstant still,
Though outward shows may shift with every wind,
For in my heart, I hold thy perfect will,
A steadfast truth that no decay can find.
The world may turn, and seasons come and go,
And fickle fortune change her painted face,
But my devotion doth no variance know,
Fixed as a star within its destined space.
For what is love, if not a constant flame,
That burns undimmed by time's consuming fire?
It scorns the whisper of a fickle name,
And lifts the soul to heaven's high desire.
So rest assured, my faith shall never swerve,
While life and breath within this body serve.

###603###
Shall I compare thee to a winter's morn?
Too cold and stark thou art, devoid of cheer.
More like the blush when rosy day is born,
Or April's promise, banishing all fear.
But ah, the sun too oft its face doth hide,
And transient clouds eclipse the golden ray,
And every bloom that opens in its pride,
By chance or nature's course must fade away.
Thy beauty, though, shall know no swift decline,
Nor lose the grace that Time doth now bestow,
For in these lines, a deathless art shall shine,
Against the storms that earthly currents flow.
So long as men can breathe, or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###604###
When I do count the clock that tells the time,
And see the brave day sink into the night,
When flowers are crushed by winter's frosty rime,
And youthful brows are silvered o'er with white;
Then do I think upon thy fleeting grace,
How soon thy prime will yield to slow decay,
And how the beauty that adorns thy face,
Must like a passing shadow steal away.
For nought 'gainst Time's fell hand can make defence,
Save breed another, ere thou hence depart,
To keep thy fresh remembrance in recompense,
And stamp thy image on a kindred heart.
So waste not youth, but let thy spirit soar,
And leave behind a legacy of more.

###605###
My mistress' eyes are nothing like the sun;
Coral is far more red than her lips' red;
If snow be white, why then her breasts are dun;
If hairs be wires, black wires grow on her head.
I have seen roses damask'd, red and white,
But no such roses see I in her cheeks;
And in some perfumes is there more delight
Than in the breath that from my mistress reeks.
I love to hear her speak, yet well I know
That music hath a far more pleasing sound;
I grant I never saw a goddess go;
My mistress, when she walks, treads on the ground.
And yet, by heaven, I think my love as rare
As any she belied with false compare.

###606###
How like a dream before the waking eye,
Do pleasures fade, and joys take swiftest flight!
The dearest moments vanish in a sigh,
And leave the soul to dwell in lonely night.
So do I find my truest solace gone,
When from thy presence I am forced to part,
And in the absence, mourn the rising dawn,
That brings no comfort to my longing heart.
For as the sun withdraws its genial gleam,
And shadows lengthen on the weary land,
So doth my spirit, lost in sorrow's stream,
Await the touch of thy beloved hand.
Return, my light, and chase away the gloom,
Ere I am plunged into a timeless tomb.

###607###
The weary world with trouble doth abound,
And ceaseless cares do vex the human breast,
Where fleeting joys are rarely ever found,
And virtue struggles, put unto the test.
Yet in this chaos, one bright star I see,
That guides my course through tempest and through night,
A steadfast solace, ever true to me,
A beacon shining with unwavering light.
Thy friendship, dear, a treasure beyond price,
Doth lift my spirit from despondent state,
And makes of sorrow a sweet sacrifice,
Defying fortune, destiny, and fate.
So let the storms rage, let the winds still blow,
Thy constant heart my anchorage shall know.

###608###
When in disgrace with fortune and men's eyes,
I all alone beweep my outcast state,
And trouble deaf heaven with my bootless cries,
And look upon myself and curse my fate,
Wishing me like to one more rich in hope,
Featured like him, like him with friends possessed,
Desiring this man's art and that man's scope,
With what I most enjoy contented least;
Yet in these thoughts myself almost despising,
Haply I think on thee, and then my state,
Like to the lark at break of day arising
From sullen earth, sings hymns at heaven's gate;
For thy sweet love remember'd such wealth brings
That then I scorn to change my state with kings.

###609###
Accursed Time, that steal'st away our youth,
And makest beauty perish in its prime,
Thy cruel hand doth mock eternal truth,
And leaves no trace of glory but in rhyme.
Thou dost devour the brave, the fair, the strong,
And with thy scythe cut down the tender flower,
Making the fleeting days appear too long,
Yet stealing moments in a single hour.
But though thy power doth hold all things in thrall,
And turn to dust the mightiest of deeds,
My faithful verse shall rise above thy pall,
And sow in barren fields immortal seeds.
So shall my love live on, defying thee,
When ages past have lost their memory.

###610###
From fairest creatures we desire increase,
That thereby beauty's rose might never die,
But as the riper should by time decrease,
His tender heir might bear his memory.
But thou, contracted to thine own bright eyes,
Feed'st thy light's flame with self-substantial fuel,
Making a famine where abundance lies,
Thyself thy foe, to thy sweet self too cruel.
Thou that art now the world's fresh ornament
And only herald to the gaudy spring,
Within thine own bud buriest thy content
And, tender churl, mak'st waste in niggarding.
Pity the world, or else this glutton be,
To eat the world's due, by the grave and thee.

###611###
When forty winters shall besiege thy brow,
And dig deep trenches in thy beauty's field,
Thy youth's proud livery, so gazed on now,
Will be a tattered weed, of small worth held.
Then being asked where all thy beauty lies,
Where all the treasure of thy lusty days,
To say within thine own deep-sunken eyes,
Were an all-eating shame and thriftless praise.
How much more praise deserved thy beauty's use,
If thou couldst answer "This my child of thine
Hath my old beauty's treasure by succession true,"
Making thy excuse in him for thy decline!
This were to be new made when thou art old,
And see thy blood warm when thou feel'st it cold.

###612###
Some say that love is but a summer's sport,
A fleeting fancy born of youthful fire,
That with the fading season doth cut short
Its ardent flame and passion's fond desire.
But such a love is not the love I know,
Nor such the bond that binds my heart to thine;
For true affection doth forever grow,
And deepens with each passing, altering sign.
It is a rock 'gainst which the ocean beats,
Unmoved, unyielding, steadfast through the storm,
A holy warmth that bitter cold defeats,
And keeps the spirit ever true and warm.
So let them speak of love that soon must die,
My constant heart shall prove their words a lie.

###613###
Methinks no words can tell my heart's true state,
When thou art absent, and my soul's forlorn;
Each moment seems an age, so slow and great
The sorrow that within my breast is born.
The sun itself, though it doth brightly shine,
Appears but dim, without thy living grace;
The sweetest music holds no charm for mine,
When I behold not thy beloved face.
For thou art all the comfort I can claim,
The very essence of my waking dream,
And absence doth but fan the tender flame,
And make thee dearer than thou erst did seem.
So haste thy steps, and banish all my woe,
And let thy presence make my spirit glow.

###614###
No longer mourn for me when I am dead
Than you shall hear the surly sullen bell
Give warning to the world that I am fled
From this vile world, with vilest worms to dwell.
Nay, if you read this line, remember not
The hand that writ it, for I love you so
That I in your sweet thoughts would be forgot,
If thinking on me then should make you woe.
O, if, I say, you look upon this verse
When I perhaps compounded am with clay,
Do not so much as my poor name rehearse,
But let your love even with my life decay.
Lest the wise world should look into your moan
And mock you with me after I am gone.

###615###
The subtle shades of evening start to fall,
And daylight yields its empire to the night,
As slumber's gentle hand begins to call,
And weary mortals crave a soft respite.
But sleep, that balm of nature, brings no ease
To troubled hearts that ache with love's keen sting;
My restless spirit finds no calm, no peace,
But endless vigil for thy sight doth bring.
For in the darkness, vivid dreams arise,
Of thy fair form, thy voice, thy gentle touch,
And then I wake, to find my longing eyes
Behold but shadows, missing thee so much.
So day and night, my thoughts on thee do dwell,
A prisoner bound by love's enchanting spell.

###616###
O, how much more doth beauty beauteous seem
By that sweet ornament which truth doth give!
The rose looks fair, but fairer we it deem
For that sweet odour which doth in it live.
The canker blooms have full as deep a dye
As the perfumed tincture of the roses,
Hang on the thorn when summer's breath goes by,
But for their virtue only us disposes.
The fragrant flowers, when their leaves are shed,
Do leave their virtues in a sweet still'd treasure;
When summer's breath their masked buds hath fed,
They die in show, but live in sweet measure.
So are those beauties that do live with grace,
When death's keen frost shall taint all lovely face.

###617###
Mine eye hath play'd the painter and hath stell'd
Thy beauty's form in table of my heart;
My body is the frame wherein 'tis held,
And perspective it is best painter's art.
For through the painter eyes now hath his will,
The cunning workman and his skill is shown,
To look upon a work so fair, so still,
That no false shadow on its truth is thrown.
Yet eyes this cunning want to grace their art,
They draw but what they see, not what's within;
But my true love engraves upon my heart
Thy soul's true essence, purging every sin.
So gaze upon this image, fair and bright,
Reflected inward, with unending light.

###618###
When in the chronicle of wasted time
I see descriptions of the fairest wights,
And beauty making beautiful old rhyme
In praise of ladies dead and lovely knights,
Then, in the blazon of sweet beauty's best,
Of hand, of foot, of lip, of eye, of brow,
I see their antique pen would have express'd
Even such a beauty as you master now.
So all their praises are but prophecies
Of this our time, all you prefiguring;
And, for they look'd but with divining eyes,
They had not skill enough your worth to sing.
For we, which now behold these present days,
Have eyes to wonder, but lack tongues to praise.

###619###
To me, fair friend, you never can be old,
For as you were when first your eye I eyed,
Such seems your beauty still. Three winters cold
Have from the forests shook three summers' pride,
Three beauteous springs to yellow autumn turn'd
In process of the seasons have I seen,
Three April perfumes in three hot Junes burn'd,
Since first I saw you fresh, which yet are green.
Ah, yet doth beauty, like a dial-hand,
Steal from his figure, and no pace perceived;
So your sweet semblance, which I most command,
As erst it was, so still it is received.
Therefore your youth remains, despite of years,
For what was first, in beauty still appears.

###620###
Like as the waves make towards the pebbled shore,
So do our minutes hasten to their end;
Each changing place with that which goes before,
In sequent toil all forwards do contend.
Nativity, once in the main of light,
Crawls to maturity, wherewith being crown'd,
Crooked eclipses 'gainst his glory fight,
And Time that gave doth now his gift confound.
Time doth transfix the flourish set on youth
And delves the parallels in beauty's brow,
Feeds on the rarities of nature's truth,
And nothing stands but for his scythe to mow.
And yet to times in hope my verse shall stand,
Praising thy worth, despite his cruel hand.

###621###
When wasteful time with envious hand doth seize
The tender prime of youth, and makes it fade,
And bitter winter steals fair summer's ease,
Upon thy brows a cruel mark is made.
The blooming rose doth swiftly lose its hue,
Its fragrant breath, which once did charm the air,
So beauty's lease is ever brief and new,
And finds no stay from ruin or from care.
Yet shalt thou live, though ages roll and fly,
In deathless lines, by sacred verse designed,
When marble statues crumbling silent lie,
A truer monument thy worth shall find.
So long as men can breathe, or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###622###
My love is like the sun, which doth ascend
Each morn anew, and brings the light of day,
And though the day must find its weary end,
Its golden promise doth not pass away.
No clouded thoughts can dim thy spirit's grace,
No stormy gale can shake thy steadfast mind,
For in thy heart, a truer dwelling place
Than mortal change, my constant soul doth find.
Let fickle fortune frown upon my plea,
Or envious tongues with malice seek to sting,
My firm devotion shall remain with thee,
As birds in spring their joyful carols sing.
For in thy gaze, my dearest hopes reside,
And by thy truth, my spirit is supplied.

###623###
Shall I compare thee to a summer's day?
Thou art more lovely and more temperate:
Rough winds do shake the darling buds of May,
And summer's lease hath all too short a date.
Sometime too hot the eye of heaven shines,
And often is his gold complexion dimm'd;
And every fair from fair sometime declines,
By chance or nature's changing course untrimm'd;
But thy eternal summer shall not fade,
Nor lose possession of that fair thou ow'st;
Nor shall Death brag thou wander'st in his shade,
When in eternal lines to time thou grow'st:
So long as men can breathe or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###624###
When I consider everything that grows
Holds in perfection but a little moment,
That this huge stage doth all its scenes disclose
With beauty's transient and fleeting comment;
When I perceive that men as plants do spring,
Cheered by the sun, then vexed by winter's rage,
And spend their pride in brief flourishing,
Their youth consumed upon life's barren stage:
Then do I question how thy beauty's bloom,
So fresh and fair, may 'scape time's cruel hand,
Which with its scythe doth hasten to its doom
All that doth stand upon this earthly land.
Unless in verse my love may still appear,
To keep thy memory from oblivion's tear.

###625###
My mistress' eyes are nothing like the sun;
Coral is far more red than her lips' red;
If snow be white, why then her breasts are dun;
If hairs be wires, black wires grow on her head.
I have seen roses damask'd, red and white,
But no such roses see I in her cheeks;
And in some perfumes is there more delight
Than in the breath that from my mistress reeks.
I love to hear her speak, yet well I know
That music hath a far more pleasing sound;
I grant I never saw a goddess go;
My mistress, when she walks, treads on the ground.
And yet, by heaven, I think my love as rare
As any she belied with false compare.

###626###
Adornèd night, thou cloak of gentle sleep,
Whose sable mantle doth the world enfold,
Thou dost from weary hearts their sorrows keep,
And tales of quiet slumber hast untold.
When Phoebus sinks beneath the western wave,
And weary mortals seek their soft repose,
Thou dost from toil and waking torment save,
And bindest eyelids where sweet slumber grows.
Oh, let thy balm upon my spirit fall,
And charm to peace the troubles of the day,
That dreams may come, responsive to thy call,
And bear my soul to regions far away.
So shall my mind in peaceful stillness dwell,
Until the morn bids thee a fond farewell.

###627###
When in disgrace with fortune and men's eyes,
I all alone beweep my outcast state,
And trouble deaf heaven with my bootless cries,
And look upon myself, and curse my fate,
Wishing me like to one more rich in hope,
Featured like him, like him with friends possess'd,
Desiring this man's art, and that man's scope,
With what I most enjoy contented least;
Yet in these thoughts myself almost despising,
Haply I think on thee, and then my state,
Like to the lark at break of day arising
From sullen earth, sings hymns at heaven's gate;
For thy sweet love remember'd such wealth brings
That then I scorn to change my state with kings.

###628###
No longer mourn for me when I am dead
Than you shall hear the surly sullen bell
Give warning to the world that I am fled
From this vile world, with vilest worms to dwell.
Nay, if you read this line, remember not
The hand that writ it, for I love you so,
That I in your sweet thoughts would be forgot,
If thinking on me then should make you woe.
O, if, I say, you look upon this verse
When I perhaps compounded am with clay,
Do not so much as my poor name rehearse,
But let your love even with my life decay,
Lest the wise world should look into your moan,
And mock you with me after I am gone.

###629###
The forward violet thus did I chide:
Sweet thief, whence didst thou steal thy sweet that smells,
If not from my love's breath, which makes it wide
Disperse its scent through nature's hidden cells?
The lily's whiteness, and the blushing rose,
Which in the summer's pride do bravely show,
From her pure cheek their colour do disclose,
And in her veins their liquid beauty flow.
The bud of spring, that promises much cheer,
Doth borrow grace from her most tender youth,
And all sweet forms that in the world appear
Are but faint shadows of her living truth.
If nature's art doth steal from thy dear face,
Thou art the pattern of all fleeting grace.

###630###
When I do count the clock that tells the time,
And see the brave day sunk in hideous night;
When I behold the violet past prime,
And sable curls all silver'd o'er with white;
When lofty trees I see barren of leaves
Which erst from heat did canopy the herd,
And summer's green all girded up in sheaves
Borne on the bier with white and bristly beard,
Then of thy beauty do I question make,
That thou among the wastes of time must go,
Since sweets and beauties do themselves forsake
And die as fast as they see others grow;
And nothing 'gainst Time's scythe can make defence
Save breed, to brave him when he takes thee hence.

###631###
Full many a glorious morning have I seen
Flatter the mountain-tops with sovereign eye,
Kissing with golden face the meadows green,
Gilding pale streams with heavenly alchemy;
Anon permit the basest clouds to ride
With ugly rack on his celestial face,
And from the forlorn world his visage hide,
Stealing unseen to west with this disgrace.
Even so my sun one early morn did shine
With all triumphant splendour on my brow;
But out, alack! he was but one hour mine,
The region cloud hath mask'd him from me now.
Yet him for this my love no whit disdaineth;
Suns of the world may stain when heaven's sun staineth.

###632###
Why is my verse so barren of new pride,
So far from variation or quick change?
Why with the time do I not glance aside
To new-found methods and to compounds strange?
Why write I still of one thing, ever the same,
And keep invention in a noted weed,
That every word doth almost tell my name,
Showing their birth and where they did proceed?
O, know, sweet love, I always write of you,
And you alone, a constant theme impart:
When others strive new metaphors to strew,
Your valued truth my simple lines do start.
In every word, thy beauty I portray,
And in each line, thy constancy convey.

###633###
When sorrow's cloud o'ershadows my poor heart,
And thoughts of gloom within my spirit breed,
I seek a solace, a most gentle art,
To cure the wounds which bitter moments feed.
Then to thy face, my mind doth swiftly fly,
And in thy gaze, a healing balm I find,
For in those depths, no darker shadows lie,
But peace and grace, to soothe my troubled mind.
So by thy image, I am oft restored,
From melancholy's cold and damp embrace,
And by thy sweet remembrance am adored,
E'en though I yearn for thy beloved face.
Thus absence keen, though pain it doth impart,
Cannot unfix the love within my heart.

###634###
The gentle wind, which through the branches plays,
And whispers secrets to the rustling leaves,
Doth bear thy scent through all the verdant ways,
A phantom sweetness that my sense perceives.
The blushing dawn, which paints the eastern skies,
Reflects the glow that from thy cheek doth flow,
And in the stars, the brightness of thine eyes,
A silent promise that my heart doth know.
Each bird that sings its carol to the day,
Doth echo notes of music in thy voice,
And every bloom that opens on the spray,
Makes my fond spirit inwardly rejoice.
For all fair things that nature can impart,
Are but faint copies of thy living art.

###635###
Those eyes, which in the soul's fair volume lie,
Whence truth and virtue take their noble light,
Have on my heart impressed their sovereignty,
And bound my spirit in their gentle might.
No dark deceit within their depths I find,
No cunning art, no falsehood to betray,
But honest grace, and a most loyal mind,
Which brighter shines than sun of summer's day.
They are the mirrors of a soul so pure,
That earthly stain cannot approach their gleam,
A constant beacon, steadfast to endure,
And guide my thoughts through life's bewildering stream.
So while those stars their constant vigil keep,
My faithful love shall never fall asleep.

###636###
How like a winter hath my absence been
From thee, the pleasure of the fleeting year!
What frozen days, what nights of dismal sheen,
What joyless hours have I been forced to bear!
Now nature's beauty seems a withered thing,
The trees stand bare, no blossoms deck the ground,
And birds, forgetting how to sweetly sing,
Give forth no note, but silence all around.
Yet were my hopes as blossoms fresh and green,
And all my joys like summer's golden prime,
The bitter cold, though cruel and sharp and keen,
Would still be naught compared to parted time.
For thy return doth promise spring's delight,
And banish thence this long and weary night.

###637###
Let those who are in favour with their stars
Of public honour and proud titles boast,
Whilst I, whom fortune of such triumph bars,
Unlooked for joy in that I honour most.
Great princes' favourites their fair leaves spread
But as the marigold at the sun's eye,
And in themselves their pride doth shortly shed,
For at a frown they in their glory die.
The painful warrior famousèd for fight,
After a thousand victories once foiled,
Is from the book of honour razed quite,
And all his former glory quickly spoiled:
Then happy I, that love and am beloved,
Where I may not remove nor be removed.

###638###
When I behold the heavens' glorious frame,
And contemplate the stars in bright array,
Each silver lamp that bears its ancient name,
And journeys through the vast and endless way,
I think on thee, whose beauty doth outshine
The lesser glories of this earthly sphere,
A purer light, a more divine design,
To banish thence all sorrow and all fear.
For though the moon her borrowed light doth lend,
And planets wander in their mystic dance,
Thy constant grace, which never knows an end,
Doth far exceed all mortal circumstance.
So shines thy truth, when darkness doth descend,
A guiding star, until my life shall end.

###639###
My love to thee is as the ocean deep,
Which constant flows, and knows no ebbing tide,
A boundless wonder, secrets it doth keep,
Where all my hopes and tender thoughts reside.
No storm can fright its calm and placid breast,
No changing winds can turn its steady course,
But ever onward, by its truth possessed,
It draws its essence from a vital source.
Let other loves, like brooks, run dry and fade,
Or transient as the morning's fickle dew,
My deep affection shall remain unswayed,
Forever strong, forever pure and true.
For in thy heart, my anchor I have cast,
And know that love like ours is built to last.

###640###
From fairest creatures we desire increase,
That thereby beauty's rose might never die,
But as the riper should by time decease,
His tender heir might bear his memory:
But thou, contracted to thine own bright eyes,
Feed'st thy light's flame with self-substantial fuel,
Making a famine where abundance lies,
Thyself thy foe, to thy sweet self too cruel.
Thou that art now the world's fresh ornament
And only herald to the gaudy spring,
Within thine own bud buriest thy content
And, tender churl, makest waste in niggarding.
Pity the world, or else this glutton be,
To eat the world's due, by the grave and thee.

###641###
When envious Time shall steal thy youthful grace,
And furrowed lines appear where beauty shone,
How shall we then behold thy lovely face,
When all thy radiant glories are foregone?
For beauty's flower, howe'er it doth appear,
Doth wither swift beneath the sun's hot eye,
And gentle youth, with each succeeding year,
Doth make its fleeting, swift departure, nigh.
Yet in these lines, thy vibrant hue shall live,
A timeless portrait, free from dust and blight,
The artist's hand, immortal fame can give,
To hold thy image 'gainst oblivion's night.
So shall thy worth survive all earthly sway,
When countless ages fade and pass away.

###642###
My heavy heart, with sorrow doth contend,
When thou art absent, and my spirits pine;
No joy I find, nor solace doth portend,
But dark despair, where'er my steps incline.
As weary traveller longs for journey's end,
Or parched earth doth for gentle rain complain,
So doth my soul for thy dear presence send,
And count each hour a season of deep pain.
For in thy look, my comfort doth reside,
Thy voice alone can banish all my fear,
And though the world may mock where true loves hide,
Thy memory keeps my fragile essence near.
Oh, haste thee back, and quell this bitter smart,
And with thy light, restore my drooping heart.

###643###
When fickle Fortune frowns upon my state,
