And bring sweet solace to my troubled breast.
So long as thou dost grace this mortal stage,
Thy worth shall fill my every written page.

###772###
When envious Fortune, with a scornful eye,
Doth cast her frown upon the hopeful soul,
And all the dreams that soared unto the sky,
Are brought to nought, beyond one's own control;
Then doth the spirit feel a chilling blight,
And question why such cruel decrees are made,
To raise to honour some with partial light,
Whilst others languish in unending shade.
The king in robes, the beggar by the way,
Are both alike beneath her changing sway,
And none can tell what dawn brings forth the day,
Or what ill turn may steal their joy away.
So little trust have I in worldly gain,
When fickle chance doth rule with bitter reign.

###773###
Lest Time's swift hand should steal thy youth away,
And leave no image of thy beauty's prime,
Thou art entreated, ere thy fading day,
To store thy graces, conquering harsh Time.
For if thou dost depart without an heir,
Thy loveliness shall vanish with thy breath,
And leave the world in darkness and despair,
A monument to beauty lost in death.
O, think what joy a child of thine could bring,
To bear thy semblance, and thy spirit's truth,
A living mirror, when thy days take wing,
A sweet reminder of thy golden youth.
So graft thyself, and leave a living copy,
Lest nature's art become a mere lost trophy.

###774###
When present joys, like shadows, swift do flee,
And fleeting pleasures mock our eager grasp,
Then turns my mind, dear love, to thoughts of thee,
And holds thy image in a loving clasp.
For memory, a treasure house divine,
Doth keep the records of each happy hour,
Wherein thy face with constant grace doth shine,
A fragrant bloom, defying winter's power.
No weary distance, nor the lapse of years,
Can blot thy features from my inner sight,
Thy gentle voice still whispers, and it cheers
My pensive soul through sorrow's darkest night.
So let the world their transient trifles chase,
My heart holds thine, in time's eternal space.

###775###
When I behold the mirror of my age,
And see the furrows Time hath deeply wrought,
I muse upon life's fleeting, transient stage,
And all the battles that my youth once fought.
The strength that flourished, like a vibrant tree,
Now bows its head beneath the wintry breeze,
The dreams that promised much, now flee from me,
Like scattered leaves upon autumnal seas.
Alas, where is the vigour and the fire,
The boundless hope that scorned all doubt and pain?
Consumed by Time's insatiable desire,
A fleeting moment, never to regain.
So doth my spirit mourn its fading prime,
And seek a refuge from relentless Time.

###776###
Mine eye doth stray, by outward show misled,
And takes delight in forms that quickly fade,
For worldly beauty, transient and dread,
Doth oft entrap the senses, unafraid.
But when I close my sight to things of earth,
And turn within, where truest virtues lie,
Then doth thy image find a second birth,
Reflecting graces that can never die.
No painted portrait, though it boast such skill,
Can show thy essence, or thy spirit's light,
For inner vision, strong and ever still,
Doth hold thy truth beyond the darkest night.
So let my gaze be clouded, dark, and dim,
My soul perceives thee, from the core within.

###777###
The restless soul, that doth for greatness strive,
And climbs ambition's ladder, day by day,
Doth find at last it cannot long survive,
But sees its fading triumphs steal away.
What boots it then, to grasp at gilded power,
To hold a sceptre, or to wear a crown?
If at the zenith of that glorious hour,
Death's cold decree shall cast all glory down.
The transient echoes of a lauded name,
Shall fade and whisper on the common breeze,
And monuments, erected for thy fame,
Shall crumble slow, ignored by passing trees.
So build no hopes on earth's uncertain stay,
For all must vanish, come a future day.

###778###
No flawless mirror nature doth display,
Though sun-kissed plains and verdant woods abound,
For shifting clouds obscure the brightest day,
And winter's blight doth chill the frozen ground.
The rose, so fair, that doth in beauty glow,
Is vex'd by thorns, and fades in sudden hour,
The strongest oak, that weathered storm and snow,
At last must yield to Time's consuming power.
Yet in thy spirit, I perceive a grace
More constant than the stars, more purely bright,
No changing season leaves its mournful trace,
But ever shines with an unblemish'd light.
So let all else succumb to swift decay,
Thy truth shall triumph, and endure for aye.

###779###
When falsehood's tongue doth weave a tangled snare,
And cunning whispers seek to stain thy name,
Let not thy noble spirit yield to care,
Nor let thy heart be troubled by their blame.
For though the shadows for a while hold sway,
And truth itself doth seem awhile conceal'd,
The dawning sun shall banish them away,
And all thy virtues shall be clear reveal'd.
No wicked stratagem, nor spiteful deed,
Can long suppress the light that dwells within,
But like a blossom from a hidden seed,
Thy stainless honour shall forever win.
So stand thou firm, and fear no foul repute,
For truth shall blossom, bearing golden fruit.

###780###
As fleeting moments on the swift stream glide,
And pass unseen, though full of tender grace,
So doth this present joy, whate'er betide,
Ere long depart, and leave no lasting trace.
The sun, that now with golden lustre shines,
Shall sink anon beneath the western sky,
And evening's shadows blur the sharpest lines,
As all things beautiful are born to die.
Yet in my mind, this hour shall find its rest,
A cherished jewel, held forever fast,
A secret treasure, singled from the blest,
Whose gentle memory shall ever last.
So farewell, fleeting joy, I hold thee dear,
Though thou must vanish, and no more appear.

###781###
When I behold the swift decay of time,
And how fair youth doth steal itself away,
Marking each hour with an unsparing crime,
That beauty's rose must wither and decay.
Then think I on thy grace, which now doth shine,
A fleeting glimpse, a treasure in the light,
How soon the wrinkles shall usurp that line,
And shadows steal the glory of thy sight.
Yet fear I not, though age doth claim its due,
For in my verse, thy spirit shall abide,
A timeless form, eternally made new,
Where death's dark hand can never thee betide.
So live thou ever, in this humble rhyme,
Beyond the ruin wrought by cruel Time.

###782###
From fairest creatures we desire increase,
That thereby beauty's rose might never die,
But as the riper should by time decrease,
His tender heir might bear his memory.
But thou, contracted to thine own bright eye,
Feed'st thy light's flame with self-substantial fuel,
Making a famine where abundance lies,
Thyself thy foe, to thy sweet self too cruel.
Pity the world, or else this greedy grave,
Which eats the world, might be thy widow's womb,
And leave the world no copy to engrave,
But that thy beauty with thyself consume.
O, be begot, that then, when thou art gone,
Thy youth's fair image may remain alone.

###783###
Mine eye hath play'd the painter and hath stell'd
Thy beauty's form in table of my heart,
My body is the frame wherein 'tis held,
And little room, but for thy perfect part.
Yet dost thou doubt the cunning of mine eye,
When it in thee a thousand graces finds?
But it is blinded when it looks on me,
And only true in what my heart reminds.
For through thy form my inner vision sees,
Beyond the outward show, thy soul's pure light,
And with a love that ever doth appease,
It holds thee dear in darkness as in bright.
So feast mine eye on thy most lovely grace,
While true my heart doth hold thy dearest place.

###784###
When I do count the clock that tells the time,
And see the brave day sunk in hideous night,
When I behold the violet past her prime,
And sable curls all silver'd o'er with white;
When lofty trees I see barren of leaves,
Which erst from heat did canopy the herd,
And summer's green all girded up in sheaves,
Borne on the bier with white and bristly beard;
Then of thy beauty do I question make,
That thou among the wastes of time must go,
Since sweets and beauties do themselves forsake,
And die as fast as they see others grow.
And nothing 'gainst Time's scythe can make defence,
Save breed, to brave him when he takes thee hence.

###785###
How heavy do I journey on the way,
When what I seek, my weary soul doth lack?
For then my thoughts, from thee afar astray,
Return unto my breast, a mournful pack.
My horse doth stop, forgetful of his pace,
As if the road were ended at its start,
And hangs his head, as lacking all his grace,
As knowing well the burden of my heart.
For as a lover, sick with his desire,
Doth lose all vigour, and his spirit fades,
So doth my love for thee my strength acquire,
And then doth leave me weak in distant glades.
No joy I find, though all the world be bright,
Unless thy presence bless my longing sight.

###786###
Thy lovely youth, that now adorns thy face,
Is but a summer, which must pass away,
And winter's frost shall steal each charming grace,
And turn to withered grey what bloom'd today.
Then shall thy heart, with sorrow deeply bound,
Lament the loss of what it held so dear,
As fields lie bare where golden grain was found,
And icy winds replace the balmy air.
But if thou plant'st thy beauty in a line,
A fair succession, springing from thy root,
Then shall thy grace with vital lustre shine,
And bear in fresh delight a gentle fruit.
So let not winter's gloom thy spring consume,
But leave behind thy youth a fragrant bloom.

###787###
If when I gaze upon thy countenance,
I find some blemish marring that fair show,
Or hear some whisper of thy fickle glance,
That tells of love that doth like breezes blow;
Think not my passion, rooted in my soul,
Will yield to doubt, or waver in its trust,
For though the world may try to take its toll,
My constant heart will turn thee into dust
Of pure devotion, which no time can stain,
No false report can tarnish with its lies,
For what I see within doth still remain,
A brighter truth than what meets mortal eyes.
So let them speak, their malice cannot move,
The steadfast pillar of my loyal love.

###788###
Why is my verse so barren of new pride,
So far from variation or quick change?
Why, having my love, do I not seek beside
New methods, for new fancies to arrange?
Say what you will, mine is a constant mind,
Not mov'd by every new-invented toy,
But with a settled purpose I am kind,
To that one subject which doth me employ.
You are my all, my theme, my very song,
The spring of thought whence all my numbers flow,
And when I write, I cannot do you wrong,
For all my best in you I ever show.
So if I always write of you and you,
It is because my love is ever true.

###789###
When thou shalt be disburthen'd of thy soul,
And all thy beauty turned to earth's own mould,
When time's swift river hath run to its goal,
And stories of thy youth are left untold;
Then shall this verse, in humble guise array'd,
Preserve the essence of thy living grace,
A monument by poets' hands well made,
To hold for ages thy remembered face.
For neither brass nor stone can bear so long
The touch of centuries, the harsh decay,
As this frail paper, fortified by song,
Shall bear thy fame through every future day.
So fear not death, nor time's consuming sway,
While in these lines thy beauty finds its stay.

###790###
My love is as a fever, longing still
For that which longer nurseth the disease,
Feeding on that which doth my life quite kill,
And racking me with more and more unrest, these
Hot fits of passion that my reason stain,
Do lead me blindfold to my soul's undoing;
My thoughts and senses are so full of pain,
That I do lose myself in love's pursuing.
Physicians have with their prescriptions tried
To cool this fire, but all their art is vain,
My malady will not be put aside,
Nor can my restless spirit peace attain.
For I am sick with love, and prove false my cure,
To say she's fair, when my sick eyes obscure.

###791###
The world's wide stage, whereon we play our part,
Doth show its scenes with swift and changing light,
A sudden joy, then sorrow's piercing dart,
And fading gleam of day gives way to night.
So do our moments, like the flying cloud,
Pass by unseen, ere we can hold them fast,
And life's brief drama, to the silent shroud,
Doth rush headlong, its fleeting beauty past.
Yet in this flux, where all things shift and move,
There is one constant star, fixed and serene,
The steadfast beacon of my loyal love,
A guiding light, though darkness intervene.
So let the world its changing shadows cast,
My love for thee shall hold thee to the last.

###792###
My mistress' eyes are nothing like the sun;
Coral is far more red than her lips' red;
If snow be white, why then her breasts are dun;
If hairs be wires, black wires grow on her head.
I have seen roses damask'd, red and white,
But no such roses see I in her cheeks;
And in some perfumes is there more delight
Than in the breath that from my mistress reeks.
I love to hear her speak, yet well I know
That music hath a far more pleasing sound;
I grant I never saw a goddess go;
My mistress, when she walks, treads on the ground.
And yet, by heaven, I think my love as rare
As any she belied with false compare.

###793###
When in disgrace with fortune and men's eyes,
I all alone beweep my outcast state,
And trouble deaf heaven with my bootless cries,
And look upon myself, and curse my fate,
Wishing me like to one more rich in hope,
Featur'd like him, with friends possess'd of skill,
Desiring this man's art and that man's scope,
With what I most enjoy contented least;
Yet in these thoughts myself almost despising,
Haply I think on thee, and then my state,
Like to the lark at break of day arising
From sullen earth, sings hymns at heaven's gate;
For thy sweet love remember'd such wealth brings
That then I scorn to change my state with kings.

###794###
Not marble, nor the gilded monuments
Of princes, shall outlive this powerful rhyme;
But you shall shine more bright in these contents
Than unswept stone, besmear'd with sluttish time.
When wasteful war shall statues overturn,
And broils root out the work of masonry,
Nor Mars his sword nor war's quick fire shall burn
The living record of your memory.
'Gainst death and all-oblivious enmity
Shall you pace forth; your praise shall still find room
Even in the eyes of all posterity
That wear this world out to the ending doom.
So till the judgment that yourself arise,
You live in this, and dwell in lovers' eyes.

###795###
The summer's lease hath all too short a date:
Sometime too hot the eye of heaven shines,
And often is his gold complexion dimm'd;
And every fair from fair sometime declines,
By chance or nature's changing course untrimm'd;
But thy eternal summer shall not fade,
Nor lose possession of that fair thou owest;
Nor shall Death brag thou wander'st in his shade,
When in eternal lines to time thou growest:
So long as men can breathe or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.
Shall I compare thee to a summer's day?
Thou art more lovely and more temperate:
Rough winds do shake the darling buds of May,

###796###
Let me not to the marriage of true minds
Admit impediments. Love is not love
Which alters when it alteration finds,
Or bends with the remover to remove:
O no! it is an ever-fixed mark
That looks on tempests and is never shaken;
It is the star to every wandering bark,
Whose worth's unknown, although his height be taken.
Love's not Time's fool, though rosy lips and cheeks
Within his bending sickle's compass come;
Love alters not with his brief hours and weeks,
But bears it out even to the edge of doom.
If this be error and upon me proved,
I never writ, nor no man ever loved.

###797###
When most I muse on that which is to come,
And trace the path where age doth swiftly lead,
My spirits sink, and fears my mind consume,
That beauty's flower must fall like blighted weed.
For in my dreams, I see thy fading grace,
The silver threads that steal among thy hair,
And wrinkles deep, usurping every space
Where youth and joy did once hold empire there.
Then do I rush to catch thy present sight,
To print thy image firmly in my soul,
Ere envious time shall quench thy youthful light,
And shadows dark upon thy beauty roll.
So let me gaze, and drink thy charms profound,
Before thy glories sleep beneath the ground.

###798###
Farewell! thou art too dear for my possessing,
And like enough thou know'st thy estimate,
The charter of thy worth gives thee releasing;
My bonds in thee are all determinate.
For how can I hold thee, whom there is none to hold
But by the heart's own secret, tender tie?
And though I claim, my title is too old,
A love that's grown too wide, too vast, too high.
Then go thy way, by my consent thou mayst;
My heart has yielded to thy cruel demand,
And though it bleeds, no longer it delays,
To set thee free from love's enduring band.
So take thy freedom, though it breaks my core,
My love for thee shall linger evermore.

###799###
What potions have I drunk of Siren tears,
Distill'd from limbecks foul as hell within,
Applying fears to hopes, and hopes to fears,
Still losing when I saw myself to win?
What wretched errors hath my heart committed,
Whilst it hath thought itself so sure of thee,
How have I sworn thy faith when I was pitted
Against a thousand proofs of perfidy?
O, from what power hast thou this powerful might,
With insufficiency to make me thine?
To make me love that which my soul doth fright,
And blindly bow before thy false shrine.
For I have sworn thee fair, and thought thee bright,
Who art as black as hell, as dark as night.

###800###
O, never say that I was false of heart,
Though absence seem'd my flame to mitigate,
But think that love, by absence, doth impart
More strength to love, and makes it more sedate.
For I have sworn thee fair, and look'd on thee
As that sole star which doth my life command,
And though the world with other joys may flee,
My heart remains within thy gentle hand.
No change in nature, though it brings the year
To winter's chill from summer's warm embrace,
Can cool the fervent vows I hold so dear,
Or steal the joy that fills this loving space.
So be thou true, as I am true to thee,
And let our loves in endless union be.

###801###
When cruel time shall steal thy beauty's grace,
And youth's fair summer doth to winter turn,
When wrinkles mar the smoothness of thy face,
And all thy ardent fires no longer burn;
Then shall the spirit of thy vibrant eyes,
Which now doth pierce the soul with loving dart,
Be shadowed by the swift years' stern demise,
And chill oblivion claim thy gentle heart.
But fear not, love, the grave's devouring maw,
Nor all the wasting hand of swift decay,
For in these lines, beyond time's harsh command,
Thy timeless essence shall forever stay.
So long as men can breathe or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###802###
Shall I compare thee to a summer's day?
Thou art more lovely and more temperate:
Rough winds do shake the darling buds of May,
And summer's lease hath all too short a date:
Sometime too hot the eye of heaven shines,
And often is his gold complexion dimmed;
And every fair from fair sometime declines,
By chance, or nature's changing course, untrimmed;
But thy eternal summer shall not fade,
Nor lose possession of that fair thou ow'st;
Nor shall death brag thou wander'st in his shade,
When in eternal lines to time thou grow'st:
So long as men can breathe or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###803###
When in disgrace with fortune and men's eyes,
I all alone beweep my outcast state,
And trouble deaf heaven with my bootless cries,
And look upon myself, and curse my fate,
Wishing me like to one more rich in hope,
Featured like him, like him with friends possessed,
Desiring this man's art, and that man's scope,
With what I most enjoy contented least;
Yet in these thoughts myself almost despising,
Haply I think on thee, and then my state,
Like to the lark at break of day arising
From sullen earth, sings hymns at heaven's gate;
For thy sweet love remembered such wealth brings
That then I scorn to change my state with kings.

###804###
The weary world doth spin its ancient round,
And fleeting hours speed with silent tread;
Each blossom born doth fall upon the ground,
And youth's sweet vigour withers to the dead.
The stately tree that bravely fronts the storm,
Whose roots embrace the soil in firmest hold,
Must bend at last before the winter's form,
And shed its leaves, grown weak and sadly old.
So man, whose life is but a fleeting gleam,
A breath, a shadow, passing swift away,
Is carried onward by time's ceaseless stream,
To dark oblivion's cold and cheerless day.
But love, though time doth all things else consume,
Shall bloom eternal, conquering the tomb.

###805###
Mine eye hath played the painter and hath steeled
Thy outward shape, the inward love to show;
Within my body's casket hath it kneeled,
And there in fairest colours made thee grow.
My body is the frame wherein 'tis set,
And in my heart the picture is enthroned;
My heart, whose thoughts thy fair idea get,
And in my breast thy image is well known.
But art it is not, for the painter's skill
Cannot express the heaven within thine eyes,
Nor can his brush portray thy potent will,
Which doth my soul with rapture surmise.
For thee alone doth my true spirit yearn,
And from thy light, all other beauties learn.

###806###
How oft when thou, my music, music play'st,
Upon that blessed wood whose motion sounds
With thy sweet fingers, when thou gently sway'st
The speaking strings to give a pleasant wounds,
Dost thou not, with an envious ear, bemoan
Those blessed sounds that make such sweet accord,
As if the touch alone was meant to own
The vibrant soul that issues from each chord?
Mark how the chords, whose love doth so combine,
Do kiss each other, as if envying thee
That joyous strain, which all thy heart define,
A single harmony for all to see.
Oh, give that gift to me, that I might be
Joined with thee in sweet accord, as those strings be.

###807###
My love is as a fever, longing still
For that which longer nurseth the disease,
Feeding on that which doth preserve the ill,
Th' uncertain object of the eye's fond pleas.
My reason, the physician to my love,
Angry that his prescriptions are not kept,
Hath left me, and I desperate now approve
Desire is death, which physic did except.
Past cure I am, now reason is past care,
And frantic-mad with evermore unrest;
My thoughts and my discourse as madmen's are,
At random from the truth vainly expressed;
For I have sworn thee fair, and thought thee bright,
Who art as black as hell, as dark as night.

###808###
When I do count the clock that tells the time,
And see the brave day sunk in hideous night;
When I behold the violet past prime,
And sable curls all silvered o'er with white;
When lofty trees I see barren of leaves,
Which erst from heat did canopy the herd,
And summer's green all girded up in sheaves,
Borne on the bier with white and bristly beard;
Then of thy beauty do I question make,
That thou among the wastes of time must go,
Since sweets and beauties do themselves forsake
And die as fast as they see others grow;
And nothing 'gainst Time's scythe can make defence
Save breed, to brave him when he takes thee hence.

###809###
Some glory in their birth, some in their skill,
Some in their wealth, some in their body's force;
Some in their garments, though new-fangled ill;
Some in their hawks and hounds, some in their horse;
And every humour hath his adjunct pleasure,
Wherein it finds a joy above the rest;
But these are all but toys of fleeting measure,
And cannot stand the age's acid test.
My mind to me a kingdom is, I find,
And in thy truth my richest treasure lies,
A constant comfort for a troubled mind,
Reflected in the brightness of thine eyes.
No outward show can match the grace within,
Where lasting joys do evermore begin.

###810###
Those hours, that with gentle work did frame
The lovely gaze where every eye doth dwell,
Will play the tyrants to the very same
And carve with wrinkles every perfect spell.
For beauty's self is but a fading show,
A fleeting flower that blossoms for a day,
And then doth swiftly to oblivion go,
As winter storms chase summer's warmth away.
Oh, how shall beauty hold a plea with time,
Whose action is to rend and to destroy,
When all things fair are subject to his crime,
And every youth he steals doth grief employ?
Unless this miracle have power to save,
That thou art treasured here beyond the grave.

###811###
From fairest creatures we desire increase,
That thereby beauty's rose might never die,
But as the riper should by time decease,
His tender heir might bear his memory:
But thou, contracted to thine own bright eyes,
Feed'st thy light's flame with self-substantial fuel,
Making a famine where abundance lies,
Thyself thy foe, to thy sweet self too cruel.
Thou that art now the world's fresh ornament
And only herald to the gaudy spring,
Within thine own bud buriest thy content
And, tender churl, makest waste in niggarding.
Pity the world, or else this glutton be,
To eat the world's due, by the grave and thee.

###812###
My love to thee is as a faithful glass,
Wherein I see my truest self expressed;
No change of fortune can my passion pass,
Nor time's swift river leave my heart distressed.
For in thy virtues, mine own joys reside,
And in thy sorrow, doth my spirit mourn;
No lesser love can ever be my guide,
Since to thy worth my lasting faith was born.
Let fickle friendships shift with every wind,
And summer's blossoms wither in the sun;
A steadfast heart such falsehood cannot find,
But holds its course till life's last race be run.
For though the world may turn and change its hue,
My constant heart shall ever beat for you.

###813###
When forty winters shall besiege thy brow,
And dig deep trenches in thy beauty's field,
Thy youth's proud livery, so gazed on now,
Will be a tattered weed, of small worth held:
Then being asked, where all thy beauty lies,
Where all the treasure of thy lusty days,
To say, within thine own deep-sunken eyes,
Were an all-eating shame, and thriftless praise.
How much more praise deserved thy beauty's use,
If thou couldst answer "This fair child of mine
Shall sum my count, and make my old excuse,"
Proving his beauty by succession thine!
This were to be new made when thou art old,
And see thy blood warm when thou feel'st it cold.

###814###
Let me not to the marriage of true minds
Admit impediments. Love is not love
Which alters when it alteration finds,
Or bends with the remover to remove.
O no! it is an ever-fixed mark
That looks on tempests and is never shaken;
It is the star to every wandering bark,
Whose worth's unknown, although his height be taken.
Love's not Time's fool, though rosy lips and cheeks
Within his bending sickle's compass come;
Love alters not with his brief hours and weeks,
But bears it out even to the edge of doom.
If this be error and upon me proved,
I never writ, nor no man ever loved.

###815###
The expense of spirit in a waste of shame
Is lust in action; and till action, lust
Is perjured, murderous, bloody, full of blame,
Savage, extreme, rude, cruel, not to trust,
Enjoyed no sooner but despised straight,
Past reason hunted; and no sooner had,
Past reason hated, as a swallowed bait
On purpose laid to make the taker mad:
Mad in pursuit, and in possession so;
Had, having, and in quest to have, extreme;
A bliss in proof, and proved, a very woe;
Before a joy proposed; behind a dream.
All this the world well knows; yet none knows well
To shun the heaven that leads men to this hell.

###816###
Mine eye and heart are at a mortal war,
How to divide the conquest of thy sight;
Mine eye my heart thy picture would bar,
My heart mine eye the freedom of that light.
My heart doth plead that thou in him dost lie,
A closet never pierced with crystal eyes,
But the defendant doth that plea deny,
And says in him thy fair perfection lies.
To 'cide this title is impanelled
A quest of thoughts, all tenants to the heart;
And by their verdict is determined
The clear possession of thy sweetest part.
As thus: mine eye's sole office is thy outward show,
And my heart's love, thy inward truth to know.

###817###
When sorrow's cloud doth dim my spirit's light,
And earthly cares do press my soul so low,
I seek the comfort of thy blessed sight,
Where peace and solace ever gently flow.
For in thine eyes, a heaven I discern,
A refuge sweet from all the world's harsh strife,
Where kindly stars with softest radiance burn,
And banish shadows from my weary life.
My heart, once burdened with a weight of pain,
Now lifts its wings and soars to pure delight,
And all my troubled thoughts grow calm again,
Illuminated by thy cheering light.
So art thou, love, my anchor and my guide,
My sun, my moon, my tide, my peace, my pride.

###818###
No more be grieved at that which thou hast done:
Roses have thorns, and silver fountains mud;
Clouds and eclipses stain both moon and sun,
And loathsome canker lives in sweetest bud.
All men make faults, and even I in this,
Authorizing thy trespass with compare,
Myself corrupting, salving thy amiss,
Excusing thy foul deeds with words more fair.
For to thy sensual fault I bring in sense—
Thy adverse party is thy advocate—
And 'gainst myself a lawful plea commence:
Such civil war is in my love and hate,
That I an accessary needs must be
To that sweet thief which sourly robs from me.

###819###
Poor soul, the center of my sinful earth,
Fooled by those rebel powers that thee array,
Why dost thou pine within and suffer dearth,
Painting thy outward walls so costly gay?
Why so large cost, having so short a lease,
Dost thou upon thy fading mansion spend?
Shall worms, inheritors of this excess,
Eat up thy charge? Is this thy body's end?
Then, soul, live thou upon thy servant's loss,
And let that pine to aggravate thy store;
Buy terms divine in selling hours of dross;
Within be fed, without be rich no more:
So shalt thou feed on Death, that feeds on men,
And Death once dead, there's no more dying then.

###820###
When in the chronicle of wasted time
I see descriptions of the fairest wights,
And beauty making beautiful old rhyme
In praise of ladies dead and lovely knights,
Then, in the blazon of sweet beauty's best,
Of hand, of foot, of lip, of eye, of brow,
I see their antique pen would have expressed
Even such a beauty as you master now.
So all their praises are but prophecies
Of this our time, all you prefiguring;
And, for they looked but with divining eyes,
They had not skill enough your worth to sing:
For we, which now behold these present days,
Have eyes to wonder, but lack tongues to praise.

###821###
When wasteful Time doth crop the fairest flower,
And beauty's fleeting grace doth fade away,
My verse shall hold thee safe from ruin's power,
And make thy lasting youth outlive this day.
For marble tombs and gilded monuments,
Shall crumble down to dust, as all things must,
But lasting words, by poet's firm intents,
Preserve thy spirit from oblivion's rust.
So long as men can breathe, or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.
Thy gentle form, encased in poesy,
Shall bloom forever, fairer than can be.
And envious death, with all his cruel might,
Shall boast no triumph o'er thy deathless light.

###822###
My love for thee, no season can impair,
Nor winter's breath, nor summer's fiery heat,
For in my heart thou ever dost repair,
A sanctuary where my soul doth meet.
Though time doth steal the roses from thy cheek,
And furrow deep the brow with cruelest hand,
My faithful eye shall still thy beauty seek,
A constant star in this inconstant land.
For love is not a thing of outward show,
That bows to time, or alters with his frown,
But constant stands, though bitter tempests blow,
And never from its fix'd estate comes down.
If this be error, and upon me proved,
I never writ, nor no man ever loved.

###823###
When I behold the passing of the hours,
And see how quickly youth doth steal away,
The fading glory of once vibrant flowers,
And sun-kissed mornings yielding to the grey;
Then do I muse upon thy fleeting grace,
How swift thy beauty hastens to its fall,
And how the years shall mar thy lovely face,
As cruel Time doth prey on one and all.
What solace then shall bide against his rage,
That sweeps all youthful fairness from our sight?
Save but in lines upon this humble page,
To hold thy image from eternal night.
So live, fair youth, in this my spoken rhyme,
Defying wrinkles and devouring Time.

###824###
How can my verse presume to praise thy worth,
When humble words lack grace to speak thy name?
My pen doth tremble, conscious of its dearth,
To light a fire from so cold a flame.
For thou art fair, beyond all mortal scope,
A jewel rare, that mocks the common gaze,
While I, a shadow, clinging to faint hope,
Do wander lost in melancholy ways.
Yet if my lines, though poor, can hold some gleam,
Of that bright light that shines within thine eye,
A dim reflection of a waking dream,
Then shall my humble spirit never die.
And thou, in them, a fairer form shalt bear,
Than painters limn, or poets can declare.

###825###
The spring gives way to summer's ardent prime,
Then mellow autumn bows to winter's chill;
So swiftly pass the seasons of our time,
And leave their mark on valley, stream, and hill.
Even so thy beauty, now in fullest bloom,
Must yield at length to age's cold decay,
And vanish quite into a silent tomb,
As yesterday doth yield to grim today.
But as the rose's essence doth remain,
Distilled in vials, though the flower be dead,
So shall thy grace, preserved from loss and pain,
Within these verses ever hold its head.
A fragrant memory, time cannot erase,
To bless the eye with beauty's lasting grace.

###826###
When gentle Sleep, that balm of weary mind,
Doth steal from me his sweet and soft embrace,
I lie awake, and fretful thoughts I find,
That haunt the shadows of this lonely place.
For then thy image, clearer than by day,
Doth rise before my mind's internal sight,
And holds my captive soul in endless sway,
Transmuting darkest gloom to purest light.
So banished is repose from my sad bed,
My eyelids heavy, yet my spirit bright,
With visions of thy form, divinely spread,
Throughout the watches of the silent night.
Thus weary day doth bring no rest to me,
For all my rest, my thoughts, are but of thee.

###827###
If in thy breast, where once my hopes did dwell,
A stranger's face doth find a warmer room,
And to some new-found friend thy secrets tell,
Consigning my old love to dusty gloom;
Then let it be, I will not stand in strife,
Nor vex thy heart with undeservèd plea,
For though it sting the current of my life,
Yet will I wish all happiness to thee.
But should it prove a momentary wile,
A fleeting fancy of a restless thought,
I pray thee, banish that deceiving smile,
And mend the breach that cruelly hath been wrought.
For true affection, tried by doubt and pain,
Will ever seek its rightful place again.

###828###
Mark how when music, heavenly, sweet, and clear,
Doth strike the ear with concord's gentle sound,
My spirit leaps, to hold its accents dear,
And in its tones a solace deep is found.
Yet when it plays, I think of thee, my love,
Who art a harmony in human guise,
A symphony that's sent from realms above,
To bless with grace the earth and azure skies.
For every note that doth thy praise rehearse,
Doth tell of beauty, truth, and virtue bright,
A living music, better than my verse,
That fills the world with everlasting light.
So let thy voice, my sweet, still charm mine ear,
And banish every doubt, and every fear.

###829###
This mortal stage, where actors play their part,
Is but a fleeting show, a breath, a dream,
And soon the curtain falls upon each heart,
As time's swift current bears us down the stream.
The proudest towers, that pierce the vaulted sky,
Shall one day crumble, dust returning to dust,
The mightiest empires, in oblivion lie,
And all earth's glories bow to nature's rust.
Why then should man, a creature born to die,
Build monuments to hold his name in store?
When all his works beneath the heavens lie,
And fade to nothing, to be seen no more.
Save in the soul, where truth and beauty bloom,
Escaping ever from the common tomb.

###830###
Look in thy glass, and tell the face thou viewest,
Now is the time that face should form another,
Whose youth and vigour, joyfully thou renewest,
Ere envious age thy beauty wholly smother.
For if thou dost neglect this fair command,
To leave no copy of thy loveliness,
Thou dost defraud the world with thine own hand,
And rob posterity of its fair grace.
What pity 'tis, to let such goodness die,
When thou hast power to make it live again,
And from thy stock a new fair blossom hie,
To mock the wrinkles and the winter's reign.
So be not cruel, but let thy grace abound,
That in thy child, thy beauty may be found.

###831###
How can I praise thee, whom my heart holds dear,
Without that praise appearing but a show?
When truth itself doth make thy virtues clear,
And every deed doth make thy goodness grow.
For in thy spirit, all that's pure doth gleam,
A constancy that mocks the changing moon,
A loyal heart, more constant than a dream,
That shines unflawed beneath the day's high noon.
No flattery can add unto thy worth,
Nor any lie diminish thy estate,
For all thou art, springs from a noble birth,
Beyond the reach of envy or of hate.
So live, my friend, in worth that none can blame,
And be thyself, in honour and in fame.

###832###
The glory of the rising sun doth fade,
When darker clouds obscure his golden ray;
The mightiest kingdoms, built and bravely made,
Decline in time, and vanish day by day.
So too the beauty, that doth grace thy face,
A transient blossom, frail and quickly gone,
Shall yield its pride to Time's unsparing pace,
And leave no vestige of its golden dawn.
Yet fear thou not, though all things pass away,
Though youth and strength surrender to the tomb,
For in these lines, thy spirit shall hold sway,
Protected ever from oblivion's gloom.
So shall thy name, in lasting verse be set,
When all save truth, the world shall quite forget.

###833###
When thou art absent, all the world seems grey,
And every joy doth lose its vibrant hue;
The sun itself doth shine with lesser ray,
And nature's colours seem no longer true.
My burdened heart, a prisoner in its cell,
Doth pine for freedom, longed-for liberty,
And in thy coming, all its hopes do swell,
To break the chains that bind my soul to thee.
No song of bird, nor scent of sweetest rose,
Can bring delight to my despairing mind,
Until thy loving presence once more glows,
The only solace that my soul can find.
So hasten home, and banish all my pain,
And make my weary spirit whole again.

###834###
Some outward show doth capture common eyes,
A painted gloss, that hides a hollow core;
And many a heart, that boasts of virtues lies,
While true worth sleeps, unheeded, evermore.
But thou, my love, art fashioned of a truth,
That needs no mask to gain the world's acclaim,
For in thy spirit, from thy tender youth,
Doth shine a light, that puts all lies to shame.
Thy honesty, a beacon, pure and bright,
Doth guide my steps through error's darkest maze,
And in thy heart, where dwells celestial light,
I find a solace for my troubled days.
So let the world pursue its gaudy prize,
My treasure lies within thy honest eyes.

###835###
When I perceive how quickly youth doth grow,
And holds but little moment in its prime,
How this huge stage doth all its wonders show,
And plays the acts of youth and fading time;
Then knowing that the world itself must change,
And things most fair to foul corruption tend,
A bitter sorrow doth my thoughts arrange,
That youth and beauty must approach their end.
But then I think of thee, whom I adore,
And know thy essence time can not defeat,
For in my verse, thy spirit shall endure,
And find a refuge from its swift retreat.
So thou shalt live, when all beside doth die,
And keep thy spring beneath an autumn sky.

###836###
Could art but capture all thy living grace,
And hold in pigments every charm thou hast,
Then painters' hands might truly limn thy face,
And bid thy transient beauty ever last.
But all their skill, howe'er it strive to gain,
The subtle fire that dances in thine eye,
Doth fail to show the spirit's bright domain,
And lets thy truest, inner beauty die.
No canvas great, nor colour's brightest hue,
Can truly speak the wonders of thy soul,
For thou art fairer than all art can do,
Beyond the painter's brush, beyond control.
So let my verse, though faint, attempt to praise,
And keep thy glory through unending days.

###837###
When Fortune's wheel doth turn with fickle spin,
And bringeth sorrow where was once delight,
When all the world doth seem to close me in,
And banish gladness from my weary sight;
Then do I turn, my dearest love, to thee,
A steadfast haven in life's stormy sea,
For in thy presence, all my sorrows flee,
And sweet content doth ever dwell with me.
Let tempest rage, and envious winds conspire,
To steal from us the peace we hold so dear,
My love for thee, a never-dying fire,
Shall burn more brightly, banishing all fear.
So let us cling, though fates against us rage,
And write our love upon life's lasting page.

###838###
When gentle thoughts of thee my spirit fill,
And bring to mind thy form, so sweet and fair,
I find a solace that doth calm me still,
And banish every shadow of despair.
For memory, a painter skilled and true,
Doth limn thy features with a loving hand,
And brings thy living image to my view,
More bright than sunshine in a summer land.
Though absent far, thy presence I can feel,
As if thou stood'st beside me, close and dear,
And all the wounds that bitter parting deal,
Are heal'd by fancy, banishing all fear.
So in my mind, thou ever shalt reside,
My constant comfort, and my truest guide.

###839###
The stately ships, that plough the ocean's foam,
And bear proud banners to the distant shore,
Shall one day rest, forever in their home,
Their valiant journeys to be made no more.
The pyramids, that pierce the azure sky,
Built by the toil of generations past,
Shall witness time's relentless passing by,
And yield at length to winds that fiercely blast.
For all that man, with might and cunning frames,
His kingdoms vast, his temples high and grand,
Is but a breath, that fleeting moment claims,
Before it crumbles back to common sand.
So let us live, and love with open heart,
For all beside, is but a transient part.

###840###
Let others praise the beauty bought with art,
The painted cheek, the hair of borrowed gold,
That steals away the truth from every heart,
And leaves the watcher's hungry gaze quite cold.
But thou, my love, art graced with truth's own hue,
A natural fairness, needing no disguise,
And in thy gentle form, both fresh and new,
A spirit beams, reflected in thine eyes.
No artificial tint thy worth can raise,
Nor any skill enhance thy native grace,
For Nature's self doth crown thee with her praise,
And sets her seal upon thy lovely face.
So let thy light, unblemish'd, ever shine,
A sacred beauty, utterly divine.
